Objections to Fourth Figure Self-Productivity
Author presents five objections to the self-productivity of fourth figure moods and begins answering the first.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to analyze a dialectical exchange in Islamic logic by identifying objections and responses related to syllogistic productivity per se.

Core Idea
Dialectical objections test whether a syllogism is productive per se; equivalence between consequent and antecedent is not required.

In this passage, the author defends the claim that the fourth figure moods are productive per se against five objections. The objector challenges the very notion of self-productivity, using dilemmas about equality, dependency, subject-predicate relations, unity of the middle term, and counterexamples. The author begins replying to the first objection, arguing that a necessary consequent need not be equivalent to its antecedent. This exchange illustrates how dialectic sharpens logical principles.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim under attack
The objector targets the claim that the four moods of the fourth figure are productive per se (muntij li-dhatihi), i.e., they yield their conclusions without external aid. Find where in the Arabic this claim is stated.
The phrase 'منتج لذاته' appears early in the passage.
2
List the five objections and their structure
The objector gives five distinct arguments. For each, note the key logical move: (1) dilemma about equality of consequent; (2) dependency on another premise; (3) subject-predicate specificity; (4) unity of the middle term; (5) counterexample in other forms. Try to reduce each to a simple conditional.
The objections are numbered in the Arabic: [١] through [٥].
3
Examine the author's response to the first objection
The author denies that a consequent must be equal to its antecedent. He argues that something can be a consequent of one thing while something else is a consequent equal to its antecedent, and that a consequent of another can exist only if separation is possible. Identify the core logical point.
The answer begins with 'لكنا نقول في الجواب [١] عن الأول'.
4
Assess the state of the debate
Note that the answer is cut off; the author has only addressed the first objection. Consider what would be needed to fully defend the claim against all five objections.
The source continues on the next page, so the full refutation is incomplete in this lesson.

ثم لقائل أن يقول إن المنتج في هذه الشكل من الضروب الأربعة لا يكون منتجا لذاته فإن من الوجوه ما ينافيه نحو الوجوه المذكورة في الشكل الأول وغيرها، وذلك من وجوه أيضًا : [١] منها أن يقال هذا اللازم لا يلزم عنه لذاته فإنه إذا لزم فلا يخلو من أن يكون مساويا له، وإنه باطل ضرورة كونه لازما للغير؛ واللازم للغير يمكن أن يوجد عند وجود ذلك الغير دون وجود غيره. أو لا يكون مساويًا، وإنه باطل أيضًا ضرورة عدم الافتراق بينهما أصلا وإلا لا يكون لازما أبدًا. [٢] ومنها أن يقال إنه لا يلزم عنه لذاته فإنه إذا لزم: فإما أن يلزم من الغير؛ وإنه محال فإنه يفتقر وجوده إلى وجود أحد ملزوميه حينئذ، وذلك هو الغير. ولو كان مفتقرًا إلى الغير فلا يلزم عنه لذاته. وإما أن لا يلزم من الغير؛ وإنه محال أيضًا فإنه يلزم من الضرب الثاني في الشكل الأول، وذلك غيره. [٣] ومنها أن يقال إنه لا يلزم عنه لذاته؛ إذ الموضوع في النتيجة وهي قولنا لا شيء من (ج) (أ) لا يخلو من أن يكون غير المحمول أو لا يكون. وإنما كان لا يكون هذا القول مختصا بتلك الضرب، ولا يكون مفتقرًا صدقه في الذهن إلى صدق ذلك كذلك، وهذا ظاهر. [٤] ومنها أن يقال إنه لا يلزم عنه لذاته إلا وأن يكون الأوسط فيه متحدا؛ والأوسط فيه لا يكون متحدا وإلا لكان متحدا في قولنا كل إنسان حيوان ولا شيء من الجماد بحيوان وليس كذلك فإن الحيوان في الصغرى والكبرى منكرًا، ومن اللوازم أن يكون المنكر الثاني في اللفظ غير الأول؛ أما سمعت قول القائل لن يُغلب عسر بيسرين؟ [٥] ومنها أن يقال إنه إذا لزم عنه لذاته كان من اللوازم أن يلزم في سائر الصور؛ وليس كذلك فإنا إذا قلنا كل إنسان متنفس، ولا شيء من المستنشق بمتنفس فلا يلزم منه لا شيء من الإنسان بمستنشق، وعلى هذا في الضروب الباقية. لكنا نقول في الجواب [١] عن الأول إنه لا يمكن أن يكون مساويا به وإن كان لازما للغير. فإن من الأشياء ما يكون لازما للشيء وغيره لازما مساويًا الملزومه. وأما قوله «اللازم للغير يمكن أن يوجد عند وجود ذلك الغير دون وجود غيره، فذلك في حيز المنع لأنه لا يمكن إلا وأن يمكن الافتراق بينهما
Then it may be said: that the productive [moods] in this figure from the four moods are not productive per se, for among the modes are those that contradict it, like the modes mentioned in the first figure and others. And that is also from several angles: [1] From them is that it is said: this consequent does not follow from it per se. For if it follows, it must either be equal to it, and that is false necessarily because it is a consequent of another; and the consequent of another can exist when that other exists without the existence of its other [antecedent]. Or it is not equal, and that is also false necessarily because there would be no separation between them at all, otherwise it would never be a consequent. [2] From them is that it is said: it does not follow from it per se; for if it follows, then either it follows from another, but that is impossible because its existence would then depend on the existence of one of its two antecedents, and that is the other. And if it depended on the other, it would not follow from it per se. Or it does not follow from another, but that is also impossible because it follows from the second mood of the first figure, and that is other than it. [3] From them is that it is said: it does not follow from it per se; for the subject in the conclusion, which is our statement 'No (J) is (A)', must either be other than the predicate or not. And this statement would not be specific to that mood, nor would its truth in the mind depend on the truth of that [mood] likewise, and this is obvious. [4] From them is that it is said: it does not follow from it per se unless the middle term in it is unified; but the middle term in it is not unified, otherwise it would be unified in our statement 'Every human is an animal' and 'No inanimate is an animal', but it is not so, for 'animal' in the minor and major is indefinite, and one of the requirements is that the second indefinite in wording is not the first; have you not heard the saying: 'A hardship will not be overcome by two ease-es'? [5] From them is that it is said: if it followed from it per se, it would be among the requirements that it follow in all forms; but it is not so, for if we say 'Every human is a breather' and 'No inhaler is a breather', it does not follow from it that 'No human is an inhaler', and likewise for the remaining moods. But we say in answer to the first: that it cannot be equal to it even if it is a consequent of another. For among things there is what is a consequent of a thing and another [thing] is a consequent equal to its antecedent. And as for his statement 'the consequent of another can exist when that other exists without the existence of its other [antecedent]', that is in the realm of denial, because it cannot be unless separation between them is possible.
Source pages p. 35

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical exchange where an objector raises five arguments against the claim that the fourth figure moods are productive per se, followed by the author’s answer to the first objection. The objector’s target is the notion that a conclusion necessarily follows from its premises alone without external assistance. The first objection argues that if a consequent follows from something, it must either be equal to it (contradicted by being a consequent of another) or not equal (contradicted by inseparability). The second objection posits that if it follows, it must follow from the other premise or not; both are impossible. The third objection focuses on the conclusion’s subject-predicate relation. The fourth objection claims the middle term must be unified (identical in wording) but gives a counterexample where 'animal' is indefinite. The fifth objection shows a counterexample in which the conclusion does not follow. In reply, the author denies that a consequent cannot be non-equivalent to its antecedent, using the example of something that is a consequent of a thing while another thing is a consequent equal to its antecedent. He also denies the objector’s claim that a consequent of another can exist independently, insisting that separation is possible only under certain conditions. The answer is cut off, likely continued on the next page. The author’s strategy is to show that necessary consequence does not require equivalence, and that the objector’s assumptions about unity and independence are false.

Checkpoint
In the first objection, the objector claims that if a consequent follows from something, it must either be equal to it or not. Why does he consider both options false?
A) Because equality would make it the same thing, and non-equality would allow separation.
B) Because equality contradicts being a consequent of another, and non-equality contradicts inseparability.
C) Because equality is always true, and non-equality is impossible.
D) Because the objector is confused about the meaning of consequence.

Key Takeaway
The self-productivity of a syllogism does not require that the conclusion be equivalent to its premises; dialectical objections often rest on mistaken assumptions about necessary consequence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Select the first objection from the passage and reconstruct it as a conditional argument: 'If the consequent follows from the premises, then it is either equal or not equal. If equal, it cannot be a consequent of another; but it is a consequent of another; therefore it is not equal. If not equal, then separation is possible, but then it is not a necessary consequent.' Then explain the author's reply in one sentence.
?
Consider the fourth objection about the unity of the middle term. Using the example 'Every human is an animal' and 'No inanimate is an animal', explain why the objector claims the middle term is not unified. Then propose a way to unify it (e.g., by stating 'animal' with the same quantifier) to make the syllogism productive per se.

Defending Conditional Syllogisms: Four Dialectical Responses
Author refutes four objections about the validity of conditional syllogisms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and evaluate the author's responses to four objections against conditional syllogisms.

Core Idea
Dialectical refutation requires precision about entailment and conversion.

In this passage, the author defends conditional syllogisms against four objections. Each objection is met with a precise logical response, clarifying the conditions under which such syllogisms are valid. Understanding these responses sharpens your ability to defend logical arguments in dialectical contexts.

Guided Path
1
Analyze the first objection and its response
The objector claims the syllogism would have to work in all forms, but a counterexample with 'man/horse/moving/stationary' is given. The author responds with the principle of mutual exclusion of entailments (tanāfī bayn al-lāzimayn). Explain how this principle addresses the objection.
Ensure the terms are in opposition for the principle to apply.
2
Examine the second response: conversion of negative premises
The author states that the negative (sāliba) in this figure must be convertible. Recall what conversion (in‘ikās) means and apply it to a sample conditional: if not A then B.
Conversion swaps subject and predicate while preserving truth.
3
Understand the third response: generality of the necessary consequent (lāzim)
The objector claimed that a necessary consequent must be more general. The author distinguishes when it is and isn't, based on equality in actual existence (tasāwī fī al-taḥaqquq). Explain this condition.
Here the author asserts equality, so generality does not fail.
4
Evaluate the fourth response: productivity of the syllogism
The author says that if someone believes both premises truly, they must believe the conclusion. Does this fully defend the syllogism? Why might this response be considered sufficient?
This invokes the commitment to logical consequence from true premises.
5
Synthesize the dialectical structure
Map each objection to its corresponding logical principle (mutual exclusion, conversion, generality condition, belief-commitment). Which response do you find most convincing? Why?
Consider how each response clarifies a condition for validity.

لا يخلو من أن يكون، أو لا يكون . [ث] ومنها أن يقال إنه لا يصح وإلا لكان منتجا في سائر الصور؛ وليس كذلك. فأما إذا قلنا كلما كان الإنسان فرسا كان متحركًا، وليس البتة إذا كان ساكنا كان متحركًا فإنه لا ينتج قولنا ليس البتة إذا كان فرسا كان ساكنا؛ وقد كان الفرس ساكناً في كثير من الأوقات. أما الجواب فنقول : [1] في الأول منها الكلام في صورة يكون هذا في مقابلة ذلك لما أنه هو الاستدلال بالتنافي بين اللازمين على التنافي بين الملزومين؛ وذلك لا يمكن إلا وأن يكون هذا في مقابلة ذلك. [ب] وفي الثاني نقول: قد مرَّ من قبل أن السالبة في هذا الشكل لا تكون إلا وأن تكون منعكسة فالسالبة في الكبرى تكون منعكسة : وحينئذ يلزم أن تكون النتيجة كذلك. [ت] وفي الثالث نقول : لا يلزم من كونه لازما للعنوان أن يكون أعم من هذا في التحقق؛ وإنما يكون كذلك إن لو لم يكن ذلك الغير مساويًا لهذا في التحقق؛ والحق فيما نحن فيه إنه يساويه فإن هذا لا يفارق ذلك في نفس الأمر. [ث] وفي الرابع نقول: قد مرَّ من قبل أن مثل هذا القياس ينتج فإن من جمع بين هاتين المقدتين على اعتقاد أن كل واحدة منهما حق يلزم أن يعتقد صدق النتيجة وحقيقتها كذلك كما إذا اعتقد كل واحدة منهما؛ وكان اعتقاده مطابقا للحقيقة.
It is not free from being or not being. [Th] Among them is that it is said that it is not valid, otherwise it would be productive in all forms; but it is not so. As for when we say 'Whenever a man is a horse, he is moving,' and 'It is not absolutely that when he is stationary he is moving,' then it does not produce our statement 'It is not absolutely that when he is a horse he is stationary'; and the horse was stationary at many times. As for the answer, we say: [1] Regarding the first of them, the discussion concerns a form where this is in opposition to that, because it is inference by the mutual exclusion between two entailments to the mutual exclusion between the two entailed; and that is not possible unless this is in opposition to that. [B] Regarding the second, we say: It has passed previously that the negative in this figure is only and must be converted, so the negative in the major premise is converted; then the conclusion must be likewise. [T] Regarding the third, we say: It does not follow from it being a necessary consequent of the subject that it is more general than this in actual existence; rather it is so only if that other is not equal to this in actual existence; and the truth in our matter is that it equals it, for this does not separate from that in the very thing itself. [Th] Regarding the fourth, we say: It has passed previously that such a syllogism is productive, for whoever combines these two premises believing that each of them is true must believe the truth of the conclusion and its reality likewise, just as if he believed each of them; and his belief corresponds to reality.
Source pages p. 103

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical exchange in which the author (likely the commentator) addresses four objections raised against the validity of certain conditional syllogisms. The objections are presented briefly, then each is answered sequentially. The first objection claimed invalidity because the syllogism would have to be productive in all forms, which is false. The author's response clarifies that the inference uses mutual exclusion of entailments, which only works when the terms are in opposition. The second objection concerns the conversion of the negative premise; the author reiterates that the negative in this figure is convertible, so the conclusion follows. The third objection relates to the generality of the necessary consequent; the author explains that it need not be more general unless the other consequent is not equal in actual existence—here they are equal. The fourth objection doubts productivity; the author affirms that if one believes both premises truly, one must believe the conclusion truly, as in a valid syllogism. The author uses illā clauses and technical terms like 'lāzim' (entailed) and 'malzūm' (entailing) to maintain precision. An Islamic example: For the second response, consider the conversion: 'If it is not obligatory, it is not fajr prayer' converts to 'If it is fajr prayer, it is obligatory'.

Conditional syllogisms are defended against dialectical objections by invoking principles of mutual exclusion, conversion, generality conditions, and belief-commitment.

Dialectical Objections to the Third Figure's First Mood
Author presents two objections against the validity of the first mood.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct and analyze two dialectical objections against the validity of the first mood of the third figure of conditional syllogisms.

Core Idea
The third figure's first mood faces serious dialectical challenges: the unknowability of temporal unity and the threat of circularity.

In this passage, the author presents two objections that challenge the productivity of the first mood of the third figure of conditional syllogisms. These objections are not easily dismissed; they reflect deep epistemological and logical concerns. Understanding them is crucial for appreciating the dialectical context in which logical theories were debated.

Guided Path
1
Identify the target of the objections
The objections are aimed at the first mood of the third figure of conditional syllogisms. Recall that in the third figure, the middle term occupies the subject position in both premises. The first mood asserts that if A implies B and A implies C, then sometimes B implies C.
The target is specifically the claim that this mood is productive (yuntij).
2
Extract the first objection
The first objection argues that for the syllogism to be productive, the middle term must be unified (muttaḥid). However, unity of the middle term requires unity in time (al-zamān muttaḥid). The objector then claims that unity in time is unknowable — we do not know what it is or how it is. Therefore, the syllogism cannot be known to be productive.
This is an epistemological challenge: even if the syllogism is formally valid, we cannot apply it without knowing the temporal unity of the middle term.
3
Extract the second objection
The second objection concerns the relationship between the truth of the conclusion and the premises. The truth of the conclusion together with the truth of one premise either requires interdependence (each depends on the other for its realization) — which leads to circularity (dawr) — or they are independent, which means the conclusion can be true without the premise, destroying the syllogistic connection. Either way, the syllogism fails to be productive.
This is a logical challenge about the necessity of the connection between premises and conclusion.
4
Assess the dialectical force
Consider why these objections would trouble a defender of the mood. The first objection requires a response about the nature of unity and time. The second forces a choice between circularity and loss of entailment. Both objections seek to show that the mood is not a reliable inference form.
The author's response is not included in this passage; it presumably follows on the next page.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا يمتنع أن يورد عليهما من الشبه كما يورد في الضرب الأول؛ وذلك من وجوه : [1] منها أن يقال: إنه لا ينتج إلا وأن يكون الأوسط متحدا، والأوسط لا يكون متحدا إلا وأن يكون الزمان متحدا. والاتحاد في الزمان من جملة ما لا تعرف حاله أنه ما هو وكيف هو فكيف يكون منتجا؟ [ب] ومنها أن يقال: إنه لا ينتج فإن صدق النتيجة مع صدق إحدى المقدمتين إما أن يفتقر كل واحد منهما إلى صاحبه في التحقق؛ وإنه من جملة ما يلزمه الدور، وإما أن لا يفتقر؛ وإنه مما يحقق الافتراق بينهما في الجملة وذلك لا يمكن بصدق النتيجة دون
And it is not impossible to raise against them (i.e., the moods) some doubts as are raised against the first mood. That is from several aspects: [1] One of them is that it is said: It is not productive unless the middle term is unified, and the middle term is not unified unless the time is unified. And unification in time is among the things whose state is not known: what it is and how it is. So how can it be productive? [B] Another is that it is said: It is not productive because the truth of the conclusion together with the truth of one of the two premises either requires that each of the two is dependent on the other for realization — and that is among the things that entails circularity — or they are not dependent — and that is among the things that realizes their separation in general, and that cannot be with the truth of the conclusion without
Source pages p. 104

Commentary
This passage presents two objections directed at the first mood of the third figure of conditional syllogisms. The first objection argues that the syllogism requires the middle term to be unified, and unity of the middle term requires unity of time; but unity of time is unknowable, so the syllogism cannot be productive. This is a skeptical epistemological challenge. The second objection concerns the relationship between the truth of the conclusion and the premises: either they are interdependent, leading to circularity (dawr), or they are independent, meaning the conclusion can be true without the premises, which would destroy the syllogistic connection. The passage cuts off before the author's response, which presumably follows on the next page. The objections are typical of classical jadal (dialectical) literature, where the opponent raises doubts about the necessity of the middle term and the logical link. An Islamic context: for the first objection, one might ask: in a syllogism like 'Whenever the adhan is called, prayer is permissible; whenever the adhan is called, the time has entered; therefore, sometimes when prayer is permissible, the time has entered' — does the unity of 'adhan' require temporal simultaneity? The objection says yes, but that is unknowable.

Key Takeaway
The first mood of the third figure is vulnerable to dialectical objections focusing on the unity of the middle term and the relationship between premises and conclusion.

The Dialectical Syllogism from Contradictory Premises
Author explains how syllogisms from contradictory premises are used in dialectical entrapment.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and construct a syllogism from verbally contradictory premises designed for dialectical entrapment.

Core Idea
Verbally contradictory premises can force an opponent into an absurd conclusion.

In jadal (dialectical disputation), logicians sometimes construct syllogisms from premises that seem contradictory to corner an opponent. This lesson unpacks the technique: the four verbal contradictory pairs, the necessity of shared terms, and the trick of substituting synonyms to conceal the contradiction. We will analyze the example given by the author to see how this works in practice.

Guided Path
1
Identify the four verbal contradictory pairs
The author lists four pairs: 'every, not every'; 'every, none'; 'some, none'; 'some, not some'. Note that only three are true contradictions in reality; 'some, not some' is not a genuine contradiction.
Memorize these pairs as tools for dialectical argumentation.
2
Understand the term-sharing condition
The two premises must share the same terms (ḥudūd); otherwise, composing them would not entail a self-negation. If the terms differ, the conclusion does not follow.
This condition is logical: without shared terms, the syllogism is invalid.
3
Recognize the substitution trick
To conceal the contradiction, the dialectician substitutes a synonym or broader term for one term, while applying the opposite judgment. This tricks the opponent into accepting the premise.
The example substitutes 'human-like' (ādamiyy) for 'human' (insān) in the second premise.
4
Analyze the given example as a categorical syllogism
The example: 'Every human is evil' (universal affirmative) and 'Nothing evil is human-like' (universal negative) yield 'No human is human-like' (universal negative). Identify the terms: major term = 'human-like', minor term = 'human', middle term = 'evil'. The syllogism is in Celarent (first figure) but the premises are contradictory in wording.
The conclusion is false, but the dialectician forces the opponent to accept it based on the premises.

فصل: [في أن القياس قد يؤلف من مقدمات متقابلة) والقياس قد يؤلف من مقدمات متقابلة; وذلك بأن يؤلف من موجبة وسالبة متقابلتين نحو المضادتين وغيرهما احتيالا وتغليطًا على المخاطب. وكثيرًا ما يستعمل هذا القياس في الجدل على سبيل المبالغة في الإلزام، والمتقابلات في اللفظ أربعة: كل، ولا كل»، «كل، ولا واحد»، «بعض، ولا واحد»، «بعض، ولا بعض». وفي الحقيقية ثلاثة لأن البعض ولا بعض من جملة ما لا تقابل فيه. وأما اشتراك المقدمتين في الحدود فذلك من اللوازم وإلا لا يلزم من تركيب القياس منهما سلب الشيء عن نفسه. وإنما يخفى عليه ذلك بأن يعبر اللفظ في المقدمة ويورد بدل اسم الحد ما يراد فيه ويحكم عليه بحكم تقابل ما حكم به على الحد في المقدمة الأولى كما في قولنا كل إنسان شر ولا شيء من الشر بآدمي لينتج لا شيء من الإنسان بآدمي، أو يورد بدل الحد كلية أو جزئية ويحكم عليه برفع الحكم عن الحد: أما الأول فكقولنا كل إنسان ناطق ولا شيء من الحيوان بناطق; فإن الحكم على الحيوان حكم على الناطق لما أن
Section: On that the syllogism may be composed from contradictory premises. The syllogism may be composed from contradictory premises: that is, by composing it from an affirmative and a negative that are contradictory, such as contraries and others, as a trick and to confound the interlocutor. This syllogism is often used in dialectic for the sake of overemphasis in forcing a conclusion. The contradictory pairs in wording are four: 'every' and 'not every', 'every' and 'none', 'some' and 'none', 'some' and 'not some'. In reality they are three, because 'some' and 'not some' are among that which has no contradiction. As for the sharing of the two premises in the terms, that is necessary; otherwise, composing the syllogism from them would not entail the negation of a thing of itself. This is concealed from the opponent by wording the premise and substituting for the name of the term something intended, and judging it with a judgment contrary to what was judged of the term in the first premise, as in our saying: Every human is evil and nothing evil is human-like, to produce: No human is human-like; or by substituting for the term a universal or particular and judging it by removing the judgment from the term. As for the first, as in our saying: Every human is rational and no animal is rational; for the judgment on 'animal' is a judgment on 'rational' because...
Source pages p. 330

Commentary
This passage explains a dialectical technique: constructing a syllogism from premises that are verbally contradictory (mutanāqiḍāt fī al-lafẓ) in order to force the opponent into a corner. The author lists four pairs of contradictory quantifiers, noting that the pair 'some' and 'not some' is not a true contradiction in reality. The key requirement is that both premises share the same limits (ḥudūd); otherwise the conclusion would not follow. The trick is to rephrase one premise using a synonym or a broader term that the opponent accepts, while the judgment is the opposite of what was said in the first premise. Example: 'Every human is evil' and 'Nothing evil is human-like' yields 'No human is human-like'. This technique is common in jadal to make the opponent appear inconsistent. The passage is cut off mid-example, so the full explanation is incomplete.

The Goal of Dialectical Argumentation: Compelling the Opponent
Author gives practical advice for submitting or refusing premises in jadal.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the dialectical use of syllogism from the scientific, and identify the two types of dialectical questions and the respondent's strategic options.

Core Idea
Dialectic aims to compel the opponent, not to find truth; premises are accepted only by the opponent's submission.

In this passage, the author distinguishes between scientific and dialectical use of syllogisms. Scientific logic seeks truth, while dialectic seeks to silence the opponent by using premises they accept, even if false. Understanding this distinction is crucial for engaging in theological and legal debates following the rules of jadal.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two contexts of syllogism use
Notice how the passage contrasts syllogism used in the sciences (based on truth) and in dialectic (based on well-known or submitted premises). This sets the stage for the rest of the advice.
2
Understand the role of submission (taslim) in dialectic
The passage states that the foundation of dialectical conversation is submission (taslim) of premises via questions (masa'il). A premise need not be true or universally accepted—only accepted by the opponent. This is key to dialectical strategy.
The goal of dialectic is ilzam (compelling) and taskit (silencing), not truth.
3
Recognize the two types of dialectical questions
The author describes two types: (1) a question about premises with a conclusion (e.g., a conditional like 'If every B is J and every J is D, then not every B is D?'), where the respondent can only submit, deny a premise, or deny the entailment; (2) a question about individual premises to build a syllogism later, requiring caution in submission and assembly.
The example given uses logical letters (أ، ب، ج، د) to illustrate the structure.
4
Apply the cautionary principle when submitting premises
The passage warns against submitting a term repeatedly in a way that strengthens the opponent's argument. One must be vigilant both when accepting premises one by one and when they are assembled into a syllogism.
This is practical advice for a respondent in a dialectical exchange.

فصل : [ في الوصايا التي ينتفع بها السائل والمجيب ] / وأما الوصايا التي ينتفع بها السائل والمجيب في تسليم المقدمات [٢٦٤,] والامتناع عن تسليمها وغير ذلك؛ فإنها من جملة ما يكون من اللوازم ذكره في هذا المقام. وذلك لأن القياس قد يستعمل في العلوم وقد يستعمل في «الجدل». والذي يستعمل في العلوم فإنه يستعمل على ما عليه الأمر في نفسه، والذي يستعمل في الجدل فذلك يستعمل على ما هو مشهور أو بتسليم وإن عوثر في المشهور ولم ينتفع في الجدل؛ فعمدة المقدّمة في المحاورة الجدلية أن يكون على سبيل التسليم والتسلم والتسليم يكون بالمسألة، والمسألة كأنها هي المقدمة. ولا يلزم أن تكون المسلمة في الجدل مسلمة في نفس الأمر أو مسلمة جميع الناس بل يلزم أن تكون مسلمة عند الخصم. والجدل إنما يحاول الإلزام والتسكيت أعني القياس على نقيض ما يتعرض له الخصم لتحقيق ما ادعاه؛ وليس الغرض الجدلي من حيث هو جدلي الحق. ولو كان كذلك لكان له أن يتعرض لما هو الأقرب إلى الغرض أي أمر كان لكن من اللوازم أن يوجه كلامه كلام غيره، ولا يتعرض للاعتراض إلا وأن يتحقق عنده كلام الخصم كما هو ولا للجواب أيضًا إلا وأن يتحقق عنده الاعتراض عليه. والمسألة الجدلية أعني السؤال في الجدل إما أن تكون عن مقدمات مع نتيجة كقولك «أ» ليس إذا كان كل ب ج » وكل ج د كان كل «ب د»؛ وهذا من جملة ما لا حيلة فيه إلا التسليم أو إنكار مقدمة من مقدمات أو إنكار إنتاجه، وإما أن تكون عن مقدمة ليجتمع منها آخر الأمر القياس المنتج فيكون فيه التحفّظ على وجهين: أحدهما عند تسليم مقدمة مقدّمة، والآخر عند اجتماعها لئلا يؤلف قياسًا، والأول يجب أن يُجتهد فيه حتى لا يُسلم حدا مكرَّرًا تسليمها قياسيا فإن ذلك مما يصحح كلام الخصم ويقويه. وأما في آخر الأمر بعد
Chapter: [On Advices Beneficial for the Questioner and Respondent]. As for the advices beneficial for the questioner and respondent in the submission of premises and refraining from submitting them and other matters; they are among the necessary things to mention in this place. That is because the syllogism may be used in the sciences and may be used in dialectic. That which is used in the sciences is used according to what the matter is in itself, while that which is used in dialectic is used according to what is well-known or by submission, even if there is a flaw in the well-known; and one does not benefit in dialectic. So the foundation of the premise in dialectical conversation is on the basis of submission and receiving; and submission occurs by the question, and the question is as if it is the premise. And it is not necessary that what is submitted in dialectic be submitted in actual fact or submitted by all people, but it is necessary that it be submitted by the opponent. Dialectic only aims at compelling and silencing, that is, a syllogism against the opposite of what the opponent puts forth to achieve what he claimed; and the dialectical purpose insofar as it is dialectical is not truth. And if it were so, he would have the right to address whatever is closest to the purpose, any matter whatsoever, but it is from the necessities that he directs his speech to the speech of others, and he does not address an objection unless he is certain of the opponent's discourse as it is, nor does he respond unless he is certain of the objection against him. And the dialectical question, that is, the question in dialectic, is either about premises with a conclusion, like your saying: 'A is not, if every B is J and every J is D, then every B is D.' This is among the things in which there is no trick except submission or denial of one of the premises or denial of its production. Or it is about a premise so that ultimately the productive syllogism is assembled from it; and in that there is caution in two ways: one at the time of submitting premises one by one, and the other at the time of their assembly to prevent composing a syllogism. And the first must be strived for so that one does not submit a term repeatedly, whose submission is syllogistic, for that corrects the opponent's discourse and strengthens it. And as for at the end after...
Source pages p. 338

Commentary
This passage begins a new chapter (fasl) offering practical advice for participants in dialectical argumentation (jadal). The author explains that syllogism is used both in scientific inquiry (based on truth) and in dialectic (based on commonly accepted notions or the opponent's admissions). In dialectical conversation, the foundation is submission (taslim) of premises via questions (masa'il). A premise in dialectic need not be true in reality or accepted by everyone; it only needs to be accepted by the opponent. The goal of dialectic is to compel and silence the opponent by constructing a syllogism that contradicts their position, not to uncover absolute truth. The author outlines two types of dialectical questions: (1) a question about premises together with a conclusion (e.g., a conditional statement like 'If every B is J and every J is D, then not every B is D?')—where the respondent can only submit, deny a premise, or deny the entailment; and (2) a question about individual premises to build a syllogism later—in which caution is needed both when submitting premises one by one and when assembling them to prevent the opponent from forming a syllogism. Specifically, one must avoid submitting a term repeatedly in a way that helps the opponent's argument.

The fundamental principle of jadal is that premises need only be accepted by the opponent, not true in themselves, because dialectic aims at compulsion, not truth.

Dialectic -- Jadal

Rules of Premise Acceptance in Dialectical Argumentation
Author explains rules of premise acceptance and denial in dialectical argumentation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the purpose of dialectic from scientific demonstration and apply the rules of premise acceptance in a jadal exchange.

Core Idea
Dialectic does not seek truth; it seeks to silence the opponent using his own premises.

In the Islamic logical tradition, syllogisms are not only tools for discovering truth but also weapons for debate. The author here lays down the ground rules for dialectical argumentation (jadal), which differs fundamentally from scientific demonstration. Understanding these rules is essential for anyone engaging in theological or juristic disputation, where the goal is not to establish absolute truth but to force the opponent into inconsistency using premises they already accept.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two contexts of syllogism use
The author states that syllogisms are used in the sciences (where truth is sought) and in dialectic (where only the opponent's acceptance matters). Note the contrast: in sciences, premises must be true in themselves; in dialectic, they need only be accepted by the opponent.
2
Determine the goal of dialectic
The author explicitly says the dialectical purpose is not truth but 'ilzām' (compelling) and 'taskīt' (silencing). The aim is to construct a syllogism whose conclusion contradicts the opponent's claim, thereby refuting them.
This distinguishes jadal from burhan (demonstration).
3
Distinguish the two types of dialectical questions
The dialectical question is either about a complete syllogism (premises + conclusion) or about a single premise that will later be combined into a syllogism. In the first case, the opponent must accept or deny a premise or the inference. In the second case, the questioner must be cautious about which premises are accepted.
The author gives an example: 'If every B is J and every J is D, then every B is D.'
4
Apply the questioner's strategy for ordering demands
For a compound syllogism (e.g., Z→H→D→J→B→A), the questioner should demand the major premise first to disrupt the order and make it harder for the answerer to see the full argument. For a simple syllogism, the questioner should directly demand the relation of the major term to the middle term.
The author illustrates with a chain: every Z is H, every H is D, etc.
5
Analyze the answerer's caution
The answerer must avoid accepting a term repeatedly (which would strengthen the opponent's argument), and after accepting premises, must reflect on the figure and mood to check if the syllogism is valid for the sought conclusion.
The author notes that the second figure does not produce affirmative conclusions, and the third does not produce universal conclusions.

النسبة التي للحد الأكبر إلى الأوسط فإذا فعل هذا حدث قياس على نقيض الموضوع وهو التسكيت. فالتسكيت قياس؛ وعلى شرائطه في الأشكال والضروب على ما عرف.
Chapter: [On the Advices Beneficial to the Questioner and Answerer] As for the advices beneficial to the questioner and answerer regarding the acceptance of premises, refraining from accepting them, and otherwise; they are among the necessary things to mention in this context. That is because the syllogism is used in the sciences and is used in 'dialectic'. That which is used in the sciences is used according to how the matter is in itself, while that which is used in dialectic is used according to what is well-known or by acceptance, even if it is flawed in the well-known and is not beneficial in dialectic. So the foundation of the premise in the dialectical exchange is that it be on the basis of acceptance and concession, and acceptance occurs by the question, and the question is as if it is the premise. It is not necessary that what is accepted in dialectic be accepted in reality or accepted by all people, but it must be accepted by the opponent. Dialectic only aims at compelling and silencing [the opponent], meaning the syllogism for the opposite of what the opponent presents to verify what he claimed; the dialectical purpose, insofar as it is dialectical, is not truth. If it were, he would be able to present what is closest to the purpose, whatever it may be, but it is necessary that he direct his speech to the speech of others, and he does not present an objection unless he has verified the opponent's speech as it is, nor does he present a response unless he has verified the objection against it. The dialectical question—meaning the question in dialectic—is either about premises with a conclusion, like your saying: 'Is it not that if every B is J and every J is D, then every B is D?' This is among the things for which there is no recourse except acceptance or denial of one of the premises or denial of its inference. Or it is about a premise to eventually combine from it the productive syllogism, in which case there is caution in two ways: one at the acceptance of premise by premise, and the other at their combination lest he compose a syllogism. The first must be striven for so that he does not accept a term repeatedly whose acceptance is syllogistic, for that strengthens and validates the opponent's speech. As for after acceptance, he must reflect on how the accepted middle term relates to the two extremes so that he knows the figure and mood, for it is possible that the figure is not productive for that sought conclusion, like the second for the affirmative and the third for the universal. This can only be achieved after memorizing the figures of syllogisms and their moods; and it is among what is necessary for the affirmative to observe. As for the questioner, he must choose in reflection what contradicts the speech of the answerer, by demanding premise by premise and preventing it if possible, otherwise accepting it if acceptance is necessary. All this with caution individually so that he can compose from what he prevents and accepts a syllogism that produces the opposite of the claim. There are thus ways of ordering. For example, if he demonstrates that every Z is A, and he demonstrates that by: every Z is H, and every H is D, and every D is J, and every J is B, and every B is A, then every Z is A. The demander either demands about the premises of the extremes; then he must demand the major premise, because if he demands the minor premise, the answerer guesses that perhaps every H is D or something else on the order other than 'H D'. But if he demands the major premise, the speech then reverses the order, so the occurrence of the two terms is less. For if it is said: 'every B is A' and 'every Z is B' explicitly, it is not upon the syllogistic composition. Rather, it is better to demand about the major premise, saying: 'Is it not that every B is A?' This is the denial; otherwise it is not by direction. Then he distances himself from it and does not demand what he answers but demands about what is far from it, so he demands about 'every H is D,' then returns to demand about what is between them. Similarly, he strives to cause a difference in the order, for there are benefits in that. This is when the syllogism is compound. As for when the syllogism is simple, he must first demand about the middle term by saying first: 'Is every B A?' For in it is a demand for the relation of the major term to the middle. When he does this, a syllogism occurs on the opposite of the subject, which is silencing. Silencing is a syllogism, and upon its conditions in figures and moods as is known.
Source pages p. 338

Commentary
This passage defines the dialectical method and provides strategic advice for the questioner and answerer. The author begins by stating that syllogisms are used in two contexts: in the sciences (where truth is sought) and in dialectic (where what is accepted by the opponent is the basis). In dialectic, the key is to secure premises the opponent acknowledges, even if they are not true in themselves. The goal is not truth but 'ilzām' (compulsion) and 'taskīt' (silencing) by constructing a syllogism whose conclusion contradicts the opponent's claim. The author then distinguishes two types of dialectical questions: one about a complete syllogism (premises + conclusion) where the opponent must either accept or deny a premise or the inference; the other about a single premise that will later be combined into a syllogism. In the latter case, the questioner must be cautious: first, not to accept a premise that could be used as a repeated term to strengthen the opponent's argument; second, after accepting premises, to analyze the figure and mood of the resulting syllogism to see if it is valid. The author gives an example of a compound syllogism with multiple terms (Z, H, D, J, B, A) and advises the questioner to demand the major premise first to disrupt the order. For simple syllogisms, the questioner should directly demand the relation of the major term to the middle term, which immediately produces a counter-syllogism.

Dialectical Duties of Responder and Questioner in Syllogistic Debate
Author explains dialectical duties for compound and simple syllogisms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to differentiate the duties of the responder and questioner in a dialectical debate and apply the correct objection strategy for compound and simple syllogisms.

Core Idea
Know your figures; attack the right premise to silence the opponent.

In Islamic dialectic (jadal), the responder (mujīb) and questioner (sāʾil) follow strict protocols when dealing with syllogisms. This passage from a classical logic text explains how to navigate compound and simple syllogisms to either defend a claim or refute it. Understanding these roles is crucial for constructing and deconstructing arguments in theological and legal debates.

Guided Path
1
Identify the roles of responder and questioner
The responder (al-mujīb) must reflect on the conceded middle term to know the figure and mood. The questioner (al-sāʾil) must choose objections that contradict the responder's speech, demanding each premise one by one.
These roles are standard in jadal.
2
Analyze the compound syllogism example
The author gives a chain: every Z is H, every H is D, every D is J, every J is B, every B is A, therefore every Z is A. The questioner should object to the major premise (every B is A) because objecting to the minor gives the responder room to adjust.
This disrupts the order.
3
Examine the simple syllogism strategy
For a simple syllogism (two premises), the questioner should first attack the middle term by asking 'Is every B A?' This targets the relation between the major and middle term, producing a counter-syllogism that silences the opponent.
This is called taskīt.
4
Apply the strategy to a new example
Given a claim 'every intoxicant is haram' proven via a simple syllogism with major premise 'every intoxicant is haram' and minor 'every nabidh is intoxicant', decide where the questioner should object.
Use the rule from the passage: object to the middle term.

التسليم فيجب أن يتأمل في أن الواسطة المسلمة كيف نسبتها إلى الطرفين حتى يعرف الشكل والضرب فإنه يمكن أن لا يكون الشكل منتجا لذلك المطلوب كالثاني للموجب والثالث للكلي، وهذا إنما يتأتى بعد حفظه أشكال القياسات وضروبها؛ وإنه من جملة ما يلزم للموجب أن يراعي وأما السائل فيجب أن يختار في التأمل إلى ما يناقض كلام المجيب، وذلك بأن يطالب مقدمة مقدّمة ويمنعها إن أمكن وإلا فتسلمها إن كان التسلم ضروريا؛ وهذه كلها مع التحفظ فردًا فردًا حتى يمكنه أن يؤلف من جملة ما يمنعه ويسلمه قياسا ينتج نقيض المدعي. ولذلك وجوه من الترتيب مثلا إن كان يبين أن كل ز أ ؛ وكان ينتج ذلك بأن كل ز ه وكل هـ د وكل د ج وكل ج ب وكل ب أ ) فكل ز أ ) . فالمطالب إما أن يطالب عن مقدمات الأطراف؛ وحينئذ يلزم أن يطالب الكبرى فإنه إذا طالب عن الصغرى حدس المجيب إنه عسى أن يكون كل هـد أو شيئًا آخر مما على النظام بخلاف هـ د ما إذا طالب عن الكبرى فإن الكلام حينئذ ينعكس عن الترتيب، فكان وقوع الحدين أقل لأنه إذا قيل كل ب أ وكل ز ب مصر حا لم يكن على التركيب القياسي بل الأولى أن يطالب عن الكبرى . فيقول أ ليس كل ب أ؛ وهذا هو الإنكار وإلا لا يكون بالتوجيه ثم يتباعد عنه ولا يطالب عما هو يجيبه بل عن البعيد عنه فيطالب عن كل هـ د، ثم يعود فيطالب عما بينهما. وكذلك يجتهد أن يوقع اختلافًا في الترتيب فإن فيه من الفوائد: هذا إذا كان القياس مركبا. فأما إذا كان القياس بسيطًا فيجب أن يطالب أولا عن الواسطة بأن يقول أولاً هل كل ب أ؛ فإن فيه مطالبة النسبة التي للحد الأكبر إلى الأوسط فإذا فعل هذا حدث قياس على نقيض الموضوع وهو التسكيت. فالتسكيت قياس؛ وعلى شرائطه في الأشكال والضروب على ما عرف.
So it is necessary for the responder to reflect on how the conceded middle term relates to the two extremes so that he knows the figure and mood, for it is possible that the figure is not productive for that desired conclusion, like the second for affirmative and the third for universal. And this only comes after memorizing the figures of syllogisms and their moods. And it is among the necessary things for the respondent to observe. As for the questioner, he must choose in reflection what contradicts the speech of the responder, by demanding premise by premise and preventing it if possible, otherwise conceding it if concession is necessary. And all this with caution, one by one, so that he can compose from what he prevents and concedes a syllogism that produces the contradictory of the claim. And for that there are ways of ordering. For example, if he demonstrates that every Z is A, and he produces that by: every Z is H, and every H is D, and every D is J, and every J is B, and every B is A, so every Z is A. Then the objector either objects to the premises of the extremes; and then it is necessary to object to the major premise, because if he objects to the minor premise, the responder might conjecture that possibly every H is D or something else following the order, unlike H D? But if he objects to the major premise, then the speech turns away from the order, so the occurrence of the two extremes is less. Because if it is said 'every B is A' and 'every Z is B' explicitly, it would not be in the syllogistic composition; rather, it is better to object to the major premise. So he says 'A is not every B A'; and this is denial, otherwise it is not with direction. Then he distances himself and does not object to what he answers, but to what is far from it. So he objects to 'every H D', then returns to object to what is between them. Likewise he strives to cause a difference in the order, for there are benefits in that. This is if the syllogism is compound. But if the syllogism is simple, then he must first object to the middle term by saying firstly: 'Is every B A?' For in that there is an objection to the relation of the major extreme to the middle. If he does this, a syllogism arises on the contradictory of the subject, which is silencing. So silencing is a syllogism, and according to its conditions in figures and moods as is known.
Source pages p. 339

Commentary
This passage discusses dialectical strategy in syllogistic debates. It opens by instructing the responder (mujib) to carefully consider the conceded middle term's relation to the extremes to determine the figure and mood, since not all figures yield all conclusions—e.g., the second figure cannot produce affirmative conclusions and the third cannot produce universal ones. The responder must memorize the valid figures and moods. The text then turns to the questioner (sa'il), who must choose objections that contradict the responder's speech. The method is to demand each premise individually: prevent it if possible, otherwise concede if necessary, while carefully tracking each step to eventually construct a syllogism that yields the contradictory of the opponent's claim. The author gives an example of a compound syllogism: if the responder proves 'every Z is A' through a chain (Z-H, H-D, D-J, J-B, B-A), the questioner should object to the extreme premises (the ends of the chain), specifically the major premise. Objecting to the minor premise allows the responder to adjust, but objecting to the major disrupts the order. The questioner then distances himself by objecting to a remote premise like 'every H is D' and later to intermediate ones. For a simple syllogism (with only two premises), the questioner should first attack the middle term by asking 'Is every B A?'—this targets the relation between the major term and the middle term, and if successful, produces a counter-syllogism that silences the opponent. Silencing (taskit) is itself a syllogism subject to the rules of figures and moods.

Preference Argument with Opposite Terms
Author presents a reductio proof that if A+C is better than B+D, then A is better than D, given opposites.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the reductio proof of the preference argument and identify its logical structure.

Core Idea
Comparative value judgments can be proven using opposites and reductio.

This passage presents a dialectical proof that if one combination of desired and avoided items is better than another, then a specific component must be better. It uses the relationship between opposites (desired/avoided) and a reductio ad absurdum. Understanding this argument sharpens your ability to analyze comparative claims in Islamic dialectics.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim and the given opposites
Note that A and D are sought (maṭlūb), B and C are fled from (ma hrūb ʿanhu), and A opposite B, D opposite C. The claim: if A+C is better than B+D, then A is better than D.
The opposites ensure that the desirability of one determines the undesirability of the other.
2
State the assumption to be disproven
Assume A is not better than D. Then either A equals D in desirability, or D is better than A. The proof will derive a contradiction in each case.
This is the reductio structure: assume the negation and derive an impossibility.
3
Derive the contradiction for the equality case
If A equals D in seeking, then their opposites B and C must equal in fleeing. Then A+C and B+D would have equal totals of seeking and fleeing, contradicting the premise that A+C is better.
The equality of totals violates the 'better than' relation.
4
Derive the contradiction for the D-better case
If D is better than A, then its opposite C must be more fled from than B. But then A+C (with a stronger sought and weaker fled) would not be better than B+D (with a weaker sought and stronger fled), again a contradiction.
The text is cut off but the logical structure is clear.
5
Conclude the original claim
Both assumptions lead to contradictions, so the original conditional must hold: if A+C > B+D, then A > D.
This shows how reductio validates comparative claims.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ونقول إنه ليكن «أ» و «د» مطلوبين و «ج» و «ب» مهروبًا عنهما و «أ» و «ب» متقابلان: فنقول إذا كان «أ» و «ج» كلاهما مجموعين أفضل من ((ب)) و ((د) مجموعين فإن «أ» أفضل من «د». وذلك لأن «أ» مطلوب كما أن «ب» مهروب عنه لأنهما متقابلان. وكذلك «ج» مطلوب مثل ما أن «د» مهروب عنه لأنهما يتقابلان. فإن لم يكن «أ» أفضل من ((د) فإما أن يكون مساويا لـ «د»، «أ» ويكون «د» أفضل لكنه إن كان مساويًا لـ «د» في أنه مطلوب فيجب أن يكون أضدادهما متساويين في أنهما مهروب عنهما أعني «أ» لـ «ج» و «أ» لـ «ب» ، فإذا جمع إلى «أ» «ج» اجتمع مطلوب ومهروب عنه. وإذا جمع إلى ب د اجتمع مطلوب ومهروب عنه. وكان جملة ذينك في الطلب والهرب لجملة هذين فلم يكن مجموع «أ» «ج أ» فضل من مجموع «ب» د ؛ وقد كان أفضل، هذا خلف. وإما إن قلنا إن «د» أفضل من «أ» في باب أنه مؤثر مطلوب فضد الدال الذي هو في غاية الخلاف
And we say: let A and D be sought after, and C and B be fled from, and A and B are opposites: so we say: if A and C together are better than B and D together, then A is better than D. That is because A is sought after just as B is fled from because they are opposites. Similarly, C is sought after just as D is fled from because they are opposites. If A is not better than D, then either A is equal to D or D is better. But if A is equal to D in being sought after, then their opposites must be equal in being fled from, i.e., the opposite of A, which is B, and the opposite of D, which is C. So when A is combined with C, a sought and a fled combine. And when B is combined with D, a sought and a fled combine. And the total of the two in seeking and fleeing would be equal to the total of the other two, so the sum of A and C would not be better than the sum of B and D, but we assumed it was better, a contradiction. And if we say D is better than A in the sense that it is a preferred sought, then the opposite of D is in extreme opposition...
Source pages p. 345

Commentary
This passage presents a logical argument about preferences using opposites. The author sets up a scenario where A and D are sought (desired), B and C are fled from (avoided), and A/B are opposites, C/D are opposites. The claim is: if the combination of A and C is better than B and D, then A is better than D. The proof is by reductio: assume A is not better than D, so either equal or worse. If equal, then their opposites (B and C) must be equal in being fled from. Then A+C and B+D would be equal in desirability, contradicting the assumption that A+C is better. If D is better than A, then the opposite of D (C) would be more fled from than A's opposite (B), but the initial condition had A+C better than B+D, which would be violated. The text is cut off but the argument is a form of comparative reasoning used in dialectics and ethics.

Three Dialectical Methods for Proving Body as Cause of Temporality
Author enumerates and critiques three methods for proving that body is the cause of temporality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three dialectical methods for proving that body is the cause of temporal origination and evaluate why each fails to establish the universal claim.

Core Idea
Dialectical methods can prove particular cases but not universal causality.

In this passage, the author presents three methods used by dialecticians (ahl al-jadal) to prove that body (jism) is the cause of temporal origination (huduth). He then critiques each method, showing that even if they work for specific instances, they do not prove that body-ness (jismiyya) as such is the universal cause. By studying these methods, you will sharpen your ability to judge the scope and validity of dialectical arguments.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three methods
Read the Arabic and translation to list the three methods: induction, condition-based reasoning, and a conditional proof using a preventer (mani').
The first method is based on incomplete induction (istiqra' naqis); the second adds a condition about the body's attributes; the third uses a conditional syllogism.
2
Analyse the first method's flaw
The author says the first method reduces to incomplete induction, which is not certain unless complete—but that is rare. Identify why incomplete induction fails to give certainty.
Incomplete induction only covers some cases, so it cannot prove a universal cause.
3
Examine the second method's condition
The second method imposes that the occurrence is not due to the body being existent or self-subsistent. The author notes it is open to discussion. Ask: why might this condition be problematic?
The condition might be arbitrary or insufficiently justified.
4
Reconstruct the third method as a conditional syllogism
The third method: if body were not the cause, there would be a preventer (mani') of temporality. But the building is temporally originated, so body is the cause. Write this in conditional form: If not (body is cause) then not (building is originated). Building is originated, therefore body is cause.
This is modus tollens: If P then Q, not Q, therefore not P. Here P: body is cause, Q: building is originated. Actually it's: If not (body is cause) then (building not originated). But building is originated, so body is cause. Equivalent to modus ponens? Better: If (body not cause) then (building not originated). Building originated, so body is cause. That's modus tollens: If not A then not B, B, therefore A.
5
Identify the overarching critique
The author concludes that all three methods, even if they prove that a particular building's body is the cause, do not prove that body-ness as such is the cause universally. He says that body-ness is perpetual, so it cannot be the cause of temporality, which is not perpetual.
The universal claim requires that every body is temporally originated because of its body-ness, but the methods only show that in specific cases the body is a cause.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
منحصرة فيما ذكرتم من الأقسام وفيه من الكلام؛ إذ هو راجع إلى الاستقراء الناقص، وذلك لا يفيد كما مر. اللهم إلا أن يكون الاستقراء تاما؛ وذلك نادر. ومنها أن يقال: إنه لا يتم إلا وأن لا يكون حدوثه لكونه موجودًا أو قائما بنفسه مثلا؛ وفيه من الكلام أيضًا. ومنها أن يقال: إنه لا يتم إلا وأن لا يكون حدوثه لذاته مع واحد من هذه الأوصاف وإن قيل لا يمكن أن يكون لهذا المجموع، ولا لذلك. وذلك كذلك بدليل التخلف فنقول هب أنه كذلك لكن لم قلتم بأنه إنما يكون لكونه جسما يمكن أن يكون لكونه جسما مع الغير. والثاني أن يقال: إن الجسمية علة للحدوث لما أن الحدوث دائر معه وجودًا وعدما أما وجودًا ففي ذلك البناء وذلك؛ إذ الجسمية موجودة والحادث ثابت. وأما عدمًا ففي صورة عدم الجسمية كما في ذات الصانع تعالى وتقدَّس وصفاته القديمة. والمعنى من الحدوث هو الحادث الزماني لا الحدوث الذاتي؛ إذ الأجسام كلها حادثة بذلك الحدوث عند الكل. ولا يقال يشكل بالأمور الاتفاقية وغيرها؛ إذ الكلام فيما يكون صالحًا للعلية: وهذا هو الطريق المستعمل عند أهل الجدل؛ وفيه من الكلام ما فيه. والثالث أن يقال: إنها علة للحدوث وإلا لا يكون ذلك البناء حادثا بالمانع عن الحدوث وهو الذي يدل على امتناع وجود الشيء السالم عن معارضة كونه علة. وقد كان ذلك البناء حادثًا فتكون الجسمية علة، وهذا هو الطريق المستعمل عند أهل الجدل وغيرهم. ثم هذه الأدلة وإن كانت دالة على كون البناء جسما علة للحدوث فلا تكون دالة على الجسمية من حيث هي الجسمية علة. وقد كانت عليتها من جملة المستحيلات فإن الجسمية للأجسام على سبيل الدوام والحدوث لا يمكن أن تكون كذلك.
Restricted to the divisions you mentioned, and there is discussion in it; for it goes back to incomplete induction, and that does not yield benefit as previously mentioned. Unless the induction is complete, and that is rare. Among them is to say: it is not complete except that its occurrence is not due to its being existent or subsisting by itself, for example; and there is discussion in that as well. Among them is to say: it is not complete except that its occurrence is not due to itself together with one of these descriptions, even if it is said that it cannot be for this collective nor for that. And that is so by the proof of [؟] (al-takhalluf), so we say: suppose it is so, but why did you say that it is only because it is a body? It could be because it is a body together with something else. The second: to say that body-ness is the cause of temporality because temporality coexists with it in existence and non-existence. As for existence, then in that building etc.; because body-ness exists and the originated thing is established. As for non-existence, then in the case of absence of body-ness, as in the essence of the Maker, exalted and sanctified, and His eternal attributes. And the meaning of temporality here is temporal origination, not essential origination; for all bodies are originated by that temporality according to everyone. And it is not said that it is problematic due to accidental matters etc.; for the discussion is about what is suitable for causality. And this is the method used by the dialecticians; and there is discussion in it. The third: to say that it is the cause of temporality, otherwise that building would not be originated due to the preventer of temporality, which indicates the impossibility of the existence of a thing free from opposition to its being a cause. And that building was originated, so body-ness is the cause. This is the method used by the dialecticians and others. Then these proofs, although they indicate that the building being a body is the cause of temporality, they do not indicate that body-ness as such is the cause. And its being a cause is among the impossibilities, for body-ness for bodies is perpetual, and temporality cannot be that.
Source pages p. 354

Commentary
This passage presents three dialectical methods used to prove that the body is the cause of temporal origination (huduth). The author first dismisses the first method because it relies on incomplete induction (istiqra' naqis), which does not yield certainty unless the induction is complete, which is rare. The second method imposes a condition that the occurrence of temporality is not due to the body being existent or self-subsistent etc., but the author notes there is discussion. The third method uses a conditional argument: if the body were not the cause, there would be a preventer (mani') of temporality, but the building is temporally originated, so it is the cause. However, the author then critiques all three: even if they prove that the building's body is the cause in a particular instance, they do not prove that body-ness (jismiyya) as such is the cause, which is impossible because body-ness is perpetual while temporality is not. Thus the methods fail to establish the universal claim.

Key Terms
الاستقراء الناقص

Defining Qiyas al-Muqawamah and Its Two Types
Author defines qiyas al-muqawamah and divides it into opposition and contradiction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify qiyas al-muqawamah and distinguish between its two types: inād and munāqaḍa.

Core Idea
Qiyas al-muqawamah is a dialectical weapon to refute a universal premise.

In debates, you often need to refute an opponent's premise without proving your own position directly. This lesson introduces qiyas al-muqawamah, a syllogism designed precisely for that purpose. The author defines it and divides it into two subtypes, which we will parse step by step.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definiendum
Find the term being defined (قياس المقاومة). Note that it is a type of syllogism (قياس).
2
Extract the genus and differentia
The genus is 'syllogism composed of two premises'. The differentia: its conclusion is opposite to a premise of another syllogism, thereby invalidating that premise.
The purpose is polemical (jadal), not demonstrative.
3
Understand the target of refutation
The text specifies that the target is the universal premise (المقدمة الكلية) of the other syllogism, because it is the most evident (أبين) part. This is the premise that the resistant syllogism aims to contradict.
4
Distinguish the two types
The author divides muqawamah into inād (opposition) and munāqaḍa (contradiction). For inād, the major premise of your counter-syllogism must be more general than the target premise and opposite in quality (e.g., universal affirmative vs. universal negative).
Munāqaḍa is not detailed in this passage but is mentioned as a second type.
5
Apply the distinction to an example
Though the example in the text is incomplete, try to construct one. If the opponent's universal premise is 'Every A is B', you might construct a syllogism with major premise 'No C is B' where C is a more general category than A, leading to a conclusion that contradicts 'Every A is B'.
Your counter-syllogism must be valid in form to be effective.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل : [ في قياس المقاومة] وأما قياس المقاومة فهو قياس مؤلف من مقدمتين منتج مقابل مقدمة هي في الغير من الأقيسة. ولما كان منتجًا مقابل مقدمة من مقدمات ذلك القياس كان مبطلا ذلك فالمقاومة يقصد بها قصد إبطال المقدّمة الكلية / التي في القياس فإنها أبين القياس؛ وذلك على وجهين : إما عنادًا وإما مناقضة. [أ] أما العناد فهو أن تجعل المقدّمة الكبرى في القياس الذي تقابل به المقدمة الكلية أشد عموماً من تلك المقدمة ومخالفة لها في الكيفية مثلا إذا كان الحكم في
Section: On the Syllogism of Resistance. As for the syllogism of resistance, it is a syllogism composed of two premises that produces a conclusion opposite to a premise that is in another of the syllogisms. And since it produces a conclusion opposite to a premise among the premises of that syllogism, it invalidates it. So resistance aims at invalidating the universal premise that is in the syllogism, for it is the most evident part of the syllogism. And that is in two ways: either by opposition or by contradiction. [A] As for opposition, it is that you make the major premise in the syllogism with which you oppose the universal premise more general than that premise and contrary to it in quality, for example, if the judgment is...
Source pages p. 356

Commentary
This passage introduces a new section defining 'qiyas al-muqawamah' (syllogism of resistance). The author defines it as a syllogism whose conclusion opposes a premise of another syllogism, thereby refuting it. The aim is to invalidate the universal premise of the target syllogism, which is the most evident part. This type of argument is divided into two types: 'inād (opposition) and munāqaḍa (contradiction). The former is elaborated: one constructs a syllogism whose major premise is more general than the universal premise of the target and opposite in quality (e.g., universal affirmative vs. universal negative). An example is begun but cut off. This is a definitional passage, and the student should parse the technical terms: muqāwamah, inād, munāqaḍa, and their roles in refutation.

مناقضة (munāqaḍa)

Key Takeaway
Qiyas al-muqawamah is a dialectical syllogism used to refute a universal premise of another argument, and it comes in two forms: inād (using a more general opposite premise) and munāqaḍa (contradiction).

Analyzing Dialectical Refinement of Causal Claims
Author presents and rebuts objections against bodily nature as cause of origination.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to analyze dialectical objections to a causal claim and identify the logical principles used in their rebuttals.

Core Idea
A cause must be complete and free of preventatives to produce its effect.

In this passage, the author defends the claim that bodily nature (jismiyyah) is the cause of origination (hudduth) by addressing a series of objections. Each rebuttal relies on a specific logical principle: the distinction between knowledge and existence, the denial of exclusivity, and the requirement of a complete cause (al-illah al-tammah). By studying these responses, you will learn how dialectical reasoning refines and strengthens a causal thesis in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the main causal claim
Find the thesis being defended: bodily nature is the cause of origination. Note that the passage does not argue for it directly but handles objections against it.
The author is engaged in a dialectical (munazara) context.
2
List each objection
Four objections are presented: (1) The cause could be the essence, not a description. (2) The cause is not exclusive to bodily nature. (3) This causality would only be known after induction. (4) Even if true, it would entail the sky is originated.
The objections challenge the specificity, exclusivity, knowability, and entailment of the causal claim.
3
Extract the logical principle in each response
For each objection, identify the principle the author uses: (1) Even if caused by description, why this one? (2) Exclusivity is denied. (3) Lack of knowledge does not prove non-existence. (4) The sky may have preventatives, and the cause must be complete.
These principles are common in usul al-fiqh and kalam.
4
Assess the dialectical strength
Evaluate whether the author's response meets the objection. Does it shift the burden of proof? Does it rely on accepted logical rules? In each case, the author does not refute the possibility of other causes but shows the objections are not conclusive.
The author successfully defends the plausibility of his thesis without proving it outright.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يجوز أن يكون معللا بالذات التي هو عارض لها فإن من الأحكام ما تكون عللها ذوات معروضاتها. وإن سلمنا أنه معلل بوصف لكن لم قلتم بأنه معلل بهذا دون غيره. ولا يقال إنها منحصرة فيما ذكرنا؛ وغيره لا يكون علة فيتعين هذا؛ إذ الانحصار في المنع لما أنه وهو البناء مؤلف من العناصر الأربعة. وذلك التركيب كان صادرًا من الآدمي في زمان معين على وجه لا يبعد أن يصدر مثله عن مثله بخلاف السماء. وإن قيل هب أنه كذلك لكن الصالح للعلية ليس إلا أو للجسمية، فنقول هذا في حيز المنع. وإن قيل لو كان لكان معلوما بعد التكلّف في الاستقراء فنقول عدم العلم لا يدل على عدم الوجود. وإن سلمنا أن الجسمية علة للحدوث لكن لم قلتم بأنه يلزم منه أن تكون السماء حادثة. وإنما يكون كذلك إن لو لم تكن السماء مشتملة على الموانع يمكن أن يكون لها من الخواص ما يكون مانعا عن الحدوث. ولأن العلة لا تكون مؤثرة إلا وأن تكون تامة وهي المستجمعة لجميع الشرائط وكونها تامة في كل صورة من هاتين الصورتين لا يكون متحققا بل في حيز المنع على ما عرف. وأما الاعتراضات على الطرفين الآخرين فإنها من جملة المشهورات فلا حاجة إلى التعرض لها في معرض الإثبات.
It is possible that it is caused by the essence which it is an accident of, for among rulings are those whose causes are the substances of their subjects. And if we grant that it is caused by a description, why do you say it is caused by this specifically and not another? It should not be said that they are restricted to what we mentioned, and other than it cannot be a cause, so this one is determined, because the restriction is denied—since it and the building are composed of the four elements. And that composition was produced by a human in a specific time in a manner not unlikely that something similar could be produced by someone similar, unlike the sky. And if it is said: suppose it is so, but the only suitable thing for causality is the bodily nature, we say this is debatable. And if it is said: if it were so, it would be known only after effort in induction, we say: lack of knowledge does not indicate lack of existence. And if we grant that bodily nature is the cause of origination, why do you say that it necessarily follows that the sky is originated? It would be so only if the sky did not contain preventatives—it is possible that it has properties that prevent origination. And because the cause is not effective unless it is complete, i.e., comprising all conditions, and its being complete in each of these two forms is not actualized but rather is debatable as is known. As for the objections on the other two sides, they are among the commonplaces, so there is no need to address them in the context of proof.
Source pages p. 356

Commentary
This passage is a series of dialectical objections and responses concerning a specific argument that bodily nature (jismiyyah) is the cause of origination (hudduth). The author begins by allowing that a ruling can be caused by the very substance it inheres in, but then challenges the opponent: even if we accept that it is caused by a description, why must it be this particular description? The objection of exclusivity (that other descriptions cannot be causes) is rejected, using the example of a building composed of four elements which is constructed by a human in a specific time, unlike the sky. The opponent then proposes that only bodily nature is suitable as cause, but the author denies this. Another objection—that this would be known only after strenuous induction—is met with the principle that absence of knowledge does not prove absence of existence. Even accepting bodily nature as cause, the opponent claims it entails that the sky is originated; the author counters that the sky may have special properties that prevent origination, and moreover, a cause must be complete (tammah) in all conditions, which is not established here. The author concludes that objections on other sides are well-known and need not be repeated. The passage demonstrates careful refutation of objections in a causal argument, typical of critical refinement in logical works.

جسمية
علة تامة

Key Takeaway
A causal claim is only established when the cause is complete and no preventatives are present; objections that ignore these conditions are dialectically weak.
