Boundaries of Dialectic vs Demonstration
Author contrasts dialectic and demonstration concerning subject, questions, and principles.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between dialectic and demonstration based on their boundaries concerning subject, questions, and principles, using examples from the Islamic sciences.

Core Idea
Dialectic has no boundaries; demonstration is strictly bounded.

This lesson examines a foundational distinction in Islamic logic: the difference between dialectic (jadal) and demonstration (burhan). While both use syllogisms, they differ fundamentally in their scope and purpose. Dialectic argues any side with available premises, while demonstration is confined to a specific subject, limited questions, and foundational principles. This contrast is essential for understanding how different sciences are structured and what questions each can legitimately address.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two methods contrasted
Read the passage and locate where the author introduces dialectic and demonstration. Note that dialectic is described as unlimited, while demonstration is limited.
The Arabic terms are جَدَل and بُرْهَان.
2
Analyze dialectic's lack of boundaries in subject and questions
The passage states that dialectic does not confine itself to essential questions; it can also deal with extraneous attributes (e.g., whether a straight line is better than a circular line). It may also support both sides of a contradiction using different syllogisms at different times.
3
Analyze dialectic's lack of boundaries in principles
Dialectic does not require principles to be essential; it uses common, often contradictory premises accepted by the interlocutor. It can even use opposite premises for opposite conclusions.
4
Examine demonstration's strict boundaries
Demonstration has a limited subject, limited questions (only those that can be demonstrated as true), and limited principles (only true and appropriate to the science). The passage emphasizes that absolute scientific questions are bounded and belong to a specific science.
5
Understand the hierarchy of sciences and principles
The author uses geometry and optics to illustrate that a science's principles cannot be proven within that science. The geometer cannot prove geometry's principles qua geometer; to do so, he must become a philosopher (metaphysician). The optician cannot prove optics' principles qua optician; to do so, he must become a geometer. All ultimate principles are demonstrated in metaphysics.
This hierarchy is key to the structure of the Islamic sciences.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
في المسائل فذلك من وجهين: أحدهما أنه لا يقتصر على المسائل الذاتية بالموضوع في الوقت بل وفي الغريبة أيضًا مثل أنه ليس بنظر هل الخط المستقيم إذا قام عليه خط كان كذا وكذا بل هو أحسن من المستدير أو ليس؟ وهل هو مضاد المستدير أو ليس؟ والثاني لأنه قد يتفق أن ينصر الضدين في النقيضين معا بقياسين في وقتين كل واحد منهما جدلي على ما ستعرفه. وأما أنه ليس بمحدود النظر في المبادئ فذلك من جهتين: إحديهما أنه لا ما في المبادئ الذاتية بالشيء بل كيف اتفق والثانية أنه بأخذ المبادئ الأولية والصادقة المشهورة التي ليست بصادقة معا وما يتسلمه من المخاطب، وقد يجعل لكل واحد من المقابلتين مبدأ لقياسه؛ وذلك في وقت، وهذا في وقت على ما عرف. وأما البرهان فإنه محدود الموضوع محدود المسألة محدود المبادئ. والمسألة هي التي يبينها بالبرهان وينصرها ويكاد أن يكون الحق هو أنه ليس في العلوم مسألة عن طريقي النقيض ولكن قد يقال مسألة علمية على وجهين: أحدهما أنه ينفع في التعليم والتعلم وهو أحد طرفي النقيض على التعيين أنه هو الحق وأنه لا يتعداه المجيب والسائل؛ وإنما قيل للتقرير. والثاني في المخاطبات الامتحانية التي تكون في العلوم، والمسائل الامتحانية علمية من جهة أن مباديها مناسبة وليست علمية من جهة أن الغرض فيها ليس إثبات علم. فلذلك إذا حققت لم تكن علمية برهانية مطلقة بل المسائل العلمية المطلقة محدودة؛ وليس كل سؤال هندسيّاً ولا حسابيًا ولا طبيا مثلا بل المسألة الهندسية إما عن مقدمة بانت بالطرق الهندسية ويراد أن بيانها غيرها فيكون عن مبدأ خاص بالمطلوب. وإما عن مبدأ عام للمسائل الهندسية تبين بها المطالب الهندسية ولا يتبين هو في الهندسية. وكذلك الحال في المسائل النظرية إما أن تكون منظرية خاصة يتبين فيها، وإما أن تكون هندسية وهي مبادئ للعلم المناظر: فإن مبادئه من الهندسة فالمسائل الهندسية هي المسائل بالنسبة إلى الهندسة لما أنها منها ونافعة فيها. وإنها هي المبادئ أيضًا بالنسبة إلى المناظر؛ والمعنى بالمسألة هنا لا المطلوب بل توخذ مقدّمة. فمن ذلك مبدأ لا يتم بيانه في ذلك العلم، ومن ذلك ما من شأنه أن يبين في ذلك العلم، ويبين به غيره أيضًا. فالمبادئ مسائل هندسية أي مسائل نافعة في الهندسة، والمطالب مسائل هندسية أي مسائل من الهندسة؛ وليس كونها مسائل هندسية بنوع واحد ولا يمكن أن يكون المبدأ مسألة من العلم الذي هو مبدؤه؛ إذ المسألة في علم ما جزء من ذلك. ولا يمكن لواحد من أصحاب العلوم أن يبين مبادئه من جهة ما هو صاحب علمه، فالمهندس من جهة ما هو مهندس لا يمكنه إثبات مبادئه، والمناظري من جهة ما هو مناظري كذلك؛ فإن تكلف المناظري ذلك في مبادئه صار هندسيّا. ومن هذه الجهة ما يبين مبادئه؛ وإن تكلف المهندس صار فيلوسوفًا ومن هذه الجهة ما يبين مبادئه. ومبادئ جميع العلوم يتبين في علم ما بعد الطبيعة. ولما لم يمكنه أن يبين مبادئه فلا كلام له إذن مع من يناقض مبادئه، ولا مع من لا يبني على مبادئه. ولا أيضًا يلزمه أن يجب إن كان مهندسا إلا غير مسألة هندسية. وعليه أي على صاحب العلم أن يعرف عن ماذا يبحث، وعلى السائل عن ماذا يسأل. ثم المسألة التي ليست علمية إما أن تكون خارجة عن ذلك العلم، وإما أن تكون داخلة بوجه مثلا لو أن سائلا سأل في الهندسة عن الأضداد هل علمها واحد فقد سأل مسألة من الفلسفة الأولى، أو عن عددين هل مجتمع منهما مكعب كما يجتمع منهما مربَّع فقد سأل مسألة حسابية؛ وكذلك في الجهل. والفرق بين الخطأ والجهل فإن كل خطأ جهل، وليس كل جهل خطأ.
Regarding questions, that is from two aspects: one is that it does not confine itself to essential questions concerning the subject at the time, but also to extraneous ones, such as whether a straight line, when another line stands upon it, is such and such, but is it better than the circular line or not? And is it opposite to the circular line or not? The second is that it may happen that he supports both opposites in two contradictories together with two syllogisms at two times, each one being dialectical, as you will know. As for it not being limited in its consideration of the principles, that is from two aspects: one is that it does not [require] that the principles be essential to the thing, but rather whatever happens; the second is that it takes primary and true famous premises which are not true together, and what he receives from the interlocutor, and he may make for each of the two opposites a principle for his syllogism; that at one time, and this at another time, as has been known. As for demonstration, it is limited in subject, limited in question, limited in principles. The question is what he demonstrates and supports by demonstration, and it is almost the truth that there is no question in the sciences concerning the two ways of the contradictory, but it may be said a scientific question in two ways: one is that it is useful in teaching and learning, and it is one of the two sides of the contradictory specifically that it is the truth, and the respondent and questioner do not exceed it; it is only said for confirmation. The second is in examination dialogues that occur in the sciences, and examination questions are scientific in that their principles are appropriate, and they are not scientific in that the purpose in them is not to establish knowledge. Therefore if you investigate, they are not absolutely demonstrative scientific questions, but absolute scientific questions are limited. And not every question is geometrical, nor arithmetical, nor medical, for example, but the geometrical question is either about a premise that has become evident by geometrical methods and it is intended that its demonstration is other than it, so it is about a principle specific to the desired conclusion; or about a general principle for geometrical questions by which geometrical problems are demonstrated, and it is not demonstrated in geometry. The same is the case for theoretical questions: either they are special theoretical questions demonstrated therein, or they are geometrical and are principles for the science of optics: for its principles come from geometry, so the geometrical questions are questions relative to geometry because they are from it and useful in it. And they are also principles relative to optics; and the meaning of 'question' here is not the desired conclusion but a premise taken. So some of that is a principle whose demonstration is not completed in that science, and some of it is such that it is demonstrated in that science, and by it other things are also demonstrated. Thus the principles are geometrical questions, i.e., questions useful in geometry, and the desired conclusions are geometrical questions, i.e., questions from geometry. And their being geometrical questions is not of one kind, and it is not possible for a principle to be a question of the science of which it is a principle, since the question in a certain science is a part of that. And it is not possible for any one of the practitioners of the sciences to demonstrate his principles insofar as he is a practitioner of his science. So the geometer, insofar as he is a geometer, cannot prove his principles, and the optician, insofar as he is an optician, similarly. For if the optician undertakes that regarding his principles, he becomes a geometer; from this aspect he demonstrates his principles. And if the geometer undertakes it, he becomes a philosopher; from this aspect he demonstrates his principles. And the principles of all sciences are demonstrated in the science of metaphysics. Since he cannot demonstrate his principles, he has no argument therefore with one who contradicts his principles, nor with one who does not build on his principles. Nor is it incumbent upon him to answer, if he is a geometer, except in a geometrical question. And it is upon the practitioner of the science to know what he is investigating, and upon the questioner to know what he is asking. Then, the question that is not scientific is either outside that science, or is inside in some way. For example, if a questioner asks in geometry about opposites, whether their knowledge is one, he has asked a question from first philosophy; or about two numbers, whether their sum is a cube as their sum is a square, he has asked an arithmetical question; and likewise with ignorance. And the difference between error and ignorance is that every error is ignorance, but not every ignorance is error.
Source pages p. 439

Commentary
This passage compares dialectic (jadal) and demonstration (burhan) in terms of their boundaries concerning subject matter (mawdu'), questions (masa'il), and principles (mabadi'). The author begins by stating that dialectic is not limited in its subject matter: it deals not only with essential attributes but also with extraneous ones, such as whether a straight line is better than a circular line. It also supports both sides of a contradiction at different times using dialectical syllogisms. Regarding principles, dialectic does not require them to be essential to the subject; it uses common premises that may not be true together, and it takes premises from the interlocutor, even using opposite premises in different arguments. In contrast, demonstration is strictly limited: its subject, questions, and principles are all bounded. The author then discusses the concept of a 'scientific question' (mas'ala 'ilmiyya). He notes that strictly, in the sciences, a question is not about both sides of a contradiction; rather, it is one side determined to be true, used in teaching. There is also a second type of scientific question used in examination dialogues, where the purpose is not to establish knowledge but to test; these are scientific only in that their principles are appropriate, but not demonstratively scientific. Absolute scientific questions are limited and belong to a particular science. The author elaborates on the hierarchy of sciences using geometry and optics as examples: geometrical questions can be premises for optics, and the principles of optics come from geometry. A principle of a science cannot be proven within that same science; the geometer cannot prove the principles of geometry qua geometer, nor can the optician prove the principles of optics qua optician. To prove them, one must ascend to a higher science: the geometer becomes a philosopher (metaphysician), and the optician becomes a geometer. Ultimately, the principles of all sciences are demonstrated in metaphysics. Thus, a practitioner of a science cannot argue with someone who denies its principles; he can only answer questions within his own science. Non-scientific questions are either outside the science or inside in a merely verbal sense, as when someone asks a metaphysical question in geometry or an arithmetic question in geometry. The passage concludes with a brief distinction: every error (khata') is ignorance (jahl), but not every ignorance is error—ignorance is broader, including lack of knowledge without false belief.

Key Terms
جدل
برهان
مسألة
مبادئ
خطأ
جهل

B) Because the principles of geometry are self-evident and need no proof

D) Because the geometer is only concerned with questions, not principles

Key Takeaway
Dialectic is unbounded in subject, questions, and principles, while demonstration is strictly bounded; principles of a science cannot be proven within that science but require ascent to a higher science.

Equivocation in Dialectic and False Contradiction
Author illustrates equivocation in dialectic using 'circle' and explains its effect on contradiction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when equivocation (iltibas) in a term prevents two propositions from being genuine contradictions (munāqaḍa).

Core Idea
Equivocation breaks contradiction: if a term means two things, two statements may not truly oppose each other.

In dialectical reasoning, words are often used ambiguously. The author illustrates this with the word 'circle', which can mean a geometric figure or a poetic structure. Understanding equivocation is crucial to avoid false contradictions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ambiguous term
Locate the word 'circle' (دائرة) in the Arabic passage. Note that it is used in more than one sense.
The passage explicitly contrasts the geometer's definition with the dialectician's usage.
2
Distinguish the two meanings
The geometric circle is a shape; the poetic circle (a poem with circular structure) is not a shape. Also, a circular argument is neither a shape nor a geometric figure.
The author gives three examples: geometric, poetic, and argumentative.
3
See how the apparent contradiction dissolves
The statement 'Every circle is a shape' is true for the geometric sense, but 'The poetic circle is not a shape' refers to a different meaning. Thus, they are not contradictories because the subject term is equivocal.
Genuine contradiction requires the terms to be univocal.
4
Generalize the rule
Whenever a key term can be interpreted in multiple ways, any apparent contradiction may be false. Always check for univocality of terms before declaring a contradiction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فيه فيكون مثل هذا الالتباس في الجدل أكثر مثلاً لفظة الدائرة عند المهندس محدودة، وعند الجدلي ملتبسة، فيشكل صدق قول من يقول كل دائرة شكل. وربَّما ظن أنه ليس كل دائرة بشكل؛ فيقع الغلط إلا أن يعرف ويميز ذلك. ولا يبعد أن يفهم من لفظة الدائرة غير ما يكون عند المهندس نحو القصيدة الدائرة الأجزاء بعضها على بعض، أو الحجة الدورية مثلا : فإن الدائرة الشعرية هي الدائرة وليست بشكل. وقد يؤخذ الموضوع في قولهم كل دائرة شكل على معنى أن كل ما يسمى دائرة لا على معنى كل ما له معنى الدائرة»، وهذا من جملة ما يمنع أن يكون مناقضة. وكذلك إذا قلت الدائرة الشعرية ليست بشكل فإنه يمنع عن أن يكون مناقضة أيضًا إذا المناقضة مقدمة بنفسها، ومناقضة بالقياس إلى غيرها.
In it, such equivocation in dialectic is more [common]. For example, the word 'circle' for the geometer is defined, and for the dialectician is ambiguous. Thus the truth of the statement 'Every circle is a shape' becomes problematic. Perhaps one might think that not every circle is a shape; so error occurs unless he knows and distinguishes that. It is not far-fetched that from the word 'circle' something other than what is meant by the geometer is understood, such as the circular poem whose parts are superimposed, or the circular argument for example. For the poetic circle is a circle but not a shape. And the subject in their statement 'Every circle is a shape' may be taken in the sense of 'everything that is called a circle', not in the sense of 'everything that has the meaning of circle'. This is among the things that prevent it from being a contradiction. Similarly, if you say 'The poetic circle is not a shape', it also prevents it from being a contradiction, since contradiction is presupposed by itself, and [what appears as] contradiction in relation to something else [is not true contradiction].
Source pages p. 442

Commentary
This passage discusses the problem of equivocation in dialectical reasoning. The author uses the word 'circle' (دائرة) as an example: for a geometer, it has a precise definition, but a dialectician may use it loosely. This can lead to a mistaken belief that 'not every circle is a shape' if one confuses the geometric circle with a 'poetic circle' (a poem with circular structure) or a 'circular argument'. The author explains that the proposition 'Every circle is a shape' is true only if we take 'circle' in the geometric sense. If someone says 'The poetic circle is not a shape', this does not contradict 'Every circle is a shape' because the subject term is being used in different senses. The key point is that a genuine contradiction requires the terms to be univocal; equivocation prevents a true contradiction from occurring.

التباس
مناقضة

C) Because shapes are not predicated of poems
D) Because the geometer's definition is incorrect

Key Takeaway
Equivocation in a term prevents two statements from being genuine logical contradictions, because they are not about the same subject.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Consider the two statements: 'Every intoxicant is forbidden' and 'Nabidh is not forbidden'. Three Differences Between Dialectic and Demonstrative Science
Author enumerates three differences in analysis, composition, and progression between dialectic and demonstrative sciences.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and explain the three differences between dialectic and demonstrative science in terms of analysis, composition, and progression.

Core Idea
Dialectic lacks the clarity and certainty of demonstration because its middle terms are numerous and its procedure is unsystematic.

Islamic logicians distinguish between two types of scientific reasoning: demonstrative (burhānī) and dialectical (jadalī). Demonstrative science builds on certain definitions, while dialectic relies on widely accepted opinions. In this passage, the author enumerates three ways they differ: analysis, composition, and progression. Understanding these differences helps you evaluate the strength of arguments in Islamic disciplines.

Guided Path
1
First Difference: Analysis
In demonstrative science, analysis is easy because predicates come from definitions, so middle terms are limited and known. In dialectic, middle terms are many (essential and accidental) and include false but well-known ones, making analysis difficult. Locate this contrast in the text.
Analysis here means working backward from a conclusion to find a middle term.
2
Second Difference: Composition
Composition, the converse of analysis, is easy in demonstration because analysis was easy. In dialectic, composition is haphazard and unsystematic because analysis was difficult. The author also notes that composition can be reversed between disputations.
Composition means constructing a syllogism from premises to conclusion.
3
Third Difference: Progression
In demonstrative science, progression from one problem to another is orderly and finite. In dialectic, progression may go on to infinity or stop and begin anew, disconnected from earlier results. The author cites Euclid's geometry as an example of proper progression.
Progression refers to how a discipline advances from one proven result to another.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم الجدل والتعاليم يتخالفان غاية التخالف في التحليل بالعكس؛ وذلك لأن التعاليم توخذ محمولات مسائلها من الحدود وما يلزم من العوارض بسبب الحدود وهي العوارض التي تعرض للأشياء بذاتها، ومن جهة ما هي هي من حيث لها حدودها؛ وأكثرها منعكس. وإذا أريد أن يطلب للمطلوب قياس من طريق التحليل بالعكس يطلب من لواحق الطرفين ما هو على الشريطة المذكورة وهي لواحق محدودة معلومة فيكون سبيل التحليل فيها سهلا وكذلك سبيل التركيب الذي هو عكس سبيل التحليل فيكون التركيب فيها أيضًا سهلا : فإن عكس السهل سهل وبطريق التركيب يندرجون من مسألة إلى مسألة من غير [٢٩٩] أن يخلو / بمقدمة ذات، ويتجاوزوا عنها إلا بعد إيضاحها بالقياسات القريبة منها، ويكون التزيد فيها تزيدا محدودًا، والطريق شهودًا
والجدل مخالف في جميع هذا: [١] أما أولا ففي التحليل؛ وذلك لأن الأوساط تكون أمورًا كثيرة عرضية وذاتية، وتكون من العرضيات صادقة وكاذبة بعد أن تكون مشهورة فتتضاعف مطالب الأوساط؛ وليس إنما يصعب التحليل في المسائل الجدلية على الإطلاق بل وفي الصادقة منها لأنها قد تحصل من الكواذب إذا كانت مشهورة أو مسلمة. [٢] وأما ثانيًا ففي التركيب لأن التحليل لما صعب عكسه وهو التركيب؛ إذ التركيب فيه لا يكون على تأليف مستقيم بل يكون كيف اتفق وبأي أوساط اتفق. وربَّما عكس التركيب فجعل ما يبينه الجدل بمقدمة منتجة لتلك المقدمة بيان بها بعينها في مجادلة أخرى فيتضاعف التركيب. وربَّما وقع في بعض المقدمات التي إذا سلمت ينعقد القياس وإلا فلا بل يلزم الرجوع من التركيب إلى التحليل فيتحلل التركيب موضع التحليل وهو موضع البحث فيطلب لذلك حد أوسط مرة أخرى وهو التحليل. [٣] وأما ثالثًا ففى التزيد؛ وهذا الموضع يمكن أن يفهم على أنه يعنى به تزيد البرهاني التعليمي لا بالتوسط على ما بينا بل بإضافة حد من خارج؛ إما إلى غير النهاية، وإما أن يقف فيبتدئ برهانا على شيء منقطع عن الأول كما فعل أو قليديس حين استعمل بزوايا حول خط قائم
Then dialectic and demonstrative sciences differ greatly in analysis by converse. That is because demonstrative sciences take the predicates of their problems from definitions and the necessary accidents that follow from definitions, which are accidents that belong to things by themselves and inasmuch as they are what they are, by virtue of having definitions. Most of them are convertible. When one wants to seek a syllogism for the required [conclusion] through the method of analysis by converse, he seeks from the concomitants of the two extremes [that which satisfies] the mentioned condition, and they are limited and known concomitants. So the path of analysis is easy, and likewise the path of composition, which is the converse of analysis, so composition in them is also easy. For the converse of what is easy is easy. And by the method of composition they proceed from one problem to another without omitting any essential premise, and they do not pass beyond it until after clarifying it by proximate syllogisms. The progression in them is limited, and the path is evident.
Dialectic is contrary in all this:
First, in analysis: because the middle terms are many things, accidental and essential, and among the accidental ones they include true and false, as long as they are well-known. So the demands for middle terms multiply. And analysis is difficult not only in dialectical problems absolutely, but even in true ones because they may be derived from false [premises] if they are well-known or conceded.
Second, in composition: because when analysis is difficult, its converse, composition, is also difficult. For composition in it is not in a straightforward order but is haphazard and with any middle terms. Perhaps composition is reversed, so that what dialectic proves by a productive premise is used to prove that same premise itself in another disputation, thus multiplying composition. And sometimes a premise occurs such that if it is conceded the syllogism holds, otherwise not; rather, one must return from composition to analysis, so composition takes the place of analysis, which is the place of investigation, and then another middle term is sought, which is analysis.
Third, in progression: And this point can be understood as meaning the progression of demonstrative instruction, not by intermediation as we explained, but by adding an external term; either to infinity, or it stops and begins a demonstration on something disconnected from the first, as Euclid did when he used angles around a straight line.
Source pages p. 442

Commentary
This passage contrasts dialectic (جدل) with demonstrative science (تعاليم برهانية) in three aspects: analysis (تحليل), composition (تركيب), and progression (تزيد). In demonstrative science, predicates come from definitions, so middle terms are limited and known; analysis and composition are easy and systematic. Progression is finite and orderly. In dialectic, middle terms are numerous (accidental and essential, true and false), making analysis hard, composition haphazard, and progression potentially infinite or disconnected. The author uses Euclid's geometry as an example of proper demonstrative progression, where each step is grounded in previous ones. The three-fold breakdown shows that dialectic lacks the clarity and certainty of demonstration.

Key Terms
تحليل
تركيب
تزيد
وسط

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Dialectic uses only probable premises

C) Dialectical syllogisms are always invalid
D) Analysis in dialectic requires converting the conclusion

Key Takeaway
The three differences—analysis, composition, progression—reveal that demonstrative science is systematic and certain, while dialectic is complex and variable.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Names of Dialectical Syllogism: Teaching, Dialogue, Disputation, etc.
Author lists names for syllogistic addresses and highlights teaching's reliance on truth.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to list the eight names of syllogistic addresses and explain why teaching (ta'lim) is distinguished as benefiting only from truth.

Core Idea
Dialectical syllogism has many names, each reflecting a different purpose in discourse.

In this chapter, the author explores why this type of syllogism is called 'dialectical' (jadal) and which names best fit it. He then lists eight distinct terms used for syllogistic addresses, from teaching to recitation. Pay close attention to the specific role of truth in teaching—it stands apart from the others.

Guided Path
1
Identify the chapter's question
Read the first sentence: the author asks why this type of syllogism is called dialectical and which names are most appropriate.
2
Locate the list of names
Find the eight names in the Arabic: التعليم، المحاورات، المناظرة، المعاندة، الاختبار، المجادلة، الخطابة، الإنشاد. Notice they are all nouns describing modes of address.
The Arabic uses 'المحاورات' (plural) but it is a single name.
3
Distinguish teaching from the others
The author explicitly states: 'التعليم لا ينفع فيه إلا الحق' — teaching is only benefited by truth. Contrast this with other names, which may involve falsehood or persuasion.
This is the only name for which a specific condition is given.
4
Consider the incompleteness
The passage cuts off during the discussion of dialogue (المحاورة). Recognise that the full classification may continue elsewhere.
Incomplete text is a reminder to consult the original source for the full argument.

This classification shows that dialectical reasoning encompasses various discursive practices, each with its own aim.

Classification of Argumentative Discourses by Purpose
Author enumerates and defines types of argumentative discourse.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the seven types of argumentative discourse based on their purposes and identify their defining characteristics as presented in the passage.

Core Idea
Not all arguments aim at truth—the purpose determines the classification.

In this passage, the author classifies the various types of argumentative discourse, ranging from pure truth-seeking (education) to deceptive sophistry. Understanding each type by its purpose is essential for evaluating arguments in Islamic sciences, where distinguishing beneficial debate from harmful obstinacy is critical.

Guided Path
1
Identify truth-seeking discourses
Look for types where the ultimate aim is acquiring knowledge (العلم). These include education (التعليم), dialogue (المحاورة), and debate (المناظرة). Note how each involves a collaborative or adversarial but truth-oriented process.
Education: only truth benefits; Dialogue: participatory, each half teacher half learner; Debate: examines opposing views to find truth.
2
Distinguish non-truth-seeking types
Find discourses where the purpose is not truth but showing incapacity (المعاندة), testing ability (الاختبار), persuasion in particulars (الخطابة), or mere recitation (الإنشاد). Note that obstinacy and examination can overlap with sophistry depending on intent.
Obstinacy aims to show opponent's deficiency; examination tests syllogistic skill; rhetoric persuades in particular matters; poetry does not aim at belief.
3
Recognize perversions of dialectic
The author explains that when obstinacy involves deception, it becomes sophistry (سوفسطائي) or quarrelsomeness (مشاغبي). The same syllogism can be used for truth, obstruction, or examination based on the speaker's intent.
Intent is key: a syllogism used for incapacitating is obstinate; for testing, examinational; for truth, it remains dialectical.
4
Understand the role of rhetoric and poetry
Rhetoric (الخطابة) is defined as persuasion in particular matters (جزئيات), while poetic recitation (الإنشاد) is stated to not aim at belief or assent at all. The passage cuts off before fully defining rhetoric, but its purpose is clearly distinct.
In Islamic tradition, rhetoric is often used in preaching and political address, while poetry is artistic.

فصل: في السبب الذي يسمي هذا الضرب من المقاييس جدلية فيجب أن ننظر الآن في أن هذه الصناعة أي الأسماء أحق بها وأنها مقصورة على المحاورة والمخاطبة لكن الأسماء المستعملة في المخاطبات القياسية هذه التعليم والمحاورات والمناظرة والمعاندة والاختبار والمجادلة والخطابة والإنشاد، ثم التعليم لا ينفع فيه إلا الحق. وأما المحاورة فليس القصد فيه إلى ما في التعليم ولكن المحاورة بالمشاركة فإن الإنسان الواحد إذا حاول أن يكون معلما لنفسه، ومتعلما من نفسه عثر عليه ذلك فيفتقر إلى آخر يشاركه في النظر، فيضم ما يجد منه ذلك إلى ما يجد بينه هو فيصير كل واحد منهما نصف معلم، وكل واحد منهما تمام متعلم؛ والغرض فيه العلم. وأما المناظرة وهي مأخوذة من النظر فالغرض منها المناقشة عن الرأيين المتقابلين لتعيين منهما الحق مساعدة الثاني عليه؛ والغرض فيه ليس إلا حصول العلم. وأما المعاندة فهي مخاطبة يحاول المخاطب بها إظهار نقص من يدعي الكمال على أي وجه كان فيكون الغرض فيها إظهار عجز لا إعطاء فائدة. ثم المعاند إذا قصد التمويه والتلبيس فذلك لا يكون مخصوصا باسم المعاند بل هو إما سوفسطائي بتشبيه بالفيلسوف، وإما المشاغبي لتشبيه بالجدلي بل إنما يكون العاند معاندًا إذا كان ظاهر قصده بعجز الآخر. ولا يقال لفظ العناد بحسب اللغة موضوع للدلالة على الخروج عن الحق والعدول عن الواجب بفضل القوة؛ لأنه وإن كان كذلك فلا يليق أن يُجعل اسما لهذه المخاطبة. وأما الاختبار فليس الغرض فيه إقناع في رأي البتة بل يعرف لمبلغ المخاطب في القوة على استبانة القياسات فكان القياس المعاند والممتحن والمغالط واحدا في الموضوع لكنه إذا استعمل على أنه إنما يراد به إثبات الحق أو الإقناع بالحق يسمّى سوفسطائياً أو مشاغبياً وإذا استعمل والغرض فيه تعجيز الخصم كان القياسُ قياس عناد. وإذا استعمل والغرض فيه استكشاف حال المخاطب المجهول أمره من غير أن يراد تضليله كان قياس امتحان. وأما الإنشاد فهو بعيد أن يكون الغرض فيه إيقاع اعتقاد وتصديق البتة. وأما الخطابة فالخطيب هو المقتدر على إقناع الناس في الأمور الجزئية فلم تبق لنا اسم أولى بهذا
Chapter: On the reason why this type of syllogism is called dialectical. We must now consider which names are most appropriate for this art, and that it is confined to conversation and address, but the names used in syllogistic discourses are: education, dialogue, debate, obstinacy, examination, disputation, rhetoric, and poetic recitation. Then education: nothing but truth is beneficial in it. As for dialogue, its aim is not that of education; rather, dialogue is participatory. For when one person tries to be a teacher to himself and a learner from himself, he stumbles upon that and needs another to share in the investigation. He then combines what he finds from that to what he finds with himself, so each becomes half a teacher and each a complete learner; its purpose is knowledge. As for debate, which is derived from 'looking', its purpose is to examine the two opposing views to determine which one is true, assisting the second upon it; its purpose is only to obtain knowledge. As for obstinacy, it is an address in which the speaker tries to show the deficiency of one who claims perfection in any way, so its purpose is to show incapacity, not to give benefit. Then if the obstinate person aims at deception and obfuscation, that is not specific to the name 'obstinate'; rather, he is either a sophist by resemblance to the philosopher, or a quarrelsome person by resemblance to the dialectician. Rather, the obstinate person is truly obstinate only if his apparent aim is to show the other's incapacity. And it is not said that the word 'obstinacy' linguistically indicates deviation from truth and turning away from obligation due to excessive force; because even if that is so, it is not fitting to make it the name for this address. As for examination, its purpose is not at all to persuade in an opinion, but rather to know the extent of the addressee's ability to discern syllogisms. So the syllogism of obstinacy, examination, and fallacy are one in subject, but if it is used to establish truth or persuade with truth, it is called sophistical or quarrelsome; if used to incapacitate the opponent, it is a syllogism of obstinacy; if used to discover the state of the addressee whose affair is unknown without intending to mislead him, it is a syllogism of examination. As for poetic recitation, it is far from having the purpose of producing belief and assent at all. As for rhetoric, the orator is one who has power to persuade people in particular matters; so no name remains more fitting for this...
Source pages p. 507

Commentary
This passage is a classification of different types of argumentative discourse. The author begins by framing the discussion: we need to see which name is most appropriate for the art of dialectic, noting that it is confined to conversation (محاورة) and address (مخاطبة). He then lists the names used: education (تعليم), dialogue (محاورة), debate (مناظرة), obstinacy (معاندة), examination (اختبار), disputation (مجادلة), rhetoric (خطابة), and poetic recitation (إنشاد). Each is defined by its purpose.

1. Education: only truth is beneficial; it aims solely at imparting truth.
2. Dialogue: it is participatory; the teacher and learner collaborate, with each contributing partly, so that each becomes both teacher and learner. The purpose is knowledge.
3. Debate: derived from 'looking' (نظر), it examines two opposing views to determine which is true, with one helping the other. Its purpose is also knowledge.
4. Obstinacy: its goal is to show the opponent's deficiency, not to give benefit. If deception is involved, it becomes sophistry (سوفسطائي) or quarrelsomeness (مشاغبي). Genuine obstinacy aims to reveal incapacity. The author notes that linguistically 'obstinacy' implies deviation, but it is still used for this type.
5. Examination: aims to test the opponent's ability to discern syllogisms, not to persuade. The same syllogism can be obstructionist, examinational, or fallacious depending on intent: if used to establish truth, it's sophistical; if to incapacitate, it's obstinate; if to discover the opponent's state, it's examinational.
6. Poetic recitation: does not aim at producing belief or assent.
7. Rhetoric: the orator persuades in particular matters; the text cuts off here.

Throughout, the author distinguishes these types by their ends, showing that the same syllogistical form may serve different purposes.

المناظرة
المعاندة
الاختبار
الخطابة
الإنشاد
السوفسطائي
المشاغبي

A) Dialogue is solitary, debate requires two parties.
B) In dialogue, both parties collaborate as half-teacher half-learner; in debate, they examine opposing views to determine truth.
C) Dialogue aims at persuasion, debate aims at truth.

Key Takeaway
The classification of argumentative discourse is based on the intended purpose, not the form of the syllogism, and ranges from pure truth-seeking to deceptive sophistry.

Definition of Jadal: Craft of Compelling with Accepted Premises
Author defines jadal as a craft using accepted premises to produce probable conclusions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define jadal by identifying its genus (malaka) and differentia (using musallamāt to produce ẓannī conclusions for compelling an opponent).

Core Idea
Jadal is a skill using conceded premises, not a mere quarrel.

This lesson unpacks the definition of jadal (dialectic) as presented by the author. You will learn how jadal is an art (ṣināʿa) that enables its possessor to construct proofs from premises accepted by the opponent, producing probable knowledge. This is essential for distinguishing dialectic from demonstration and for understanding its role in Islamic scholarly debate.

Guided Path
1
Locate the genus of jadal
Find the phrase that places jadal in a broader category. The author calls it a 'ṣināʿa' (craft) and a 'malaka' (habitual disposition). This is its genus.
In definitional analysis, the genus places the thing in a family.
2
Extract the differentia
Identify what distinguishes jadal from other crafts. The differentia is: 'prepared for compelling the opponent in a praiseworthy and acceptable way to the public' and 'composing a proof from accepted premises (musallamāt) to produce a probable conclusion'.
The differentia includes purpose (ilzām al-khaṣm) and material (muqaddimāt musallamāt).
3
Verify the definition's completeness
Check if this definition covers all instances of jadal and excludes non-instances. Does it include the Quranic command to argue in the best way? Yes. Does it exclude pure sophistry? Yes, because jadal uses accepted premises and is praiseworthy.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اسم الجدل حتى تكون الصناعة المعدة لإلزام الخصم بطريق محمود مقبول عند الجمهور جدلا فيعرف إذن أنه ملكة صناعية يتمكن صاحبها بها من تركيب الحجة من المقدمات والمسلمات لإنتاج نتيجة ظنية كما يقال إن كان الإحسان إلى الأصدقاء جميلًا فالإساءة إلى الأعداء كذلك لكن الإحسان إلى الأصدقاء جميل فالإساءة إلى الأعداء كذلك فالقياس الجدلي نافع في سائر العلوم كما مرَّ، ومتعددة تعدد المسائل خلقية كانت أو طبيعية أو منطقية، فيكون مؤثرًا في تحصيل العقائد النافعة تارةً والدافعة للشبهات أخرى. ثم إنه حيث هو هو لا يكون من المحظورات فإن ما يكون على الوجه الأخص كما قال تعالى: ﴿وَجَدِلْهُم بِالَّتِي هِيَ أَحْسَنُ ﴾ [النحل: ١٢٥]، فذلك مشتمل على نفس الجدل بل إذا كان منها؛ فذلك بحسب الغرض منه؛ إذ الغرض قد يكون من المحظورات.
The name 'jadal' (dialectic) such that the craft prepared for compelling the opponent in a praiseworthy and acceptable way to the public is called dialectic. It is therefore known that it is an artistic disposition by which its possessor is able to compose a proof from premises and accepted propositions to produce a probable conclusion, as it is said: 'If doing good to friends is beautiful, then treating enemies badly is likewise; but doing good to friends is beautiful; so treating enemies badly is likewise.' So the dialectical syllogism is useful in all sciences, as has passed, and multiples according to the multiplicity of issues, whether ethical, natural, or logical. So it is effective in acquiring beneficial beliefs sometimes and repelling doubts other times. Further, it in itself is not among the forbidden things; for what is on the most specific face, as He said Exalted: 'And argue with them in the way that is best' (Quran 16:125), that includes dialectic itself; rather if it is from them, that is according to its purpose; for the purpose may be among the forbidden things.
Source pages p. 509

Commentary
This passage defines jadal (dialectic) as an art or craft (ṣināʿa) prepared for compelling an opponent in a praiseworthy and publicly acceptable manner. The author states that it is a malaka (habitual disposition) that enables its possessor to construct a proof (ḥujja) from premises that are musallamāt (accepted, conceded by the opponent). The conclusion produced is ẓanniyya (probable), not certain. A concrete example is given: a conditional syllogism. The example: 'If doing good to friends is beautiful, then treating enemies badly is likewise; but doing good to friends is beautiful; so treating enemies badly is likewise.' This is a conditional syllogism (modus ponens). The dialectical syllogism (qiyās jadalī) is useful in all sciences, as previous discussion indicated, and it applies to multiple issues—ethical, natural, or logical. It serves to acquire beneficial beliefs and repel doubts. The author then addresses the permissibility of dialectic: in itself, it is not forbidden (min al-maḥẓūrāt). Citing Quran 16:125, 'And argue with them in the way that is best,' the author notes that this verse includes the very act of dialectic. However, whether dialectic becomes forbidden depends on its purpose (al-gharaḍ). If the purpose is objectionable, the dialectic becomes forbidden. Thus, the moral evaluation of dialectic is tied to its intention, not its form.

الملكة الصناعية
المقدمات المسلمات
النتيجة الظنية
القياس الجدلي

Key Takeaway
Jadal is a praiseworthy art that uses accepted premises to produce probable conclusions, and its permissibility depends on the purpose.

Take the definition of jadal and apply it to a modern issue: Is a YouTube debate about the permissibility of interest (riba) an instance of jadal? Identify the genus (craft) and differentia (accepted premises, probable conclusion).

For example: 'If gharar (excessive uncertainty) invalidates a contract, then futures trading in shares is invalid. But gharar invalidates contracts (accepted by all scholars). Therefore, futures trading in shares is invalid.' State whether the conclusion is probable or certain.

The Dialectical Syllogism and Its Permissibility
Author gives a conditional syllogism example and rules on the permissibility of dialectic.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the structure of a conditional syllogism used in jadal and evaluate its moral status based on the purpose of the argument.

Core Idea
Dialectical syllogisms are tools; their moral value depends on the user's intention.

This lesson focuses on the example of a conditional syllogism provided in the passage and the author's ruling on the permissibility of dialectic. You will analyze the syllogism's form and understand that jadal is not forbidden in itself but becomes so if the purpose is evil. This is crucial for using logical tools responsibly in Islamic discourse.

Guided Path
1
Identify the conditional syllogism
Find the sentence that begins with 'as it is said: If doing good to friends is beautiful...' This is a conditional syllogism of the form: If A then B; A, therefore B.
This is a conditional (istithnā'ī) syllogism, specifically modus ponens.
2
Extract the premises and conclusion
The first premise is 'If doing good to friends is beautiful, then treating enemies badly is likewise.' The second premise is 'But doing good to friends is beautiful.' The conclusion is 'So treating enemies badly is likewise.' The premises are accepted (musallamāt) based on common opinion.
The conclusion is probable (ẓannī) because the premises are not certain.
3
Analyze the moral ruling
The author states that jadal in itself is not from the forbidden things, citing Quran 16:125. He then qualifies: its permissibility depends on the purpose (al-gharaḍ). If the purpose is forbidden, then that particular instance of jadal becomes forbidden.
This is a conditional permissibility ruling: jadal is permissible unless the purpose makes it prohibited.

Cultivating the Dialectical Art: Definition and Acquisition
Author defines the dialectical craft and its acquisition

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define the dialectical craft (ṣināʿa) as a psychological disposition and identify its two sources of acquisition from this passage.

Core Idea
Dialectic is a craft aiming at persuasion, acquired by nature or practice.

This passage from al-Kabīr introduces the art of jadal (dialectic). The author defines what a craft is—a psychological disposition—and clarifies the purpose of dialectic: persuasion. He also explains that such a skill can be either innate or developed through practice. Understanding this foundation is crucial before diving into the rules of dialectical exchange.

Guided Path
1
Identify the purpose of jadal
Find the author's statement of the goal: to acquire a craft that lets you produce proofs from penetrating premises and maintain consistency when responding to objections.
The phrase 'mā yūḍaʿu maṭlūban' indicates the thesis to be defended.
2
Define the craft (ṣināʿa)
Look for the definition: 'a psychological disposition from which its possessor performs voluntary actions by means of it without deliberation.' Note the key components: malaka nafsāniyya, voluntary actions, no need for reflection.
All sciences are considered crafts in this framework.
3
Recognize the two sources of acquisition
The passage mentions two ways: clear innate preparedness (istiʿdād jalī) and practice (mumārasa) with particulars. Consider how these apply to dialectical skill.
Some crafts require great effort; jadal may be acquired with relative ease due to innate talent or diligence.
4
Confirm the specific aim of this craft
The passage concludes: 'What is aimed at in this craft is persuasion (al-iqnāʿ).' Contrast with demonstration (burhān), which aims at certainty.
This distinguishes jadal from other logical arts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل: في بيان ما يتعلق بالسائل والمجيب ثمّ الغرض في هذا الفن هو تحصيل صناعة يمكننا بها أن نأتي بالحجة على كل ما يوضع مطلوبًا من مقدمات ذائغة، وإذا أجبنا عما يكون واردا على الحجة لم يوجد منا ما يناقض وضعنا . والصناعة ملكة نفسانية تصدر من صاحبها الأفعال الاختيارية بواسطتها من غير روِيَّة؛ والعلوم كلها صناعة. وهذه الصناعة أعني الجدلية قد يعين على حصولها الاستعداد الجلي في بعض الناس، وقد يعين على حصولها الممارسة واستعمال الجزئيات والصنائع مختلفة، منها ما لا يحصل إلا بكلفة ومشقة مدة مديدة. ومنها ما يحصل بدون ذلك، وفي كل واحدة منها غرض غير الغرض في الغير ما يغرض في هذه الصناعة هو الإقناع
Section: On explaining matters related to the questioner and the answerer. Then the purpose of this art is to acquire a craft by which we can bring a proof for every matter placed as a demand from penetrating premises, and when we answer what is presented against the proof, we do not find from ourselves what contradicts our position. The craft (ṣināʿa) is a psychological disposition from which its possessor performs voluntary actions by means of it without deliberation; and all sciences are a craft. This craft, namely the dialectical one, may be aided in its acquisition by clear preparedness in some people, and may be aided in its acquisition by practice and use of particulars. And the crafts differ; some are not obtained except with effort and difficulty over a long period; and some are obtained without that. In each one of them, the purpose is different from the purpose in another. What is aimed at in this craft is persuasion (al-iqnāʿ).
Source pages p. 509

Commentary
This section opens with a heading (faṣl) concerning the questioner (al-sāʾil) and the answerer (al-mujīb) in dialectic. The author then states the purpose (al-gharaḍ) of this art: to acquire a craft (ṣināʿa) that enables one to produce a proof (ḥujja) for any demanded proposition from 'penetrating premises' (muqaddimāt dhāʾiġa). Additionally, when an objection is raised against the proof, the practitioner should not find themselves contradicting their own position; i.e., they must maintain consistency. The craft is defined as a psychological disposition (malaka nafsāniyya) from which voluntary actions emanate without need for reflection (rawiyya). All sciences (ʿulūm) are considered crafts. The dialectical craft may be acquired through innate preparedness (istiʿdād jalī) in some people, or through practice (mumārasa) and application to particular cases. Crafts differ: some require great effort and a long time, others do not. Each craft has a distinct purpose; the purpose of this craft (dialectic) is persuasion (al-iqnāʿ). This passage emphasizes that jadal is a skill that can be developed and has a clear objective—persuasion based on accepted premises.

Definition of Jadal as a Dialectical Art
Defines dialectic as an art for obligating opponents with accepted premises to produce probable conclusions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define jadal as a craft-like disposition (malaka sina'iyya) and explain its purpose in producing probable conclusions from accepted premises.

Core Idea
Jadal is a disciplined craft, not mere argumentation: it obligates opponents using accepted premises.

In this passage, the author defines dialectic (jadal) as a craft-like disposition that enables its possessor to construct arguments from accepted premises to produce probable conclusions. This definition distinguishes jadal from mere disputation and establishes it as a methodologically rigorous art. The author also addresses the permissibility of jadal, citing the Quranic command to argue in the best manner. Understanding this definition is essential for engaging in Islamic dialectical theology (kalam) and juristic debate.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definiendum
The term being defined is 'al-jadal' (dialectic). Locate it in the Arabic and note that it is the subject of the definition.
2
Extract the genus and differentia
The genus is 'a craft-like disposition' (malaka sina'iyya). The differentia is 'enabling one to construct arguments from accepted premises to produce probable conclusions.' Write these down.
This is a real definition (hadd) because it specifies the category and the unique characteristic.
3
Understand the purpose
The purpose of jadal is to obligate the opponent in a praiseworthy way acceptable to the public, using accepted premises. This shows that jadal is not about force but about rational persuasion.
4
Note the permissibility condition
The author clarifies that jadal itself is not prohibited; its permissibility depends on the purpose. The Quranic verse (16:125) supports praiseworthy argumentation.
This distinction is important for avoiding the misconception that all debate is forbidden.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اسم الجدل حتى تكون الصناعة المعدة لإلزام الخصم بطريق محمود مقبول عند الجمهور جدلا فيعرف إذن أنه ملكة صناعية يتمكن صاحبها بها من تركيب الحجة من المقدمات والمسلمات لإنتاج نتيجة ظنية كما يقال إن كان الإحسان إلى الأصدقاء جميلًا فالإساءة إلى الأعداء كذلك لكن الإحسان إلى الأصدقاء جميل فالإساءة إلى الأعداء كذلك فالقياس الجدلي نافع في سائر العلوم كما مرَّ، ومتعددة تعدد المسائل خلقية كانت أو طبيعية أو منطقية، فيكون مؤثرًا في تحصيل العقائد النافعة تارةً والدافعة للشبهات أخرى. ثم إنه حيث هو هو لا يكون من المحظورات فإن ما يكون على الوجه الأخص كما قال تعالى: ﴿وَجَدِلْهُم بِالَّتِي هِيَ أَحْسَنُ﴾ [النحل: ١٢٥]، فذلك مشتمل على نفس الجدل بل إذا كان منها؛ فذلك بحسب الغرض منه؛ إذ الغرض قد يكون من المحظورات. وإذا أجبنا عما يكون واردا على الحجة لم يوجد منا ما يناقض وضعنا.
The name of dialectic is that the craft prepared for obligating the opponent in a praiseworthy way acceptable to the public is called dialectic. So it is known that it is a craft-like disposition by which its possessor is able to compose an argument from premises and accepted propositions to produce a probable conclusion, as it is said: 'If kindness to friends is beautiful, then maltreatment of enemies is likewise; but kindness to friends is beautiful; so maltreatment of enemies is likewise.' Thus dialectical syllogism is useful in all sciences as has passed, and it is multiple according to the multiplicity of issues, whether ethical, natural, or logical. So it is effective in obtaining beneficial beliefs sometimes and in repelling doubts at other times. Moreover, it in itself is not among the prohibited things, for what is in the most specific manner? As Allah said: 'And argue with them in the way that is best' (Quran 16:125), for that includes dialectic itself, but if it is from them, then that depends on the purpose; for the purpose may be among the prohibited things. And if we answer what comes as an objection to the argument, nothing from us that contradicts our position will be found.
Source pages p. 509

Commentary
This passage defines dialectic (jadal) as a craft-like disposition (malaka sina'iyya) that enables its possessor to construct arguments from premises and accepted propositions (musallamat) to produce a probable conclusion (natija zanniyya). The example given is a conditional syllogism: if kindness to friends is beautiful, then hostility to enemies is also beautiful; but kindness to friends is beautiful; so hostility to enemies is beautiful. This demonstrates the dialectical syllogism (qiyas jadali), which is useful across sciences—ethical, natural, or logical—for both establishing beneficial beliefs and repelling doubts. The author then clarifies that dialectic itself is not prohibited; rather, it is commanded in the Quran (16:125). However, the purpose may render it prohibited if used wrongly. The passage concludes by noting that when we respond to an objection against our argument, we must ensure nothing contradicts our position.

The Dialectical Syllogism: Logical Structure and Example
Provides an example of a dialectical syllogism using a conditional argument.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the logical form of a dialectical syllogism as a conditional modus ponens and evaluate its validity using Islamic examples.

Core Idea
The dialectical syllogism is a conditional argument: if the premise is accepted, the conclusion follows by modus ponens.

The author provides an explicit example of a dialectical syllogism: 'If kindness to friends is beautiful, then maltreatment of enemies is likewise; but kindness to friends is beautiful; so maltreatment of enemies is likewise.' This is a conditional argument (modus ponens) using accepted ethical premises. In this lesson, we will analyze its logical structure and see how dialectical syllogisms operate across Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the conditional premise
Find the 'if...then' statement: 'If kindness to friends is beautiful, then maltreatment of enemies is likewise.' This is the major premise of the syllogism.
2
Identify the minor premise
The minor premise affirms the antecedent: 'But kindness to friends is beautiful.' It states that the condition is true.
3
Derive the conclusion
The conclusion affirms the consequent: 'So maltreatment of enemies is likewise.' This follows logically if the premises are accepted.
4
Test validity
This is modus ponens, a valid form. The argument is formally valid, but its persuasiveness depends on whether the premises are indeed accepted by the audience.
Dialectical syllogisms yield probable conclusions, not certain ones.

The Craft of Dialectic and Its Purpose
Defines dialectical craft and its purpose

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the purpose of dialectic (ilzam and iqna') and distinguish it from other crafts.

Core Idea
Dialectic's goal is obligation, not certainty; it works on either side of a contradiction.

This lesson unpacks the definition of dialectic as a psychic disposition and its primary purpose of obligating the opponent. The author compares it to medicine to illustrate that the core goal remains unchanged even as methods vary.

Guided Path
1
Identify the art's definition
Find the phrase 'الصناعة ملكة نفسانية' – the craft is a psychic disposition from which actions emanate without deliberation.
This definition classifies dialectic as an intellectual craft.
2
Extract the purpose
Locate 'الغرض في هذه الصناعة هو الإقناع والإلزام' – persuasion (iqna') and obligation (ilzam).
Obligation is the primary goal; persuasion is a means.
3
Understand primary vs accidental
Note 'الغرض الأول في الجدل هو الإلزام' and the medical analogy: the physician's goal is health, achieved by heating or cooling. Similarly, dialectic's goal is ilzam, achieved by arguing for either side.
The goal does not change with the subject matter.
4
Distinguish argument from craft
Recognize that 'الحجة الجدلية أعم من القياس الجدلي' – the dialectical argument includes both syllogism and induction. Neither is the craft itself; they are acts of the craft.
The craft (disposition) produces arguments, but is not identical to them.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل: في بيان ما يتعلق بالسائل والمجيب. ثمّ الغرض في هذا الفن هو تحصيل صناعة يمكننا بها أن نأتي بالحجة على كل ما يوضع مطلوبًا من مقدمات ذائغة، وإذا أجبنا عما يكون واردا على الحجة لم يوجد منا ما يناقض وضعنا . والصناعة ملكة نفسانية تصدر من صاحبها الأفعال الاختيارية بواسطتها من غير روِيَّة؛ والعلوم كلها صناعة. وهذه الصناعة أعني الجدلية قد يعين على حصولها الاستعداد الجلي في بعض الناس، وقد يعين على حصولها الممارسة واستعمال الجزئيات والصنائع مختلفة، منها ما لا يحصل إلا بكلفة ومشقة مدة مديدة. ومنها ما يحصل بدون ذلك، وفي كل واحدة منها غرض غير الغرض في الغير ما يغرض في هذه الصناعة هو الإقناع والإلزام لا في واحد من طرفي النقيض بعينه بل في كل واحد منهما إذا كان من شأنه أن يُبحث عنه. ثم الغرض الأول في الجدل هو الإلزام، وأما الإلزام في هذه المسألة فهو أمر عارض؛ ولذلك لا يتغيَّر الغرض لأن قصد الإلزام لم يتغير بالتعرُّض لهذا أو لما يقابله كما أن الطبيب غرضه الأول إفادة الصحة: ثم له أن يفيدها تارةً بالتسخين وتارة بالتبريد. والحجة الجدلية أعم من القياس الجدلي فإنها قياسية واستقرائية؛ وليس واحد منهما هو صناعة الجدل بـل فعل من أفعال صناعة الجدل كما أن العلاج ليس صناعة الطب بل فعل يصدر أوله عن صناعة الطبّ. والطب ملكة يتمكن بها على العلاج كذلك صناعة الجدل ملكة يصدر عنها تأليف على النحو المذكور، أو الاستقراء على النحو المذكور؛ وبئس ما ظن من ظن أن القياس الجدلي هو فعل يصدر عن السائل لا غير كأنه لم يسمع المعلم الأوّل في أنالوطيقا يقول: إن المجيب يقيس من المشهورات، والسائل من المسلمات بل المجيب إنما هو مجيب من حيث هو حافظ وضع. والسائل إنما هو سائل من حيث هو ناقض الوضع؛ فإذا قاس قايس على رأي هو وضع يحفظه كان مجيبا، وكان السائل من يفسد عليه قياسه. ثم السائل إذا قاس على ما يقابل وضع المجيب بمقدمات يتسلمها صار مجيبا، والمجيب إذا أراد أن يفسد عليه قياسه صار سائلا . فليس كل قياس جدلي إنما هو قياس جدلي؛ لأنه سائل بل إنما يكون سائلا إذا حاول إبطال وضع ينصره ناصر. واعلم بأن السائل الجدلي إنما يسمَّى الآن سائلا من جهة أنه يقصد فيبتدئ فيسأل مخاطبًا له عن رأيه في أمره؛ فإذا أجاب بما هو رأيه كان مجيبا فكان غيره سائلا ومسألة هي ما يسأل من نفس الرأي؛ ثم بعد ذلك لا يسأل بالحقيقة (1) أنا لوطيقا الثانية لأرسطو طاليس، ص۳۷۱، ۳۷۲.
Chapter: On explaining what pertains to the questioner and respondent. Then the purpose in this art is to acquire a craft by which we can bring an argument for every demand that is placed from accepted premises, and if we answer what comes as an objection to the argument, nothing from us that contradicts our position will be found. The craft is a psychic disposition from which its possessor produces voluntary actions by means of it without deliberation; and all sciences are crafts. This craft, namely the dialectical, may be helped in its acquisition by clear preparedness in some people, and may be helped by practice and use of particulars. Crafts differ: some are acquired only with effort and difficulty over a long period, and some are acquired without that. In each one there is a purpose different from the purpose in another. What is purposed in this craft is persuasion and obligation, not in one of the two contradictory sides specifically but in each one of them if it is such that it is investigated. Then the primary purpose in dialectic is obligation; as for obligation in this particular issue, it is accidental; therefore the purpose does not change because the intention of obligation does not change by addressing this or its opposite. Just as the physician's primary purpose is to provide health, then he may provide it sometimes by heating and sometimes by cooling. The dialectical argument is more general than the dialectical syllogism, for it includes syllogistic and inductive arguments; neither one of them is the craft of dialectic but rather an act from the acts of the craft of dialectic, just as treatment is not the craft of medicine but an act that emanates from the craft of medicine. Medicine is a disposition by which one is able to treat; likewise, the craft of dialectic is a disposition from which emanates composition in the mentioned manner, or induction in the mentioned manner. How bad is what someone thought that the dialectical syllogism is an act emanating from the questioner only, as if he did not hear the First Teacher in the Analytics say: 'The respondent argues from well-known premises, and the questioner from accepted premises.' Rather, the respondent is only a respondent insofar as he is a defender of a position, and the questioner is only a questioner insofar as he is a refuter of the position. So when he argues based on an opinion that is a position he defends, he is a respondent, and the questioner is the one who corrupts his argument. Then the questioner, when he argues based on what opposes the respondent's position using premises he receives, becomes a respondent, and the respondent, when he wants to corrupt the questioner's argument, becomes a questioner. So not every dialectical syllogism is a dialectical syllogism because it is from a questioner; rather, he is only a questioner when he tries to invalidate a position supported by a supporter. And know that the dialectical questioner is now called a questioner in the sense that he intends and begins to ask his interlocutor about his opinion on his matter. So if he answers with his opinion, he is a respondent, and the other becomes a questioner. The question (mas'ala) is what is asked from the very opinion; then after that, he does not actually ask. (1) Aristotle's Second Analytics, pp. 371, 372.
Source pages p. 509

Commentary
This chapter discusses the roles of the questioner (sa'il) and respondent (mujib) in dialectic. The overall goal of the art is to acquire a craft (sina'a) that enables one to construct arguments from accepted premises and to answer objections without contradicting one's own position. The craft is a psychic disposition that produces voluntary actions effortlessly. The dialectical craft specifically aims at persuasion (iqna') and obligation (ilzam) on either side of a contradiction, not exclusively one side. The primary purpose of dialectic is obligation, but in any particular issue this obligation is accidental; the core intention remains unchanged, akin to a physician whose primary goal is health, achieved sometimes by heating, sometimes by cooling. The dialectical argument is broader than the dialectical syllogism, including also induction. Neither the syllogism nor induction is the craft itself; they are acts of the craft, just as treatment is an act of medicine. The author refutes the misconception that the dialectical syllogism is only an act of the questioner, citing Aristotle's Analytics: the respondent argues from well-known premises (mashhurat), the questioner from accepted premises (musallamat). In fact, roles are interchangeable: the respondent defends a position; the questioner attacks it. If the respondent argues using premises that defend his position, he is respondent; the questioner tries to corrupt that argument. But if the questioner constructs an argument opposing the respondent's position using premises the respondent accepts, the questioner becomes a respondent, and the original respondent becomes a questioner when trying to refute. Thus, one is a questioner only when attempting to invalidate a supported position. The dialectical questioner begins by asking the opponent about his opinion; upon receiving an answer, the answerer becomes the respondent, and the asker becomes the questioner. The question (mas'ala) is about the very opinion; thereafter, no literal questioning occurs.

Key Terms
الصناعة الجدلية
الإقناع
الإلزام

A) Persuasion (iqna')
B) Obligation (ilzam)
C) Certainty (yaqin)

Key Takeaway
The primary purpose of dialectic is obligation (ilzam) on either side of a contradiction, achieved by constructing arguments from accepted premises.

Interchangeability of Questioner and Respondent
Explains role interchangeability in dialectic

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain how the roles of questioner (sa'il) and respondent (mujib) switch based on who is defending or refuting a position.

Core Idea
Questioner and respondent are defined by function (defending vs refuting), not by who speaks first.

Many assume the questioner always asks and the respondent always answers. This lesson reveals a dynamic interchange: whoever constructs an argument to defend a position becomes the respondent, and whoever refutes becomes the questioner.

Guided Path
1
Identify the initial definitions
Find the phrases: 'المجيب إنما هو مجيب من حيث هو حافظ وضع' and 'السائل إنما هو سائل من حيث هو ناقض الوضع'. A respondent defends a position; a questioner refutes it.
Role is tied to function, not to who initiates the exchange.
2
Note the criterion for role assignment
The criterion is 'حفظ وضع' (defending a position) vs 'نقض الوضع' (refuting a position).
This implies one can switch roles if the function changes.
3
Trace the first switch
Read: 'فإذا قاس قايس على رأي هو وضع يحفظه كان مجيبا' – if someone argues to defend a position, he is a respondent.
Even if he started as questioner, he becomes respondent when defending.
4
See the reverse switch
Read: 'ثم السائل إذا قاس على ما يقابل وضع المجيب بمقدمات يتسلمها صار مجيبا' – the questioner becomes respondent when he constructs an argument opposing the respondent's position using premises he receives.
This shows the fluidity.
5
Synthesize the final clarification
The author concludes: 'فليس كل قياس جدلي إنما هو قياس جدلي لأنه سائل' – not every dialectical syllogism is from a questioner; it depends on the intention to refute a supported position.
The same person can produce a dialectical syllogism either as questioner or respondent.

فصل: في بيان ما يتعلق بالسائل والمجيب. ثمّ الغرض في هذا الفن هو تحصيل صناعة يمكننا بها أن نأتي بالحجة على كل ما يوضع مطلوبًا من مقدمات ذائغة، وإذا أجبنا عما يكون واردا على الحجة لم يوجد منا ما يناقض وضعنا . والصناعة ملكة نفسانية تصدر من صاحبها الأفعال الاختيارية بواسطتها من غير روِيَّة؛ والعلوم كلها صناعة. وهذه الصناعة أعني الجدلية قد يعين على حصولها الاستعداد الجلي في بعض الناس، وقد يعين على حصولها الممارسة واستعمال الجزئيات والصنائع مختلفة، منها ما لا يحصل إلا بكلفة ومشقة مدة مديدة. ومنها ما يحصل بدون ذلك، وفي كل واحدة منها غرض غير الغرض في الغير ما يغرض في هذه الصناعة هو الإقناع والإلزام لا في واحد من طرفي النقيض بعينه بل في كل واحد منهما إذا كان من شأنه أن يُبحث عنه. ثم الغرض الأول في الجدل هو الإلزام، وأما الإلزام في هذه المسألة فهو أمر عارض؛ ولذلك لا يتغيَّر الغرض لأن قصد الإلزام لم يتغير بالتعرُّض لهذا أو لما يقابله كما أن الطبيب غرضه الأول إفادة الصحة: ثم له أن يفيدها تارةً بالتسخين وتارة بالتبريد. والحجة الجدلية أعم من القياس الجدلي فإنها قياسية واستقرائية؛ وليس واحد منهما هو صناعة الجدل بـل فعل من أفعال صناعة الجدل كما أن العلاج ليس صناعة الطب بل فعل يصدر أوله عن صناعة الطبّ. والطب ملكة يتمكن بها على العلاج كذلك صناعة الجدل ملكة يصدر عنها تأليف على النحو المذكور، أو الاستقراء على النحو المذكور؛ وبئس ما ظن من ظن أن القياس الجدلي هو فعل يصدر عن السائل لا غير كأنه لم يسمع المعلم الأوّل في أنالوطيقا يقول: إن المجيب يقيس من المشهورات، والسائل من المسلمات بل المجيب إنما هو مجيب من حيث هو حافظ وضع. والسائل إنما هو سائل من حيث هو ناقض الوضع؛ فإذا قاس قايس على رأي هو وضع يحفظه كان مجيبا، وكان السائل من يفسد عليه قياسه. ثم السائل إذا قاس على ما يقابل وضع المجيب بمقدمات يتسلمها صار مجيبا، والمجيب إذا أراد أن يفسد عليه قياسه صار سائلا . فليس كل قياس جدلي إنما هو قياس جدلي؛ لأنه سائل بل إنما يكون سائلا إذا حاول إبطال وضع ينصره ناصر. واعلم بأن السائل الجدلي إنما يسمَّى الآن سائلا من جهة أنه يقصد فيبتدئ فيسأل مخاطبًا له عن رأيه في أمره؛ فإذا أجاب بما هو رأيه كان مجيبا فكان غيره سائلا ومسألة هي ما يسأل من نفس الرأي؛ ثم بعد ذلك لا يسأل بالحقيقة (1) أنا لوطيقا الثانية لأرسطو طاليس، ص۳۷۱، ۳۷۲.
Chapter: On explaining what pertains to the questioner and respondent. Then the purpose in this art is to acquire a craft by which we can bring an argument for every demand that is placed from accepted premises, and if we answer what comes as an objection to the argument, nothing from us that contradicts our position will be found. The craft is a psychic disposition from which its possessor produces voluntary actions by means of it without deliberation; and all sciences are crafts. This craft, namely the dialectical, may be helped in its acquisition by clear preparedness in some people, and may be helped by practice and use of particulars. Crafts differ: some are acquired only with effort and difficulty over a long period, and some are acquired without that. In each one there is a purpose different from the purpose in another. What is purposed in this craft is persuasion and obligation, not in one of the two contradictory sides specifically but in each one of them if it is such that it is investigated. Then the primary purpose in dialectic is obligation; as for obligation in this particular issue, it is accidental; therefore the purpose does not change because the intention of obligation does not change by addressing this or its opposite. Just as the physician's primary purpose is to provide health, then he may provide it sometimes by heating and sometimes by cooling. The dialectical argument is more general than the dialectical syllogism, for it includes syllogistic and inductive arguments; neither one of them is the craft of dialectic but rather an act from the acts of the craft of dialectic, just as treatment is not the craft of medicine but an act that emanates from the craft of medicine. Medicine is a disposition by which one is able to treat; likewise, the craft of dialectic is a disposition from which emanates composition in the mentioned manner, or induction in the mentioned manner. How bad is what someone thought that the dialectical syllogism is an act emanating from the questioner only, as if he did not hear the First Teacher in the Analytics say: 'The respondent argues from well-known premises, and the questioner from accepted premises.' Rather, the respondent is only a respondent insofar as he is a defender of a position, and the questioner is only a questioner insofar as he is a refuter of the position. So when he argues based on an opinion that is a position he defends, he is a respondent, and the questioner is the one who corrupts his argument. Then the questioner, when he argues based on what opposes the respondent's position using premises he receives, becomes a respondent, and the respondent, when he wants to corrupt the questioner's argument, becomes a questioner. So not every dialectical syllogism is a dialectical syllogism because it is from a questioner; rather, he is only a questioner when he tries to invalidate a position supported by a supporter. And know that the dialectical questioner is now called a questioner in the sense that he intends and begins to ask his interlocutor about his opinion on his matter. So if he answers with his opinion, he is a respondent, and the other becomes a questioner. The question (mas'ala) is what is asked from the very opinion; then after that, he does not actually ask. (1) Aristotle's Second Analytics, pp. 371, 372.
Source pages p. 509

Commentary
This chapter discusses the roles of the questioner (sa'il) and respondent (mujib) in dialectic. The overall goal of the art is to acquire a craft (sina'a) that enables one to construct arguments from accepted premises and to answer objections without contradicting one's own position. The craft is a psychic disposition that produces voluntary actions effortlessly. The dialectical craft specifically aims at persuasion (iqna') and obligation (ilzam) on either side of a contradiction, not exclusively one side. The primary purpose of dialectic is obligation, but in any particular issue this obligation is accidental; the core intention remains unchanged, akin to a physician whose primary goal is health, achieved sometimes by heating, sometimes by cooling. The dialectical argument is broader than the dialectical syllogism, including also induction. Neither the syllogism nor induction is the craft itself; they are acts of the craft, just as treatment is an act of medicine. The author refutes the misconception that the dialectical syllogism is only an act of the questioner, citing Aristotle's Analytics: the respondent argues from well-known premises (mashhurat), the questioner from accepted premises (musallamat). In fact, roles are interchangeable: the respondent defends a position; the questioner attacks it. If the respondent argues using premises that defend his position, he is respondent; the questioner tries to corrupt that argument. But if the questioner constructs an argument opposing the respondent's position using premises the respondent accepts, the questioner becomes a respondent, and the original respondent becomes a questioner when trying to refute. Thus, one is a questioner only when attempting to invalidate a supported position. The dialectical questioner begins by asking the opponent about his opinion; upon receiving an answer, the answerer becomes the respondent, and the asker becomes the questioner. The question (mas'ala) is about the very opinion; thereafter, no literal questioning occurs.

المجيب
وضع
حفظ
نقض

Key Takeaway
The roles of questioner and respondent are not fixed; they interchange based on whether one is defending or refuting a position.

The Goal of Dialectic: Ilzam
Author explains the primary goal of dialectic and its flexibility.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why ilzam is the primary goal of dialectic and why it is not tied to a specific side of a contradiction.

Core Idea
Ilzam is the heart of dialectic, regardless of position.

In the opening of this passage, the author establishes that the primary purpose (gharad) of dialectical argumentation is ilzam – forcing the opponent to accept a conclusion. He clarifies that ilzam is not inherently attached to a particular side of a contradiction; rather, it can be directed towards either side when investigated. This is crucial because it shows that the dialectician's goal remains constant whether defending or attacking a thesis, much like a physician's goal of health does not change whether he applies heat or cold.

Guided Path
1
Identify the primary goal of dialectic
Find the phrase 'الغرض الأول في الجدل هو الإلزام' and note that ilzam is the first purpose.
This is the thesis of the passage.
2
Understand why ilzam is 'accidental' (عارض)
The author says ilzam in this matter (the specific debate) is accidental, meaning it does not affect the overarching goal. The intent to force remains unchanged regardless of which side is taken.
This refutes the idea that the goal changes with the subject.
3
Analyze the physician analogy
Compare the dialectician's flexibility in achieving ilzam to a physician using heating or cooling to achieve health. The means vary but the end is constant.
This analogy illustrates that the goal is not tied to one method.
4
Recognize the flexibility of ilzam across contradictions
The author explicitly states that ilzam can apply to either side of a contradiction (نقيضين). Understand that the dialectician pursues ilzam on whatever position is under investigation.
This shows the neutral nature of the goal.
