لم يستعمل بنقيضه إلا بما لا يدخل في الحكم المذكور من جزئياته التي هي جزئيات الكلي الأول، فربَّما لم يوجد له مناقضة ووجد لكله مناقضة، وربَّما كان هو أقرب إلى الشهرة فكان الكلي أمرًا عقليا غير مشهور. ثم الشهرة والتسليم ليسا مبنيين على الحقيقة بل على حسب مناسبتهما بالأذهان، وبحسب أصناف التخيل من الإنسان. فمن المشهورات ما يكون السبب في شهرته تعلق المصلحة العامة، واتفاق أرباب الملل عليه، ومنها ما لا يكون كذلك بل يكون منها بالانقياد والإذعان فله لا محالة انسياق مختلف يحسن موقعها ولطفه، ولسوء موقعها وعنفه والأمر الشديد، الكلية بعيد عن الذهن ستشتميز عند الذهن ولا تغلبه فيكون استعمال الجزئي نافعًا محمودًا دون استعمال الكلي، فإن إيراد النقص الكلي بالنسبة إلى الجزئي. واعلم أن كثيرا من المقدمات المشهورة هي مواضع فقط لا يحسن استعمالها مقدمات قياس مثل أن طرفي النقيض لا يجتمعان؛ وإن الكلي الموجب ينعكس جزئياً موجبا. ومنها ما يحسنُ أن يكون موضوعًا ومقدمة فيكون نافعا في أنه قانون، ونافعًا في أنه جزء قياس؛ وذلك مثل ما قد مر . ومنها ما لا يحسن أن يكون قانونا على سبيل الكلية بل على سبيل التمثيل؛ وذلك لا يسمى موضعا بل الموضع ما يولد الجزئيات من حيث إنه ينزل إليها حكمها. وهذا وإن اتفق أن كان موضعا ومقدمة فهو موضع من جهة أن يستعمل على أنه قانون، ومقدمة من جهة أنه جزء قياس؛ ويُشبه أن يكون الموضع إنما يسمى موضعا لأنه جهة قصد الذهن معتبر معتدية. وكما أن الموضع المكاني عام يعم موضع أمن وموضع خوفٍ كذلك هذا الموضع يقال إن هنا موضعَ بَحْثٍ وموضع نظر وموضع انتفاع وموضع اعتبار وحفظ. وهذا الكتاب ليس كله نظرًا في المواضع بل ذلك أكثر أجزائه؛ وفيه نظر يتقدم المواضع، ونظر يتقدم عن المواضع لكن عمدة ما فيه: وأكثره هو الموضع وسائر ذلك إنما يقال في كيفيته اكتساب الموضع أو كيفيه استعماله؛ ويسمّى الكليات باسم الغالب من أجزائه.
It is not used in its opposite except for what does not enter into the mentioned judgment from its particulars that are particulars of the first universal; thus perhaps it has no opposition while its whole has opposition, and perhaps it is closer to commonness, so the universal is a mental matter not well-known. Then commonness and acceptance are not based on truth but on their suitability to minds and on the kinds of imagination of man. Among common premises, some have the reason for their commonness being the attachment of public interest and the agreement of the people of religious creeds upon it; and among them are those that are not like that but rather are due to submission and assent; so inevitably they have different inclinations: the goodness of their position and its gentleness, or the badness of its position and its violence, and the severe matter; the universal is far from the mind, [yet] it will be distinguished in the mind and does not overcome it; so using the particular is beneficial and praiseworthy without using the universal, for introducing the defect of the universal relative to the particular. Know that many common premises are only topics that are not good to use as premises of a syllogism, such as 'the two contradictories cannot coexist' and 'the affirmative universal converts to a particular affirmative'. And among them are what is good to be a subject and a premise, being beneficial as a law and beneficial as a part of a syllogism, as has passed. And among them are what is not good to be a law in a universal way but rather by way of example; that is not called a topic, but rather a topic is what generates particulars in the sense that their judgment descends to them. And if it happens to be both a topic and a premise, it is a topic in that it is used as a law, and a premise in that it is a part of a syllogism. It resembles that a topic is called a topic because it is a direction of the mind's intent, considered and transitive. Just as a spatial location is general, encompassing a place of safety and a place of fear, similarly this topic is said: here is a place of investigation, a place of consideration, a place of benefit, a place of reflection, and a place of preservation. This book is not entirely about topics, but that is most of its parts; it contains consideration that precedes topics and consideration that comes after topics, but the main part of it — most of it — is the topic; and the rest is said regarding the quality of acquiring the topic or the quality of its use; and the universals are named after the predominant of its parts.
Source pages p. 514

Commentary
This passage discusses the nature of common premises (mashhurat) in dialectical reasoning and the strategic use of particular versus universal terms. The author begins by explaining that a universal term may not have a well-known opposite, while its particular instances do; thus using the particular can be more effective when the universal is obscure. The foundation of commonness (shuhra) is not objective truth but mental suitability and types of imagination. Among common premises, some arise from public interest and religious consensus, others from submission. The universal is abstract and may not dominate the mind, so using a particular instance is often more beneficial. The author then distinguishes different uses of common premises: some are merely 'topics' (mawadi') not suited as syllogistic premises (e.g., logical axioms like non-contradiction or conversion of universal affirmative), some can serve as both a law and a premise in a syllogism, and some are only useful as examples. A 'topic' (mawdi') is defined as a generative principle from which particulars derive their judgment. If a statement functions both as a topic and a premise, it is considered a topic when used as a law and a premise when part of a syllogism. The term 'topic' is analogous to spatial location: a general direction for thought. The author concludes by noting that most of this book is about topics, with additional discussion on how to acquire and use them, and that universal names are taken from the predominant part.

Common premises are the backbone of dialectical reasoning; their acceptance depends on mental suitability and social consensus, not on objective certainty.

Strategic Use of Particulars and the Concept of Topic
Explains when to use particular terms and defines topics.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why using a particular term can be more effective than a universal in dialectical reasoning, and define what a 'topic' (mawdi') is.

Core Idea
A particular instance can be more persuasive than an abstract universal.

In dialectical arguments, the choice between universal and particular terms matters. The author argues that the universal is often too abstract and not well-known, so using a particular can be more beneficial. This lesson also introduces the concept of a 'topic' (mawdi'), a generative principle that yields particular instances.

Guided Path
1
Identify the problem with universals
The author says the universal is 'far from the mind' and may not be well-known. Find where in the passage this is stated.
2
Recognize the benefit of particulars
Using a particular instance can be 'beneficial and praiseworthy' because it is more concrete and relatable.
3
Define 'topic' (mawdi')
A topic is a principle that generates particulars by descending their judgment to them. It is like a general direction for thought.
4
Distinguish between using a statement as a law vs as a premise
Some statements can serve both functions. The author gives examples like 'the two contradictories cannot coexist', which is a topic but not good as a premise.
5
Apply to an Islamic example
In fiqh, the principle 'every harmful act is prohibited' is a topic (mawdi') that generates particular rulings (e.g., smoking is harmful, so it is prohibited). Show how it functions as both a law and a premise in a syllogism.

First Two Benefits of Dialectic: Training and Disputation
First two benefits of dialectic: training and disputation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the first two benefits of teaching dialectic: training in argumentation and its use in disputation (munāẓara), and distinguish munāẓara from contentious debate (mujādala).

Core Idea
Dialectic trains the mind and enables truth-seeking disputation, distinct from mere victory-seeking debate.

The author explains that after learning the topics for deriving arguments and how to use them, a person becomes practiced and trained. This training involves multiplying acts and improving their use through laws. The second benefit is in disputation, where one can construct syllogisms for both sides, leading to the discernment of truth.

Guided Path
1
Identify the first benefit
The first benefit is training (irtiyāḍ). It comes from having the topics for arguments ready and knowing how to use them, which increases acts of a single genus and improves their application.
The author calls this 'being practiced and trained' (mutaraḍḍin mutaḥarrijīn).
2
Understand the second benefit
The second benefit is in munāẓara (disputation). The ability to construct syllogisms for any claim allows one to also construct them for the opposite, enabling two parties to seek truth together.
Munāẓara is contrasted with mujādala, which aims at victory and imposition.
3
Distinguish munāẓara from mujādala
In munāẓara, both parties share the goal of truth; in mujādala, they seek victory. One can be a disputant (munāẓir) with oneself, but not a contentious debater (mujādil) with oneself.
This distinction is crucial: dialectic benefits truth-seeking, not eristic.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم إذا أوضحنا الحال في القياس الجدلي أنه ما هو، وفيما يخالف به غيره يجب علينا أن نجمع جوامع ما أشرنا إليه من منافع تعليم صناعة الجدل فنقول: إذا حصلنا المواضع التي منها نستنبط الحجج على كل مطلوب والآيات التي منها نتوصل إلى استنباطها، وعرفنا كيفية استعمالها كنا مرتاضين متحرّجين؛ ونعني الارتياض الممكن من تكثر أفعال جنس واحد وتحقيقه. فأما التكثير فبأن يكون مواضع استنباط الحجة لنا معلومة معدة. وأما التحسين فيما يتعلمه من القوانين في جودة استعمال تلك الأفعال فهذه منفعته. [أ] وأما منفعته في المناظرة فقد عرفت من قبل ما المناظرة وبماذا يخالف المجادلة فلأنه إذا كانت لنا قدرة على اتخاذ القياس على كل مطلوب كان لنا قدرة على اتخاذ القياس على الشيء وعلى ما / يقابله. وإذا تعارض اثنان على سبيل التصارع والتشارك معا في العمل وفي الغاية فكان كل مطلب على قياس الآخر موضع الآخر لم يلبث أن نستبين الحق لهما فيما بينهما. وكما مرَّ أن الإنسان يكون معلما ومتعلما من وجهين فيمكن أن يكون مناظرًا ومناظرا، ولا يمكن أن يكون مجادلا ومجادلا . فإن منفعة المناظرة قد يحصل للواحد مع نفسه، ومنفعة العلية والإلزام لا يحصل له مع نفسه، فلهذا ليست المجادلة مناظرة. [ب] فإذا قلنا أن صناعة الجدل ينفع في المناظرة لم يكن كأنا قلنا إنها تنفع في المجادلة؛ وهذا أيضًا قسم من منافعه . [ت] والمنفعة الثالثة هي نفس المنفعة المقصودة في الجدل؛ وذلك في أشعار الجمهور العاجزين عن الإصغاء إلى البرهان إذا يواقعه لهم على ما بيناه من قبل. [ث] والمنفعة الرابعة كالجزئي لهذه المنفعة؛ وهو منفعته في إقناع المتعلّم يعتقد مبادئ علم. ولا يلتفت إلى ما يقال إنه لما كانت المبادئ للعلوم لا مبادئ لها فلا قياس من مقدمات صادقة برهانية. فلا بد من أن يكون القياس من مقدمات مشهورة؛ فإن المبادئ
Then, when we have clarified the state of the dialectical syllogism, what it is, and how it differs from others, it is incumbent on us to collect the comprehensive points of what we have indicated of the benefits of teaching the art of dialectic. So we say: If we have obtained the topics (mawāḍi') from which we derive arguments (ḥujaj) for every requested matter, and the signs (āyāt) from which we reach their derivation, and we know the manner of using them, we become practiced and trained (mutaraḍḍin mutaḥarrijīn). By 'possible practice' we mean the multiplication of acts of a single genus and its verification. As for the multiplication, it is that the topics of deriving the argument are known and prepared for us. As for the improvement, it is in what one learns of the laws (qawānīn) concerning the quality of using those acts. So this is its benefit. [Alif] As for its benefit in disputation (munāẓara), you have known before what disputation is and how it differs from contentious debate (mujādala). Because if we have the ability to construct a syllogism for every requested matter, we have the ability to construct a syllogism for the thing and for its opposite. And if two persons oppose each other in the manner of struggle and sharing together both in action and in goal, then every requested matter is upon the syllogism of the other, the position of the other, and it does not delay that we discern the truth for both of them between them. Just as it has passed that a person can be a teacher and a learner from two aspects, so it is possible that one is a disputant and [also] a disputant, and it is not possible that one is a contentious debater and [also] a contentious debater. For the benefit of disputation may be obtained by one with himself, but the benefit of causing (al-illiyya) and imposing (al-ilzām) is not obtained by one with himself. For this reason, contentious debate is not disputation. [Ba] If we say that the art of dialectic benefits in disputation, it is not as if we said that it benefits in contentious debate; this is also a section of its benefits. [Ta] The third benefit is the very intended benefit in dialectic; that is in making the common masses (al-jumhūr) who are incapable of listening to demonstration aware (ash'ār) when it occurs to them according to what we have explained before. [Tha] The fourth benefit is like a subordinate of this benefit; it is its benefit in convincing the student to believe the principles (mabādi') of a science. And one should not pay attention to what is said that, since the principles of sciences have no principles, there is no syllogism from true demonstrative premises. It is necessary that the syllogism be from well-known premises; for the principles...
Source pages p. 517

Commentary
This passage enumerates the benefits of teaching the art of dialectic (jadal). The author begins by stating that after clarifying the nature of the dialectical syllogism, we must collect the benefits. The first benefit is that by learning the topics (mawāḍi') for deriving arguments and the procedures, one becomes practiced and trained. This training involves multiplying acts of a single genus and improving the use of laws. The second benefit (marked by [أ]) is its use in disputation (munāẓara). Munāẓara is distinct from mujādala (contentious debate) because in munāẓara both parties seek truth, while mujādala seeks victory. The author explains that if one can construct syllogisms for both sides of an issue, truth can be discerned in a dispute. The third benefit (marked by [ت]) is to address the common masses who cannot follow demonstration; dialectic can convince them using well-known premises. The fourth benefit (marked by [ث]) is a specific application of the third: convincing a student to accept the principles of a science. Since the principles of a science have no prior demonstration, one must use well-known premises (mashhūrāt) instead of demonstrative ones. This highlights the pedagogical necessity of dialectic. The passage ends with the beginning of an explanation of why well-known premises are needed for principles.

مناظرة
مجادلة
ارتياض

Key Takeaway
Dialectic's first two benefits are training in argumentation and enabling truth-seeking disputation, distinct from eristic debate.

Last Two Benefits of Dialectic: Convincing the Masses and Teaching Principles
Last two benefits of dialectic: convincing the masses and teaching principles.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the third and fourth benefits of dialectic: convincing the common masses who cannot follow demonstration, and teaching the first principles of a science using well-known premises.

Core Idea
Dialectic is essential for teaching foundational principles and addressing those who cannot grasp demonstration.

The author continues listing the benefits of dialectic. The third benefit is for the common people who cannot follow demonstrative proofs; dialectic can convince them using well-known premises. The fourth benefit is a specific application: teaching the first principles of a science to students. Since principles themselves cannot be demonstrated, teachers must use dialectical premises.

Guided Path
1
Identify the third benefit
The third benefit is for the common masses (al-jumhūr) who are incapable of listening to demonstration. Dialectic uses well-known premises (mashhūrāt) to make them aware of truths.
The term 'ash'ār' (awareness) is used, indicating a lower epistemic level than certainty.
2
Identify the fourth benefit
The fourth benefit is like a subordinate of the third: it is to convince a student to accept the principles (mabādi') of a science. Since principles are foundational and cannot be proved by demonstration, one must use dialectical syllogisms from well-known premises.
The author warns against the objection that since principles have no premises, they cannot be proved; dialectic provides a way.
3
Connect to the necessity of well-known premises
The passage begins an explanation of why well-known premises are needed for teaching principles: because true demonstrative premises are not available for the principles themselves.
The text cuts off but the idea is clear: dialectic fills a pedagogical gap.

Commentary
This passage enumerates the benefits of teaching the art of dialectic (jadal). The author begins by stating that after clarifying the nature of the dialectical syllogism, we must collect the benefits. The first benefit is that by learning the topics (mawāḍi') for deriving arguments and the procedures, one becomes practiced and trained. The author explains that if one can construct syllogisms for both sides of an issue, truth can be discerned in a dispute. The third benefit (marked by [ت]) is to address the common masses who cannot follow demonstration; dialectic can convince them using well-known premises. The passage ends with the beginning of an explanation of why well-known premises are needed for principles.

The Four Benefits of Dialectic
Author enumerates four benefits of dialectic.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and distinguish the four benefits of dialectic (jadal) and differentiate munazara from mujadala.

Core Idea
Dialectic trains the mind, enables fair disputation, persuades the masses, and teaches first principles.

After clarifying what dialectical syllogism is and how it differs from other forms, the author now gathers the key benefits of learning this art. These benefits show why dialectic is indispensable in Islamic sciences, from kalam to fiqh. The passage distinguishes genuine disputation (munazara) from contentious argument (mujadala) and explains how dialectic serves both elite and common audiences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the first benefit: practice (riyada)
The author says that by obtaining the topics from which arguments are derived and learning how to use them, one becomes well-practiced. Practice has two aspects: multiplication (tathir) of acts and improvement (tahsin) through rules.
This is about acquiring skill through repeated application and refinement.
2
Understand the second benefit: disputation (munazara)
Dialectic enables one to construct syllogisms for both a claim and its opposite. In a cooperative dispute (munazara), this helps both parties clarify the truth. The author carefully distinguishes munazara from mujadala: the former can be done alone and seeks truth, while the latter requires opposition and aims at victory.
Note the key distinction: munazara is cooperative, mujadala is competitive.
3
Recognize the third benefit: persuasion of common people
The third benefit is the main intended one: convincing common people who cannot follow demonstrative arguments. Dialectic uses accepted premises (mashhurat) to make them assent.
This shows the pedagogical and public function of dialectic.
4
Analyze the fourth benefit: teaching principles of sciences
The fourth benefit is a particular application of the third: persuading a student to accept the first principles of a science. Since principles cannot be demonstrated, dialectical syllogisms from accepted premises are necessary—unless the principles are self-evident or known by sense experience.
This justifies the use of dialectic even in sciences that rely on demonstration.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

[أ] وأما منفعته في المناظرة فقد عرفت من قبل ما المناظرة وبماذا يخالف المجادلة فلأنه إذا كانت لنا قدرة على اتخاذ القياس على كل مطلوب كان لنا قدرة على اتخاذ القياس على الشيء وعلى ما يقابله. وإذا تعارض اثنان على سبيل التصارع والتشارك معا في العمل وفي الغاية فكان كل مطلب على قياس الآخر موضع الآخر لم يلبث أن نستبين الحق لهما فيما بينهما. وكما مرَّ أن الإنسان يكون معلما ومتعلما من وجهين فيمكن أن يكون مناظرًا ومناظرا، ولا يمكن أن يكون مجادلا ومجادلا . فإن منفعة المناظرة قد يحصل للواحد مع نفسه، ومنفعة العلية والإلزام لا يحصل له مع نفسه، فلهذا ليست المجادلة مناظرة. [ب] فإذا قلنا أن صناعة الجدل ينفع في المناظرة لم يكن كأنا قلنا إنها تنفع في المجادلة؛ وهذا أيضًا قسم من منافعه . Then, when we have clarified the situation regarding the dialectical syllogism—what it is and how it differs from others—we must gather the summaries of what we have indicated regarding the benefits of teaching the art of dialectic. So we say: If we have obtained the topics from which we derive arguments for every sought matter and the signs from which we reach their derivation, and we know how to use them, we become well-practiced and trained. By 'possible practice' we mean the multiplication of acts of one kind and its perfection. As for multiplication, it is that the topics for deriving an argument for us are known and prepared. As for improvement, it is in what he learns of the rules for the quality of using those acts—this is its benefit. [A] As for its benefit in disputation, you have already known what disputation is and how it differs from contentious argument. Because if we have the ability to construct a syllogism for every sought matter, we have the ability to construct a syllogism for a thing and its opposite. And when two persons conflict in a contest and share together in action and in the goal, then each sought matter is in analogy to the other's sought matter as the other's topic; they will not delay until the truth becomes clear to both of them between them. And as it has passed that a person is a teacher and a learner from two aspects, so it is possible for him to be a disputant and a respondent, but it is not possible for him to be a contentious arguer and a contentious arguer. For the benefit of disputation may be obtained by oneself, but the benefit of opposition and imposition cannot be obtained by oneself; therefore contention is not disputation. [B] So when we say that the art of dialectic benefits disputation, it is not as if we said it benefits contention; this too is a division of its benefits. [C] The third benefit is the very intended benefit in dialectic: that is, in making the common people, who are incapable of listening to demonstration, assent to it, as we have explained before. [D] The fourth benefit is like a particular of this benefit: it is its benefit in persuading the student to believe the principles of a science. And no attention is paid to what is said that since the principles of sciences have no principles, there is no syllogism from true demonstrative premises. Therefore the syllogism must be from accepted premises. For if the principles of the sciences are self-evident, the student does not need dialectical syllogisms to clarify them, for self-evident things are clearer than dialectical ones; and if they are made clear by sense and experience, there is no need to listen to the dialectician.
Source pages p. 517

Commentary
This passage is an enumeration of the benefits of teaching the art of dialectic (jadal). The author begins by stating that after clarifying what dialectical syllogism is and how it differs from other types, it is necessary to collect the points of benefit. He then lists four benefits. The first benefit (أ) relates to practice (riyada): by acquiring the topics from which arguments are derived and learning how to use them, one becomes practiced. Practice involves both multiplication (tathir) of acts and improvement (tahsin) through learning rules. The second benefit (ب) concerns disputation (munazara). If one can construct syllogisms for any claim, one can also construct a syllogism for its opposite. In a genuine disputation where both parties seek truth, this ability helps clarify truth. The author distinguishes disputation from contentious argument (mujadala): in disputation, one can be both disputant and respondent, and its benefit can be obtained even by oneself, whereas contention involves opposition and imposition which cannot be done alone. Thus dialectic benefits disputation, not contention. The third benefit (ت) is the main intended goal: convincing the common people who cannot follow demonstrations. The fourth benefit (ث) is a particular application of the third: persuading a student to accept the principles of a science. This is necessary because the principles themselves cannot be demonstrated (as they are starting points), so they must be based on accepted premises (mashhurat). If the principles are self-evident or known by sense/experience, then dialectic is not needed.

Key Terms
الجدل (al-jadal): Dialectic: an art of reasoning that uses accepted premises (mashhurat) to convince an opponent or a common audience, distinct from demonstration and contentious argument. [Note: In later texts, jadal often refers specifically to the art of disputation in the adab al-bahth tradition.]
المناظرة (al-munazara): Disputation: a cooperative dialogue where both parties seek truth, distinguished from contentious argument (mujadala). [Note: In later dialectical works, munazara becomes the formal term for scholarly debate with rules.]
المجادلة (al-mujadala): Contentious argument: a debate aimed at victory or refutation, not necessarily truth. [Note: In Quranic usage, mujadala can be neutral or negative, but here it is contrasted with munazara.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Disputation uses demonstrative premises while contention uses accepted premises.
B) Disputation can be done alone, while contention requires an opponent.
C) The benefit of disputation can be obtained by oneself, but the benefit of contention requires two people.
D) Disputation is for common people, contention is for scholars.

Key Takeaway
Dialectic serves multiple purposes: personal training, fair debate, public persuasion, and foundational instruction, all while being distinct from contentious argument.

The Asymmetry of Proof and Refutation
Author explains asymmetry of proof and refutation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why refutation is easier than proof when multiple conditions are required, and apply this principle to a fiqh or kalam argument.

Core Idea
The harder something is to prove, the easier it is to refute — one condition defeated is enough.

The author observes a key strategic insight: things that are difficult to prove are correspondingly easier to refute. This is because proof often requires multiple conditions, while refutation only needs to invalidate one condition. This principle is crucial for the disputant to know where to focus effort in dialectic and demonstration.

Guided Path
1
Identify the principle
Find the sentence: 'كل ما يكون إثباته أعسر فيكون إبطاله أيسر' and 'كل ما يفتقر في إثباته إلى إثبات شرائط فيكفي في إبطاله إبطال شرط'. This states the asymmetry.
2
Understand the logical basis
Reflecting on why this is so: a proof that requires multiple conditions is conjunctive; if any condition fails, the whole proof fails. Thus it is easier to attack a single condition than to establish all of them.
3
Apply to a real Islamic science argument
Consider a typical qiyas argument in fiqh: to prove a ruling via analogy, you need to establish the 'illa, the asl, the hukm, and the far'. An opponent can refute the whole analogy by showing just one condition is not met, e.g., the 'illa is not present in the far'.
This shows why jadal experts often focus on challenging a single condition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل: في كيفية الانتفاع بالمواضع المعدَّة نحو هذه الأمور وكيفية اعتبارها في المقولات فهذه هي الأمور التي تجعل محمولات المسائل، وإليها يتوجه الإثبات والإبطال، فمنها ما إثباته أسهل ومنها ما إثباته أعسر ؛ وكل ما يكون إثباته أعسر فيكون إبطاله أيسر. وكل ما يفتقر في إثباته إلى إثبات شرائط فيكفي في إبطاله إبطال شرط؛ فمن المعلوم أن الإثبات شرائط ما لا يكون للإبطال فيكون الإبطال أسهل. والجدل في الحد يحتاج إلى إثباته أن يثبت أنه محمول وأن يثبت أنه مقوم ذاتي ويثبت أنه هو الاسم في المعنى أن المدلول به هو المدلول بالاسم. وأما في البرهان فلا يحتاج أن يثبت أنه موجود بل لا يمكن؛ وقد علمتَ هذا. وذلك لأن الحدود في الجدل قد يكون لا بالحقيقة بل بحسب الشهرة. وربما لم يكن ما ظن حدا بحد بل ربما لم يكن حمله (۱) بحق ولكن يحتاج في البرهان إلى إيضاح حق شرط زائد؛ وهو أنه يكون مع المساواة في العموم مساويًا في المعنى حتى يكون حدًّا تامًّا : وقد علمت كيفية هذا. ويمكن أن يكون هذا غير محتاج إليه في الجدل. وأما الجنس فيحتاج أن يثبت في الجدل أنه موجود ويثبت أنه مقوم ويثبت أنه أعم. والخاصة تحتاج أن يثبت أنها موجودة وأنها مساوية وأنها ليست في الجوهر. والعرض يحتاج أن يثبت أنه موجود وأنه غير مقوم وأنه غير منعكس لكن عادة الناس أن يقولوا إن الحد
Chapter: On how to benefit from the topics prepared for these matters and how to consider them in the categories. These are the matters that are made predicates of the questions, and to them proof and refutation are directed. Among them, some are easier to prove and some are harder. And every one that is harder to prove, its refutation is easier. And every one that requires in its proof the establishment of conditions, it is sufficient in its refutation to refute one condition. So it is known that the proof of conditions is not for the refutation, thus refutation is easier. Dialectic concerning the definition requires, for its proof, that it be established as a predicate, that it be established as an essential constituent, and that it be established that it is the name in meaning, i.e., that what is denoted by it is what is denoted by the name. As for demonstration, it does not require that it be established as existent; rather it is impossible; you already know this. That is because definitions in dialectic may be not according to reality but according to common usage. Perhaps what was thought to be a definition is not a definition, and perhaps its predication is not correct. But in demonstration, it requires the clarification of an additional condition, namely that it be equal in generality and equal in meaning so that it be a complete definition. You know how this is. And it is possible that this is not needed in dialectic. As for genus, in dialectic it requires that its existence be established, that it be established as a constituent, and that it be established as more general. As for property, it requires that its existence be established, that it be equal, and that it is not in the essence. As for accident, it requires that its existence be established, that it is not a constituent, and that it is not convertible. But the habit of people is that they say that the definition...
Source pages p. 521

Commentary
This chapter introduces how to utilize the topics (mawadi') in argumentation, distinguishing between dialectic (jadal) and demonstration (burhan). The author first notes that proof and refutation are directed toward predicates. He observes an asymmetry: things that are harder to prove are easier to refute, and if proof requires multiple conditions, it suffices to refute just one condition to invalidate the whole. He then details the requirements for establishing definitions in dialectic: the definition must be shown to be a predicate, an essential constituent, and equivalent in meaning to the name. In demonstration, the existence of the definition need not be proved; rather, additional conditions are required: the definition must be equal in generality and equal in meaning to the definiendum. Similarly, the author specifies what must be established for genus (existence, constitution, greater generality), property (existence, equality, non-essentiality), and accident (existence, non-constitution, non-convertibility) in dialectical contexts. The passage emphasizes the practical application of logical concepts in disputation.

Key Terms
المواضع
الإثبات
الإبطال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is refutation easier than proof?
A) Because definition requires equal generality
B) Because refutation only needs to invalidate one condition when proof requires multiple
C) Because dialectic is based on common opinion
D) Because demonstration does not require existence

Key Takeaway
When an argument requires multiple conditions, refuting one condition is enough to overturn the whole argument, making refutation systematically easier than proof.

Proving Definitions in Dialectic vs Demonstration
Author explains requirements for proving definitions in dialectic vs demonstration.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the requirements for proving a definition in dialectic versus demonstration, and identify which mode is being used in a given theological or juristic argument.

Core Idea
Definitions in dialectic rest on common usage, not reality; demonstration demands exact correspondence in meaning and generality.

The author explains that dialectical definitions need not be real; they may be based on common usage (shuhra). In contrast, demonstrative definitions require the definition to be equal in generality and equal in meaning to the definiendum. Understanding this distinction helps you determine what is needed to establish or refute a definition in different argumentative contexts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the requirements for dialectical definition
The author lists three things: the definition must be established as a predicate, as an essential constituent, and as meaning the same as the name. Note that existence is not a requirement.
Dialectical definitions may be based on common usage, not necessarily on reality.
2
Identify the requirements for demonstrative definition
In demonstration, an additional condition is required: the definition must be equal in generality and equal in meaning to the definiendum. Existence cannot be proved; it is assumed.
The author says 'لا يمكن' (it is impossible) to prove existence of the definition in demonstration.
3
Compare the two contexts
Note the difference: dialectic allows definitions that may not be real (بالحقيقة بل بحسب الشهرة), while demonstration demands complete correspondence. This affects what counts as a successful proof or refutation.
4
Apply to a real example
Consider the definition of 'al-'alam (the world) in kalam: 'everything other than Allah'. In dialectic, this definition is acceptable if it matches common usage; but in demonstration, one would need to show it is equal in meaning and generality to the term being defined.

Chapter: On how to benefit from the topics prepared for these matters and how to consider them in the categories. These are the matters that are made predicates of the questions, and to them proof and refutation are directed. Among them, some are easier to prove and some are harder. And every one that is harder to prove, its refutation is easier. And every one that requires in its proof the establishment of conditions, it is sufficient in its refutation to refute one condition. So it is known that the proof of conditions is not for the refutation, thus refutation is easier. As for demonstration, it does not require that it be established as existent; rather it is impossible; you already know this. That is because definitions in dialectic may be not according to reality but according to common usage. As for property, it requires that its existence be established, that it be equal, and that it is not in the essence. Commentary
This chapter introduces how to utilize the topics (mawadi') in argumentation, distinguishing between dialectic (jadal) and demonstration (burhan). The author first notes that proof and refutation are directed toward predicates. He observes an asymmetry: things that are harder to prove are easier to refute, and if proof requires multiple conditions, it suffices to refute just one condition to invalidate the whole. He then details the requirements for establishing definitions in dialectic: the definition must be shown to be a predicate, an essential constituent, and equivalent in meaning to the name. In demonstration, the existence of the definition need not be proved; rather, additional conditions are required: the definition must be equal in generality and equal in meaning to the definiendum. Similarly, the author specifies what must be established for genus (existence, constitution, greater generality), property (existence, equality, non-essentiality), and accident (existence, non-constitution, non-convertibility) in dialectical contexts. The passage emphasizes the practical application of logical concepts in disputation.

Establishing Genus, Property, and Accident in Dialectic
Author lists requirements for proving genus, property, and accident in dialectic.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to list the requirements for proving genus, property, and accident in dialectic, and apply them to a concrete example from Islamic logic.

Core Idea
In dialectic, each type of predicate has specific conditions: genus requires existence, constitution, and greater generality; property requires existence, equality, and non-essentiality; accident requires existence, non-constitution, and non-convertibility.

After discussing definitions, the author turns to other types of predicates: genus, property, and accident. For each, he specifies what must be established in a dialectical context. Understanding these conditions is essential for constructing and evaluating arguments in disputation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the requirements for genus
The author says: الجنس فيحتاج أن يثبت في الجدل أنه موجود ويثبت أنه مقوم ويثبت أنه أعم. So genus must be shown to exist, to be a constituent, and to be more general.
2
Identify the requirements for property
الخاصة تحتاج أن يثبت أنها موجودة وأنها مساوية وأنها ليست في الجوهر. So property must be shown to exist, to be equal (coextensive), and not to be in the essence (i.e., not essential).
3
Identify the requirements for accident
العرض يحتاج أن يثبت أنه موجود وأنه غير مقوم وأنه غير منعكس. So accident must be shown to exist, not to be a constituent, and not convertible.
4
Apply to a concrete example
Take the predicate 'hayawan' for 'insan'. As a genus, it must be more general, constitutive, and exist. Check: 'hayawan' applies to all animals, is a constituent of human, and exists. Now consider 'dahik' (laughing) as a property of human: it is equal (only humans laugh, in the classical view), not essential, and exists. Finally, 'abyad' (white) as an accident: it exists in some humans, is not a constituent, and is not convertible.

Conditions for Proving Definitions, Properties, and Accidents in Dialectic
Author compares difficulty of proving definitions and other predicates

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to list and apply the conditions for proving a genus, property, and accident in dialectic, and compare their difficulty relative to proving a definition.

Core Idea
Each predicate type in dialectic has distinct proof conditions; definitions require all combined.

In dialectical argument, proving a definition is not straightforward—it depends on the conditions of genus, property, and accident. This passage clarifies what must be shown for each type and how different authorities view their difficulty. Understanding these distinctions helps you evaluate the strength of definitions in Islamic scholarly debates.

Guided Path
1
Identify the conditions for genus
Find in the passage the three things that must be proved for the genus: existence, being constitutive, and being more general.
These conditions ensure the genus properly includes the species.
2
Identify the conditions for property
Find the three conditions for property: existence, coextensiveness, and not being in the substance (non-essential).
Property here is a non-essential but coextensive attribute.
3
Identify the conditions for accident
Find the three conditions for accident: existence, being non-constitutive, and being non-convertible.
Accident is non-essential and not coextensive.
4
Compare the views on difficulty
Note how the Shaykh, the common way, and the First Teacher differ on which is easiest or hardest to prove.
The common way says accident easiest; Shaykh says definition hardest; First Teacher sees all as useful.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الجنس فيحتاج أن يثبت في الجدل أنه موجود ويثبت أنه مقوم ويثبت أنه أعم. والخاصة تحتاج أن يثبت أنها موجودة وأنها مساوية وأنها ليست في الجوهر. والعرض يحتاج أن يثبت أنه موجود وأنه غير مقوم وأنه غير منعكس لكن عادة الناس أن يقولوا إن الحد يحتاج فيه إلى كل ما يحتاج إليه الجنس والخاصة والعرض. والطريق الذي أشار إليه الشيخ هو إن الحدَّ أصعب إثباتاً من الآخر) وذلك من جهة كثرة عدد ما يجب أن يراعى فيه، وعدد ما يعتبر في العرض والخاصة والجنس متقاربًا بعضه لبعض. وأما الطريق المشهور فيوجب أن يكون العرض يسهل إثباته حدا ويصعب إبطاله حدا؛ والحد يصعب إثباته حدا ويسهل إبطاله حدا: وتكون المواضع التي / بعد نحو إثبات العرض هي المواضع التي بعد نحو الإثبات المطلق. وأما المعلّم الأوّل فإنه لم يصرح بهذا المذهب بل يقول إن جميع المباحث والمواضع التي لغير الحد مواضع لما ينتفع : فإن ما يحتاج أن يقال في العرض من الإثبات قد يحتاج إليه في إثبات معنى الحد للمحدود إلى أن تبين أنه حد وإن ما يحتاج أن يقال في الخاصة من طريق ما هي مساوية فيحتاج أن يقال في الحد. وما يحتاج أن يثبت به الجنس من طريق ما هو جوهري يحتاج إلي مثله في الحد. وأيضًا فإن إثبات الجنس يحتاج في إثبات الحد فيبطل بإبطاله الحد فيكون الحد مشاركًا إياها في ضرب من الضروب؛ فجرت لفظة الجميع لهذا السبيل عليه فظن أنه جميع على سبيل عموم العدد، ولشيء أن في كل باب بحث خاص. وبالجملة فالعرض قد يقبل الأشد والأضعف؛ ويكون بعض الأشياء التي من شأنها أن يشترك أولى بعرض من بعض؛ ولا كذلك فيما هو حد أو جنس أو خاصة. وإن جملة كل ما تفيده صناعة الجدل من حيث هي هي طريق الأولى والأحرى ؛ وقد يشكل في كثير من الأمور فيجعل البحث عن ذلك جاريًا مجرى العرض فإن الذاتي من حيث ذاتيته لا شك في وجوده
As for the genus, it needs in dialectic to prove that it exists, to prove that it is constitutive, and to prove that it is more general. The property needs to prove that it exists, that it is coextensive, and that it is not in the substance. The accident needs to prove that it exists, that it is not constitutive, and that it is not convertible. But the habit of people is to say that the definition needs all that the genus, property, and accident need. The way indicated by the Shaykh is that the definition is more difficult to prove than the other, due to the multitude of the number of things that must be observed in it, while the number considered in the accident, property, and genus are close to each other. As for the common way, it requires that the accident is easy to prove as a definition and difficult to refute as a definition, and the definition is difficult to prove as a definition and easy to refute as a definition; and the places that are for the mode of proving the accident are the places that are for the mode of absolute proof. As for the First Teacher, he did not explicitly state this doctrine, but says that all investigations and places that are for non-definitions are places that benefit: for what needs to be said in the accident of proof may be needed in proving the meaning of the definition for the definiendum until it is shown that it is a definition; and what needs to be said in the property from the aspect that it is coextensive needs to be said in the definition; and what needs to be proved by the genus from the aspect that it is essential needs the like in the definition. Also, the proof of the genus is needed in the proof of the definition, so by refuting the genus the definition is refuted, so the definition shares with them in some manner; thus the word 'all' was applied to it in this way, so it was thought that it is 'all' in the sense of universal number, but there is a specific investigation in each category. In summary, the accident may admit intensity and weakness; and some things that are apt to be shared are more appropriate to an accident than others; it is not so with what is a definition, genus, or property. And the sum total of all that the art of dialectic benefits, inasmuch as it is, is the way of better and more appropriate; and it may be problematic in many matters, so the investigation concerning that is made to proceed in the course of the accident, for the essential inasmuch as it is essential is not doubted in its existence.
Source pages p. 521

Commentary
This passage continues the discussion on the ease of proving and refuting different types of predicates: genus (jins), property (khassa), accident (arad), and definition (hadd). The author first lists the conditions for establishing each in dialectic: for genus, one must prove its existence, its being constitutive, and its being more general (than the species). For property, one must prove its existence, its coextensiveness (musawiya), and that it is not in the substance (i.e., not essential). For accident, one must prove its existence, that it is not constitutive, and that it is not convertible (ghayr mun'akis). Then he notes the common belief that the definition requires all the conditions of genus, property, and accident combined. The author then contrasts two views. The 'Shaykh' (likely Ibn Sina or perhaps a reference to Aristotle in the commentary tradition) holds that the definition is hardest to prove due to the many conditions, while the conditions for the others are similar in number. The 'common way' (al-tariq al-mashhur) asserts that accidents are easiest to prove as definitions but hardest to refute, while definitions are hardest to prove but easiest to refute. The 'First Teacher' (Aristotle) did not explicitly adopt this dichotomy; rather, he held that all investigations and places pertaining to non-definitions are useful for definitions as well, because the proof of an accident may help in proving a definition, the coextensiveness of a property helps in proving a definition, and the essential nature of a genus is needed in a definition. Moreover, proving a genus is part of proving a definition, so refuting the genus refutes the definition. Thus, the term 'all' was used loosely, but in reality each category (definition, genus, property, accident) has its own specific investigation. Finally, the author notes that accidents admit degrees (shidda wa da'f) and are not as fixed as definitions or genera; dialectic, as an art, primarily uses the way of 'better and more appropriate' (tariq al-awla wa al-ahra), and when difficulties arise, investigation may proceed along the lines of accident, since the essential as such is indubitable in its existence.

Key Terms
الجنس
الخاصة
العرض
الشيخ
المعلم الأول

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The definition itself
B) The genus
C) The property
D) The accident

Key Takeaway
In dialectic, the conditions for proving definitions vary, and understanding them critically refines one's ability to construct and evaluate definitions.

?

Comparing Difficulty of Proving Definition vs. Accident
Comparison of proving definition vs. accident

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the differences between proving a definition (hadd) and proving an accidental property (arad) in dialectical argumentation.

Core Idea
Proving a definition requires more conditions than proving an accident; thus definition is harder to prove but easier to refute.

In dialectical debates, not all predicates are equally easy to establish. This passage from the logic text explains why definitions are more challenging to prove than accidents, and what that implies for argumentation. Understanding this contrast helps a debater strategize which claims to defend or attack.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two predicate types
Locate where the passage distinguishes between 'definition' (hadd), 'accident' (arad), 'special property' (khassa), and 'genus' (jins). Note that definition is singled out as more difficult to prove.
The terms are standard in Aristotelian logic.
2
Understand the 'Shaykh's' method
The passage says: 'the definition is harder to prove than the other, due to the large number of things that must be observed in it.' Compare this to accident, special property, and genus which are closer in number.
This is the core claim.
3
Contrast with the well-known method
The well-known method reverses ease: accident is easy to prove as a definition but hard to refute; definition is hard to prove but easy to refute. Notice the symmetry.
4
Recognize Aristotle's nuanced view
Aristotle did not explicitly state this, but argued that topics for accident, special property, and genus are all needed to establish a definition. This implies definition requires more conditions.
The author builds on Aristotle.
5
Note accident's degree-accepting nature
Accident admits 'more and less' (al-ashadd wa al-ad'af), whereas definition, genus, and special property do not. This affects how they are argued.
This is a key distinguishing feature.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يحتاج فيه إلى كل ما يحتاج إليه الجنس والخاصة والعرض. والطريق الذي أشار إليه الشيخ هو أن الحدَّ أصعب إثباتاً من الآخر وذلك من جهة كثرة عدد ما يجب أن يراعى فيه، وعدد ما يعتبر في العرض والخاصة والجنس متقاربًا بعضه لبعض. وأما الطريق المشهور فيوجب أن يكون العرض يسهل إثباته حدا ويصعب إبطاله حدا؛ والحد يصعب إثباته حدا ويسهل إبطاله حدا: وتكون المواضع التي بعد نحو إثبات العرض هي المواضع التي بعد نحو الإثبات المطلق. وأما المعلّم الأوّل فإنه لم يصرح بهذا المذهب بل يقول إن جميع المباحث والمواضع التي لغير الحد مواضع لما ينتفع : فإن ما يحتاج أن يقال في العرض من الإثبات قد يحتاج إليه في إثبات معنى الحد للمحدود إلى أن تبين أنه حد وإن ما يحتاج أن يقال في الخاصة من طريق ما هي مساوية فيحتاج أن يقال في الحد. وما يحتاج أن يثبت به الجنس من طريق ما هو جوهري يحتاج إلي مثله في الحد. وأيضًا فإن إثبات الجنس يحتاج في إثبات الحد فيبطل بإبطاله الحد فيكون الحد مشاركًا إياها في ضرب من الضروب؛ فجرت لفظة الجميع لهذا السبيل عليه فظن أنه جميع على سبيل عموم العدد، ولشيء أن في كل باب بحث خاص. وبالجملة فالعرض قد يقبل الأشد والأضعف؛ ويكون بعض الأشياء التي من شأنها أن يشترك أولى بعرض من بعض؛ ولا كذلك فيما هو حد أو جنس أو خاصة. وإن جملة كل ما تفيده صناعة الجدل من حيث هي هي طريق الأولى والأحرى؛ وقد يشكل في كثير من الأمور فيجعل البحث عن ذلك جاريًا مجرى العرض فإن الذاتي من حيث ذاتيته لا شك في وجوده لما هو موجود له بل كل منفعة منه بالوجود ولا يدرى بأن يجري مجرى ما يعرض. وعلى أن اعتبار الأولوية للشيء إنما هو بحسب نسبته إلى كل شيء؛ وكل نسبة عارضة تعرض من هذه الأسباب إن زيد باب البحث. غير أنه هل كذا آثر وأولى لشيء؛ ورسم الباب بباب اللا آثر فزادت مواضع في الهُو هو ومواضع في الآثَرِ. وخصوصا إذا كان النظر في الأولى والآثر أشبه نظر بما يراد به الإقناع، ومع هذا كله. فإن الخاصة أضيفت إلى الحد وجعل الجنس والفصل في باب واحد لاشتراكهما في الذاتية والتقويم فانحلت المباحث عن المواضع إلى مواضع الإثبات المطلق ومواضع العرض ومواضع الآثر ومواضع الجنس ومواضع الفصل ومواضع الخاصة ومواضع الحد ومواضع الهو هو، فإذا أعطيت هذه المواضع فقد أعطيت المواضع كلها.
It needs in it everything that genus, special property, and accident need. And the method which the Shaykh indicated is that the definition is harder to prove than the other, due to the large number of things that must be observed in it, while the number considered in accident, special property, and genus are close to each other. As for the well-known method, it requires that the accident be easy to prove as a definition and hard to refute as a definition; and the definition is hard to prove as a definition and easy to refute as a definition. And the topics that are after the mode of proving an accident are the topics that are after the mode of absolute proof. As for the First Teacher, he did not explicitly state this doctrine, but rather says that all investigations and topics that are for other than the definition are topics that are useful: for what needs to be said about the accident in terms of proof may be needed in proving the meaning of the definition for the defined until it is shown that it is a definition, and what needs to be said about the special property in the way of its equivalence is needed for the definition. And what needs to be proven by the genus in the way of its substantiality is needed similarly in the definition. Also, proving the genus is needed in proving the definition, so if it is refuted the definition is refuted, so the definition shares with them in some manner. So the word 'all' came upon this path, so one thought it is 'all' in the sense of universal number, while there is a specific investigation in each chapter. In summary, the accident may accept the more and the less; and some things that are by nature shared are more worthy of an accident than others; not so for what is a definition, genus, or special property. And the entirety of what the art of dialectic provides in itself is the way of priority and appropriateness. It may be problematic in many matters, so the investigation about that proceeds in the manner of accident, for the essential in its essentiality is not doubted in existence for what exists for it, but all benefit from it through existence, and it is not known that it proceeds in the manner of an accident. And the consideration of priority for something is only according to its relation to everything; and every relation is accidental that occurs from these causes if the chapter of investigation is added. However, whether something is more effective and more appropriate for something; and he designated the chapter as the chapter of the less effective, so topics increased in the 'is identical' and topics in the 'more effective'. Especially if the consideration of priority and appropriateness is more akin to consideration aiming at persuasion. And with all this, the special property was added to the definition, and genus and differentia were placed in one chapter due to their sharing in essentiality and constitution. So the investigations about topics dissolved into topics of absolute proof, topics of accident, topics of the more effective, topics of genus, topics of differentia, topics of special property, topics of definition, and topics of 'is identical'. So if these topics are given, then all topics have been given.
Source pages p. 522

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical discussion comparing the difficulty of proving a definition (hadd) versus proving an accidental property (arad), and then classifying all dialectical topics into eight categories. The author begins by stating that proving a definition requires everything needed for genus, special property, and accident. The method of the 'Shaykh' (Aristotle) holds that definition is harder to prove due to more conditions. The well-known method contrasts ease of proving vs. refuting: accident is easy to prove as a definition but hard to refute, while definition is hard to prove and easy to refute. Topics for proving accident follow absolute proof. Aristotle (al-Mu'allim al-Awwal) did not explicitly endorse this, but argued that topics for other things also benefit definition: proofs for accident, special property, and genus are all needed to establish a definition. Proving genus is needed for definition, so refuting genus refutes definition. The word 'all' was misapplied. Accident accepts more and less, but definition, genus, and special property do not. The art of dialectic primarily deals with priority and appropriateness. However, essential things are not doubted in existence, but their benefit is through existence. Priority is relative. The discussion of priority and appropriateness leads to persuasion. Special property was added to definition; genus and differentia share essentiality and constitution. The investigations reduce to eight topics: absolute proof, accident, the more effective (athar), genus, differentia, special property, definition, and 'is identical' (huwa huwa). These eight encompass all dialectical topics.

Key Takeaway
In dialectic, the difficulty of proving a definition stems from its many conditions; accidents are easier to prove but harder to refute.

Classification of Dialectical Topics into Eight Categories
Classification of all dialectical topics into eight categories

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to list the eight dialectical topics (al-mawadi') and explain how they are derived from the passage.

Core Idea
All dialectical arguments fall into eight topics: absolute proof, accident, the more effective, genus, differentia, special property, definition, and identity.

The author concludes that the investigations of dialectic reduce to eight fundamental topics. This classification organizes all possible lines of argumentation. Understanding these topics equips a debater with a complete toolbox for constructing or refuting arguments.

Guided Path
1
Recognize that the passage reduces the number of topics
The passage states that the investigations dissolved into eight topics. This is the final classification. Note that earlier it referred to more scattered topics.
2
List the eight topics from the text
Identify each: (1) مواضع الإثبات المطلق (absolute proof), (2) مواضع العرض (accident), (3) مواضع الآثر (the more effective), (4) مواضع الجنس (genus), (5) مواضع الفصل (differentia), (6) مواضع الخاصة (special property), (7) مواضع الحد (definition), (8) مواضع الهو هو (is identical).
The order in the passage: 'مواضع الإثبات المطلق ومواضع العرض ومواضع الآثر ومواضع الجنس ومواضع الفصل ومواضع الخاصة ومواضع الحد ومواضع الهو هو'
3
Understand the rationale for grouping
Special property was added to definition; genus and differentia were placed in one chapter because they share in essentiality and constitution. This merging reduces the number.
The passage says: 'فإن الخاصة أضيفت إلى الحد وجعل الجنس والفصل في باب واحد'
4
Note the role of priority and appropriateness
The art of dialectic primarily deals with 'the way of priority and appropriateness' (طريق الأولى والأحرى). This underlies many topics, especially 'the more effective' and 'is identical'.
The passage mentions that priority is relative and that the consideration leads to persuasion.
5
Verify that all topics are covered
The passage asserts: 'فإذا أعطيت هذه المواضع فقد أعطيت المواضع كلها' (if these topics are given, then all topics have been given). So these eight are exhaustive.
This is a claim of completeness.

The Three Meanings of 'Huwa Huwa' in Dialectic
Author defines 'huwa huwa' and distinguishes its three types: generic, specific, and individual.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the three types of identity statements (huwa huwa) used in dialectical arguments: generic, specific, and individual.

Core Idea
'Huwa huwa' covers generic, specific, and individual identity – not just personal identity.

In dialectical debates, opponents often make identity claims. The author introduces a key term 'huwa huwa' (he is he) used to express such claims. He identifies three distinct meanings: generic, specific, and individual. This lesson will help you parse these meanings so you can recognise the type of identity being asserted in any argument.

Guided Path
1
Locate the definition of 'huwa huwa'
Find the sentence where the author says 'huwa huwa' is used for three meanings. Underline the three types mentioned.
2
Identify the first type: generic identity
Find the example given: 'man is the horse'. Why is this generic? Because they share a common genus (animal).
3
Identify the second type: specific identity
Find the example: 'Zayd is al-'Amr'. Why specific? Both are human individuals, sharing the species 'human'.
4
Identify the third type: individual identity
Find the example: 'Zayd is this white [person]'. Why individual? It identifies a specific person by an attribute.
5
Check the author's warning
At the end, the author warns that sometimes one mistakes individual for specific identity. Note the example of confusion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم يحتاج أن يعطي القانون في استعمالها عند المحاورات الجدلية ولكن بالحري أن يكون لنا معنى الهو هو محصلا فنقول إن الهو هو يقال على طريق الاختصار على معانٍ ثلاثة؛ فيقال هو هو لما يشارك شيئًا في معنى عام جنسي كما يقال إن الإنسان هو الفرس، ويقال في معنى عام نوعي كما يقال إن زيدًا هو العمرو، ويقال في معنى خاص شخصي إن زيدًا هو هذا الأبيض. وكل ما يقال فيه هو هو يفيد اثنينية ما أولا بوجه ثم يردُّ إلى وحدانية. والاثنينية في المعنى الشخصي بالعرض، والوحدة بالموضوع ؛ وقد يكون الاثنينية بالموضوع والعرض والوحدة بالمجتمع الذي يتناول الإشارة حملية كما في قولنا زيد هو هذا الكاتب، وربَّما كانت الكثرة بحسب اسمين والوحدة بحسب المعنى وهو أولى ما يقال هو هو مثل قولنا الإنسان هو البشر. وربَّما أشكل في الشيء الواحد أمر هو هو : فظن أنه بالشخص. وإنما هو بالنوع مثلا نحو
Then one needs to give the law for their use in dialectical discussions, but it is more appropriate that we have the meaning of 'huwa huwa' established. So we say that 'huwa huwa' is said in a concise way for three meanings: it is said 'huwa huwa' for what shares with something in a common generic meaning, as when we say 'man is the horse'; and it is said in a common specific meaning, as when we say 'Zayd is al-'Amr'; and it is said in a particular individual meaning, that Zayd is this white [person]. And everything in which 'huwa huwa' is said indicates a duality first in some way, then returns to unity. The duality in the individual meaning is accidental, and the unity is in the subject; and duality may be in the subject and accident, and unity in the compound that takes the predicative reference, as in our saying 'Zayd is this writer'; and perhaps the multiplicity is in terms of two names and the unity in meaning, and that is more deserving of being called 'huwa huwa', like our saying 'man is the human'. And perhaps in one thing the matter of 'huwa huwa' may be problematic, so one thinks it is individual, while it is actually specific, for example.
Source pages p. 523

Commentary
This passage defines the term 'huwa huwa' (literally 'he is he' or 'it is it'), a dialectical topic concerning identity statements. The author states that 'huwa huwa' is used in a concise way for three meanings: (1) generic identity, where two things share a common genus, e.g., 'man is the horse' (both are animals); (2) specific identity, where they share a common species, e.g., 'Zayd is al-'Amr' (both are human individuals with a common nature? Actually the example suggests two individuals of the same species); (3) individual identity, where it refers to a particular person, e.g., 'Zayd is this white [person]' (identifying a specific attribute). Every 'huwa huwa' statement implies a duality initially (the two terms are distinct in reference or aspect) and then reduces to unity (they are the same thing). In individual identity, the duality is accidental (e.g., Zayd and his whiteness), while unity is in the subject. Sometimes duality is in subject and accident, and unity in the compound that is referred to predicatively, like 'Zayd is this writer' (Zayd and the writer are the same). Sometimes multiplicity is in names but unity in meaning, which is the most genuine 'huwa huwa', like 'man is the human' (two names for one essence). The author warns that sometimes one might confuse individual identity with specific identity, e.g., thinking a statement is about a particular when it is about a type.

Key Terms
الهو هو
المعنى العام الجنسي
المعنى العام النوعي
المعنى الخاص الشخصي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which example illustrates generic identity (huwa huwa in a common generic meaning)?
A) 'Zayd is this writer'
B) 'Man is the horse'
C) 'Zayd is al-'Amr'
D) 'Man is the human'

Key Takeaway
Huwa huwa statements in dialectic are of three types: generic, specific, and individual; each involves a duality reduced to unity.

Duality and Unity in Huwa Huwa Statements
Author discusses the duality and unity in huwa huwa statements.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to analyse the duality (ithnayniyya) and unity (wahdaniyya) in each type of huwa huwa statement, and identify the source of duality and unity.

Core Idea
Every huwa huwa statement begins with a duality and ends in unity.

After introducing the three types, the author explains a deeper structure: every identity statement implies a duality (the two terms are distinct in some aspect) and then reduces to unity (they are the same in another aspect). This lesson will help you recognise the duality and unity in different types of huwa huwa, and avoid confusion.

Guided Path
1
Find the general statement about duality and unity
Locate the sentence: 'كل ما يقال فيه هو هو يفيد اثنينية ما أولا بوجه ثم يرد إلى وحدانية'. This is the key principle.
2
Analyse the individual type
In 'Zayd is this white', the duality is accidental (whiteness is an accident of Zayd), and unity is in the subject. Note the phrase: 'الاثنينية في المعنى الشخصي بالعرض، والوحدة بالموضوع'.
3
Analyse the dual subject-accident type
In 'Zayd is this writer', duality is in both subject and accident? Actually the text says: 'قد تكون الاثنينية بالموضوع والعرض والوحدة بالمجتمع الذي يتناول الإشارة حملية'. Unity is in the compound referred to predicatively.
4
Analyse the type with two names, one meaning
In 'man is the human', multiplicity is in names, unity in meaning. This is the most genuine huwa huwa.
5
Understand the warning
The author warns that sometimes one confuses individual with specific identity. This likely happens when the duality is not clear.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم يحتاج أن يعطي القانون في استعمالها عند المحاورات الجدلية ولكن بالحري أن يكون لنا معنى الهو هو محصلا فنقول إن الهو هو يقال على طريق الاختصار على معانٍ ثلاثة؛ فيقال هو هو لما يشارك شيئًا في معنى عام جنسي كما يقال إن الإنسان هو الفرس، ويقال في معنى عام نوعي كما يقال إن زيدًا هو العمرو، ويقال في معنى خاص شخصي إن زيدًا هو هذا الأبيض. وكل ما يقال فيه هو هو يفيد اثنينية ما أولا بوجه ثم يردُّ إلى وحدانية. والاثنينية في المعنى الشخصي بالعرض، والوحدة بالموضوع ؛ وقد يكون الاثنينية بالموضوع والعرض والوحدة بالمجتمع الذي يتناول الإشارة حملية كما في قولنا زيد هو هذا الكاتب، وربَّما كانت الكثرة بحسب اسمين والوحدة بحسب المعنى وهو أولى ما يقال هو هو مثل قولنا الإنسان هو البشر. وربَّما أشكل في الشيء الواحد أمر هو هو : فظن أنه بالشخص. وإنما هو بالنوع مثلا نحو
Then one needs to give the law for their use in dialectical discussions, but it is more appropriate that we have the meaning of 'huwa huwa' established. So we say that 'huwa huwa' is said in a concise way for three meanings: it is said 'huwa huwa' for what shares with something in a common generic meaning, as when we say 'man is the horse'; and it is said in a common specific meaning, as when we say 'Zayd is al-'Amr'; and it is said in a particular individual meaning, that Zayd is this white [person]. And everything in which 'huwa huwa' is said indicates a duality first in some way, then returns to unity. The duality in the individual meaning is accidental, and the unity is in the subject; and duality may be in the subject and accident, and unity in the compound that takes the predicative reference, as in our saying 'Zayd is this writer'; and perhaps the multiplicity is in terms of two names and the unity in meaning, and that is more deserving of being called 'huwa huwa', like our saying 'man is the human'. And perhaps in one thing the matter of 'huwa huwa' may be problematic, so one thinks it is individual, while it is actually specific, for example.
Source pages p. 523

Commentary
This passage defines the term 'huwa huwa' (literally 'he is he' or 'it is it'), a dialectical topic concerning identity statements. The author states that 'huwa huwa' is used in a concise way for three meanings: (1) generic identity, where two things share a common genus, e.g., 'man is the horse' (both are animals); (2) specific identity, where they share a common species, e.g., 'Zayd is al-'Amr' (both are human individuals with a common nature? Actually the example suggests two individuals of the same species); (3) individual identity, where it refers to a particular person, e.g., 'Zayd is this white [person]' (identifying a specific attribute). Every 'huwa huwa' statement implies a duality initially (the two terms are distinct in reference or aspect) and then reduces to unity (they are the same thing). In individual identity, the duality is accidental (e.g., Zayd and his whiteness), while unity is in the subject. Sometimes duality is in subject and accident, and unity in the compound that is referred to predicatively, like 'Zayd is this writer' (Zayd and the writer are the same). Sometimes multiplicity is in names but unity in meaning, which is the most genuine 'huwa huwa', like 'man is the human' (two names for one essence). The author warns that sometimes one might confuse individual identity with specific identity, e.g., thinking a statement is about a particular when it is about a type.

الوحدة
بالعرض
بالموضوع
المجتمع الذي يتناول الإشارة حملية

Key Takeaway
Huwa huwa statements always involve a duality that is resolved into unity; the type of duality varies with the type of identity.

The Three Meanings of 'Huwa Huwa' in Dialectical Discourse
Author defines 'huwa huwa' and distinguishes its three meanings

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the three types of identity (generic, specific, personal) in Arabic dialectical statements and identify which type is intended based on the terms used.

Core Idea
'Huwa huwa' asserts identity at three levels: genus, species, or individual.

In dialectical debates, the phrase 'huwa huwa' (هو هو) is used to claim identity between two things. But not all identities are the same. The logician must know whether the identity is at the level of genus, species, or the individual person. This passage from the logic text defines these three meanings and also shows how to recognize them.

Guided Path
1
Locate the three meanings
Find where the author lists the three meanings of 'huwa huwa' in the Arabic text. Identify the Arabic terms for each: معنى عام جنسي (generic), معنى عام نوعي (specific), معنى خاص شخصي (personal).
2
Analyze the examples
For each meaning, note the example given: generic (man and horse), specific (Zayd and 'Amr), personal (Zayd and this white thing). Understand why each example fits that category.
3
Examine duality and unity
Understand the author's point: every 'huwa huwa' initially presents a duality (two terms) that is reduced to unity. In personal identity, duality is accidental; the unity is in the subject.
4
Learn the hierarchy of identity
The author gives an order of priority: numerical (by number), by definition, by property (e.g., 'man is the laughing one'), and by accident. Recognize that the highest is numerical identity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم يحتاج أن يعطي القانون في استعمالها عند المحاورات الجدلية ولكن بالحري أن يكون لنا معنى الهو هو محصلا فنقول إن الهو هو يقال على طريق الاختصار على معانٍ ثلاثة؛ فيقال هو هو لما يشارك شيئًا في معنى عام جنسي كما يقال إن الإنسان هو الفرس، ويقال في معنى عام نوعي كما يقال إن زيدًا هو العمرو، ويقال في معنى خاص شخصي إن زيدًا هو هذا الأبيض. وكل ما يقال فيه هو هو يفيد اثنينية ما أولا بوجه ثم يردُّ إلى وحدانية. والاثنينية في المعنى الشخصي بالعرض، والوحدة بالموضوع ؛ وقد يكون الاثنينية بالموضوع والعرض والوحدة بالمجتمع الذي يتناول الإشارة حملية كما في قولنا زيد هو هذا الكاتب، وربَّما كانت الكثرة بحسب اسمين والوحدة بحسب المعنى وهو أولى ما يقال هو هو مثل قولنا الإنسان هو البشر. وربَّما أشكل في الشيء الواحد أمر هو هو : فظن أنه بالشخص. وإنما هو بالنوع مثلا نحو المياه المتفرقة في الأوعية فإنها متحدة بالنوع دون الشخص؛ فأحق ما يقال له هو هو هو ما يكون بالعدد، ومنه ما يكون بالحد ؛ ثم ما يكون بالخاصة كقولنا إن الإنسان هو الضحاك. ثمَّ ما بالعرض. وقد يدخل في هذا ما يكون هو هو على سبيل المناسبة بوجه ما ؛ فهذه هي المباحث المفروضة للمطالب.
Then one needs to give the law in using them during dialectical dialogues, but it is more fitting that we have the meaning of 'huwa huwa' well-defined. So we say: 'huwa huwa' is used in a concise way for three meanings. It is said 'huwa huwa' for something that shares with something else in a generic common meaning, as when we say 'Man is the horse' (i.e., both are animal). And it is said in a specific common meaning, as when we say 'Zayd is the 'Amr' (i.e., both are human). And it is said in a particular personal meaning, as when we say 'Zayd is this white thing.' And every instance of 'huwa huwa' conveys a certain duality initially in one respect, then reduces to unity. The duality in the personal meaning is accidental, and the unity is in the subject. And sometimes the duality is in subject and accident, and the unity is in the composite that takes the predicative reference, as in our saying 'Zayd is this writer.' And sometimes the multiplicity is due to two names and the unity is due to the meaning, and that is the most worthy of being called 'huwa huwa', like our saying 'Man is human.' And sometimes a thing may be problematic regarding 'huwa huwa', so one thinks it is personal, but it is specific, as with water that is separated in different vessels – they are unified in species but not in person. So the most worthy of being called 'huwa huwa' is that which is numerical, then that which is by definition, then that which is by property, as in our saying 'Man is the laughing one.' Then that which is accidental. And into this enters what is 'huwa huwa' by way of appropriate in some respect. These are the assigned investigations for the demands.
Source pages p. 523

Commentary
This passage defines the concept of 'huwa huwa' (identity) and distinguishes its three meanings. The author states that 'huwa huwa' is used in three ways: generic (jinsi), specific (naw'i), and personal (shakhsi). The generic meaning is when two things share a common genus, e.g., 'Man is the horse' – both are animals. The specific meaning is when they share a species, e.g., 'Zayd is 'Amr' – both are human. The personal meaning is when something is identical to an individual, e.g., 'Zayd is this white thing' (i.e., the white thing is Zayd). Then the author explains that every such statement implies a duality that is reduced to unity; the duality is accidental in the case of the personal, and the unity is in the subject. The author gives further examples and notes that the most proper 'huwa huwa' is numerical identity, then identity by definition, then by property, then by accident. This passage is from a section on dialectical places and serves to clarify the use of identity statements in debate. All examples are from the Islamic tradition of logic, such as 'Man is the laughing one' as a property.
