Dialectical Premises and Demands: Conditions and Types
Defines dialectical premise and demand with conditions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two types of dialectical premises and the condition for a dialectical demand, and distinguish absolute from non-absolute generally accepted opinions.

Core Idea
Dialectical premises are either generally accepted or conceded; dialectical demands must be debatable.

In dialectical reasoning, not every statement can serve as a premise or a demand. The author, following Aristotle's Topics, specifies that a dialectical premise must be either an unqualified generally accepted opinion (مشهورة مطلقة) or a conceded opinion (مسلمة). A dialectical demand, on the other hand, must be a non-evident issue that can be debated. Understanding these conditions is crucial for constructing valid dialectical arguments and avoiding fallacies in munazara.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of dialectical premises
From the passage, a dialectical premise can only be either an unqualified generally accepted opinion (مشهورة مطلقة) or a conceded opinion (مسلمة). Any other type is subject to rejection (منع).
2
Distinguish absolute from non-absolute generally accepted opinions
The author mentions that there is a non-absolute generally accepted opinion (مشهورة غير مطلقة) that is disputed or not agreed upon. Such opinions can still be used as premises, but they are not unqualified.
3
Define the condition for a dialectical demand
A dialectical demand must be one of the two contradictory opposites and must not be self-evident. If it is self-evident, it is not a valid demand except for dealing with sophists.
4
Note the role of contrary premises in reductio arguments
Premises that contradict generally accepted opinions can sometimes be used dialectically, as in a reductio ad absurdum argument (قياس الخلف), where one shows that the opposite leads to an absurdity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في تفصيل المقدمات المشهورة الجدلية والمطلوبات الجدلية لكنه يلزمنا أن نعرف كيف تستنبط المواضع؛ وقبل ذلك فيلزمنا أن نحد المقدّمة الجدلية التي هي جزء قياس جدلي. والمطلب الجدلي الذي هو أحد طرفي النقيض بما يسوق إليه القياس الجدلي وهو للمجيب ما ينصره ويحفظ؛ وللسائل ما يقابله هكذا يجب أن نفهم هذا الموضع من التعليم الأول إلا كما ظن من أنه يعنى بهما شيئًا واحدا هو بالفعل أو بالقوة جزء القياس الجدلي: أما ما هو بالفعل فإذا استعملت مقدمة بالقوة فإذا أخرجت على جهة السؤال كما تسلم فإنه غير موافق للغرض. والمقدمة التي تؤخذ في التعليم على أنها مسألة؛ إذ لا فرق بحسب الغرض الذي في هذا الموضع بين أن يقال مقدمة أو يقال مسألة فنقول: إنه ليس يمكن أن تكون مقدمة جدلية إلا مشهورة مطلقة أو مسلمة وإلا لكانت في حيز المنع. وأما المطلب الجدلي فليس أيضًا يصلح أن يكون كل شيء فإن الأمر الذي هو بين غني عن الإثبات فلا يكون مطلبًا جدليًّا إلا بالقياس إلى المغالطين في الجدل. وأما المشهورة الغير المطلقة وهي التي وقع الاختلاف فيها أو هي غير متفق عليها فللجدلي أن يطلب عنها وأن يقيس على أحد طرفي النقيض فيها. وأما النتيجة فإنها بعد اللزوم يكون متحققا منها أنها مجهولة في نفسها. وإنما تكون معرفتها بالقياس؛ وفيما نحن فيه لا يكون كذلك بل يكون الانتقال يفضي فيها بأنها من حقها أن لا يجهل أبدا. وأيضًا المقدّمة المضادة للمشهورة والمقابلة التي ليست بمشهورة أيضًا تكون جدلية من وجه؛ وذلك لأنها تجعل مقدّمة في إبانة أن ما ينتج التسع فهو التسع كما في قياس الخلف؛ ومن قبل ما نحن فيه جميع المقدمات والآراء الذائعة في الصناعة فإن من الآراء المأخوذة في الصناعات ما يذيع ويشتهر وأما التي بالنقل منها فمثل أنه إذا كان مشهورًا
Chapter on detailing the generally accepted dialectical premises and dialectical demands. But it is necessary for us to know how the topics are derived; and before that it is necessary for us to define the dialectical premise, which is a part of a dialectical syllogism. And the dialectical demand, which is one of the two contradictory opposites that the dialectical syllogism leads to, and it is for the respondent what he defends and preserves; and for the questioner what opposes it. Thus we must understand this place from the First Teaching, except what some think that by the two [terms] a single thing is meant that is actually or potentially part of the dialectical syllogism: as for what is actual, if a premise is used potentially and then brought out in the form of a question as it is conceded, it is not in accordance with the purpose. And the premise that is taken in the [First] Teaching as a problem; for there is no difference according to the purpose in this place between saying 'premise' or 'problem'. So we say: It is not possible for a dialectical premise to be other than an unqualified generally accepted [opinion] or a conceded [opinion]; otherwise it would be subject to rejection. As for the dialectical demand, it also is not suitable to be anything; for the matter that is evident does not need proof, so it cannot be a dialectical demand except in relation to sophists in dialectic. As for the not-unqualified generally accepted [opinion] - that which is disputed or not agreed upon - the dialectician may inquire about it and syllogize on one of the two contradictory opposites concerning it. As for the conclusion, after the implication it is verified that it is unknown in itself; and its knowledge is only through syllogism. But in what we are dealing with, it is not so; rather, the transition leads to it being that it is entitled never to be unknown. Also, the premise contradictory to the generally accepted and the opposite which is not generally accepted are dialectical in a sense; because they are made a premise in showing that what produces [؟] the thing is that thing, as in the syllogism of absurdity; and from before what we are dealing with, all the premises and widely circulated opinions in an art: for of the opinions taken in the arts, some become widely circulated and famous, and as for those which are transmitted, like that if it is generally accepted...
Source pages p. 525

Commentary
This passage is a definitional exposition of the key elements of dialectical reasoning: the dialectical premise (مقدمة جدلية) and the dialectical demand (مطلب جدلي). The author, likely following Aristotle’s Topics, explains that a dialectical premise must be either an unqualified generally accepted opinion (مشهورة مطلقة) or a conceded opinion (مسلمة). If it is neither, it falls into the domain of rejection (منع). The dialectical demand, on the other hand, must be a debatable issue — something that is not self-evident. If it is evident, it cannot be a demand except when dealing with sophists who deny the obvious. The author distinguishes between two types of generally accepted opinions: the absolute (unqualified) and the non-absolute (where there is disagreement). For the latter, a dialectician may still argue. He also discusses the conclusion of a dialectical syllogism, noting that in dialectic the conclusion should be something not previously known. However, in the present context, the conclusion is not unknown — this might refer to a peculiarity of the dialectical method. Finally, he notes that premises contrary to generally accepted opinions can still be used dialectically, especially in reduction ad absurdum arguments (قياس الخلف). The passage ends abruptly discussing the circulation of opinions in arts. All examples are from the Aristotelian dialectical tradition, but the author uses them in an Islamic logical context.

The Absolute Dialectical Premise
Author explains the absolute dialectical premise and its use by both parties.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the absolute dialectical premise and explain why it does not require the opponent's prior acceptance.

Core Idea
Absolute dialectical premises are the common currency of debate: accepted by all, dependent on none.

In a dialectical debate, not all premises need to be explicitly granted by the opponent. Some are so widely accepted—like ethical maxims—that they can be used freely. The author calls these 'absolute dialectical premises' and shows how they function like self-evident truths in demonstration. Understanding this tool helps you navigate debates without getting stuck on unnecessary approvals.

Guided Path
1
Identify the type of premise
Look for premises that are presented as commonly known or widely accepted (mashhurat). In the text, the example 'beneficence towards friends is obligatory' is such a premise.
These are not necessarily proven, but they are not in dispute.
2
Check if it depends on the opponent
Ask: Does the arguer need the opponent's explicit agreement to use this premise? The author says no; the premise is 'absolute' because it is used without seeking acceptance.
Compare with premises that require istislam (beseeching acceptance).
3
Observe the reasoning pattern
Notice how the author applies the premise by opposition: if beneficence to friends is obligatory, then harming them is also obligatory. This demonstrates how dialectical reasoning works with opposites.
This pattern is common in jadal: taking a known judgment and transferring it to its opposite.
4
Contrast with demonstration
The text likens these premises to primary premises (awwaliyyat) in demonstration: both are accepted without proof. Understand that dialectic relies on social acceptance, not logical necessity.
This shows the epistemological status of dialectical premises.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
محمودًا أن العلم بالمضادات واحد واسع [؟] وكذلك الحس بالمضادات واحد صار هذا محمودا. وروي أنه واجب الإقرار به؛ وذلك لأن مشابهة العلم للحس ظاهرة جدًّا. وكذلك إن لم يكن مشابهة بل مقابلة لا كان مقابلة الحكم؛ وذلك مثل أنه إذا كان مشهورًا أن الإحسان إلى الأصدقاء واجب فإن الإساءة إليهم واجب، وهذا إذا أخذنا نقيض مقابل الإحسان فأقمناه مقامه وقولنا إن كان محمودًا أن الإحسان إلى الأصدقاء عدل، محمود أن الإساءة إلى الأعداء عدل وهذا إذا أخذنا شيئًا وألفينا بينه وبين الشيء وحكمنا بحكم؛ فجعلنا مقابل الشيء الأول المقابل الشيء الثاني بذلك الحكم. وسيظهر لك أن هذا أمر واجب في نفس الأمر وليس بواجب إلا أنه وأمثاله محمود؛ فهذه المقدّمة الجدلية المطلقة، ويشترك في استعمالها السائل والمجيب. فإن المجيب يستعمل المشهورات غير ملتفت إلى ما يسلمه السائل أو لا يسلمه، والسائل إذا استعمل المشهورات لم يحتج إلى التسليم في أكثر الأمر؛ فإن حال المشهورات في الجدل مثل حال الأوليات في البرهان.
Praised that knowledge of opposites is one broad [ ? ] and likewise sense perception of opposites is one, so this becomes praised. And it is narrated that it is obligatory to affirm it; because the similarity of knowledge to sense is very apparent. Likewise, if there is not similarity but rather opposition, then the opposition of judgment would be considered; such as when it is well-known that beneficence towards friends is obligatory, then harming them is obligatory, and this is when we take the contrary counterpart of beneficence and put it in its place, and our saying: if it is praised that beneficence towards friends is justice, it is praised that harming enemies is justice, and this is when we take something and place between it and the thing and judge by a judgment; so we make the counterpart of the first thing correspond to the second thing by that judgment. And it will appear to you that this matter is obligatory in itself, but it is not obligatory except that it and its likes are praised; so this is the absolute dialectical premise, and both the questioner and the answerer share in its use. For the answerer uses well-known premises without attending to whether the questioner grants them or not, and the questioner, when he uses well-known premises, does not need to seek acceptance in most cases; for the state of well-known premises in dialectic is like the state of primary premises in demonstration.
Source pages p. 526

Commentary
This passage explains the concept of the absolute dialectical premise (al-muqaddima al-jadaliyya al-mutlaqa). The author begins by illustrating how knowledge of opposites is unified, comparing it to sense perception. Then he provides a concrete example: if it is well-known that beneficence towards friends is obligatory, then by opposition, harming them is also obligatory. Similarly, if beneficence to friends is praised as justice, then harming enemies is praised as justice. This reasoning works by taking a known judgment and applying its opposite to the opposite subject. The author clarifies that this premise is 'absolute' because it does not depend on the opponent's specific acceptance; rather, it is based on common acceptance (mashhurat) that both parties can use. In dialectic, well-known premises function like primary premises (awwaliyyat) in demonstration: they are accepted without requiring proof from the opponent. The answerer can use them freely, and the questioner does not need to secure prior agreement. The example used (beneficence/harm to friends/enemies) is rooted in ethical common sense, fitting the Islamic sciences context.

Key Terms
المقدّمة الجدلية المطلقة (al-muqaddima al-jadaliyya al-mutlaqa): A premise that is well-known (mashhura) and does not require the opponent's specific acceptance; it is used freely by both parties in dialectical debate. [Note: This term appears in Aristotelian dialectic; later Muslim logicians refine it further, but the core idea remains stable.]
المشهورات (al-mashhurat): Well-known opinions or premises that are widely accepted and serve as the foundation for dialectical arguments. [Note: In earlier logic, mashhurat are contrasted with awwaliyyat; later, especially in Ibn Taymiyya's critique, the reliability of mashhurat is questioned.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, how do well-known premises (mashhurat) function in dialectic compared to primary premises (awwaliyyat) in demonstration?
A) Both require the opponent's explicit acceptance.
B) Mashhurat require less acceptance than awwaliyyat.
C) Mashhurat in dialectic are like awwaliyyat in demonstration: they are accepted without needing the opponent's specific agreement.
D) Mashhurat are weaker than awwaliyyat and always need proof.

Key Takeaway
The absolute dialectical premise is a well-known statement that both parties in a debate can use freely, without requiring the opponent's prior agreement.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Identify whether the following premise is an absolute dialectical premise: 'The world is temporally originated.' Explain why or why not based on the text.
?
In a debate about riba, one debater says: 'It is well-known that injustice is forbidden.' The opponent says: 'I do not accept that premise.' According to the text, can the first debater still use that premise? Justify your answer.

Dialectic -- Jadal
beginner
5 min
Definition and Purpose of a Dialectical Question
Author defines the dialectical question and its essential characteristics.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the defining features of a dialectical question and explain how it serves to prove a desired conclusion.

Core Idea
A dialectical question is a premise that seeks acceptance to help prove a practical or doctrinal conclusion.

In this passage, the author gives a precise definition of a dialectical question (mas'ala jadaliyya). Understanding this definition is essential for anyone engaging in dialectical debate (jadal) in Islamic sciences, as it determines which premises are acceptable. We will break down the definition to see what makes a premise a proper question in debate.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definiendum and its immediate definition
Find the phrase 'مسألة جدلية' and note that it is defined as a premise taken in the manner of a question. This tells us the genus is a premise and the differentia is the questioning mode.
2
Extract the purpose: proving a desired conclusion
The definition states that the premise is used to prove a desired conclusion (matlub). This conclusion can be practical (what to prefer or avoid) or doctrinal (what is true) – both are examples from Islamic ethics and theology.
3
Understand the three ways the premise can serve
The premise may directly achieve the goal by its acceptance, help produce another premise, or serve as a logical rule that aids reasoning. Each way shows how the premise functions within the debate.
The third way refers to logical principles like 'every effect has a cause'.
4
Check the definition's completeness
Assess if the definition covers all instances of dialectical questions. It includes formal (definitional) and material (source) conditions, but the author later adds the source classification, so this definition is part of a broader description.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبالجملة فالتي هي الأولى بأن تكون مسألة جدلية أي أن تكون مقدمة تؤخذ على سبيل المسألة فهي ما يكون طلب التسليم فيها لمعني ينتفع في إثبات مطلوب من باب ما يوثر أو يجتنب، أو مطلوب اعتقادي من باب ما يرى حقا ويقصد فيه المعرفة: فيكون إما أن يبلغ بتسلمه الغرض في ذلك، أو بأن يجعل مقدّمة تُعِينُ في إنتاج ما تؤخذ مقدّمة أو يكون معينا على ذلك بأن يكون قانونًا منطقيًا ينفع منفعة المنطق. ويكون إما عن المشهورات الغير المطلقة وإما عن غيرها؛ وذلك من جملة ما لا اعتقاد فيه للفلاسفة اعتقادًا مشهورًا أو من جملة ما فيه اختلاف بين الفلاسفة وبين غيرهم كما في اللذة، فإن الجمهور ذهبوا إلى أن اللذة خير، والفلاسفة لم يذهبوا إليه، أو يكون فيه للفلاسفة فيما بينهم اختلاف أو يكون فيه للجمهور فيما بينهم اختلاف.
In summary, that which is primarily fit to be a dialectical question—i.e., a premise taken in the manner of a question—is that which seeks acceptance for a meaning that is useful in proving a desired conclusion from the category of what is preferred or avoided, or a doctrinal desired conclusion from the category of what is considered true and for which knowledge is intended. It may either achieve the goal by its acceptance, or serve as a premise that helps in producing what is taken as a premise, or be an aid to that by being a logical rule beneficial for logic. And it may be from non-absolute well-known premises or from others; this includes what the philosophers do not hold as a well-known belief, or what involves disagreement between philosophers and others, as in the case of pleasure—for the public hold that pleasure is good, while the philosophers do not hold that—or it may involve disagreement among the philosophers themselves, or disagreement among the public themselves.
Source pages p. 526

Commentary
This passage provides a formal definition of a dialectical question (mas'ala jadaliyya). The author states that a dialectical question is a premise that is put forward as a request for acceptance. The purpose of seeking acceptance is to use it in proving a desired conclusion (matlub). The desired conclusion may be practical (relating to what is preferred or avoided, such as ethical actions) or theoretical (relating to what is considered true, aiming at knowledge). The premise can serve in three ways: (1) its mere acceptance directly achieves the goal, (2) it helps produce another premise that leads to the goal, or (3) it is a logical principle that aids reasoning. The source of the premise can be non-absolute mashhurat (well-known premises that are not universally accepted) or other types, such as philosophical opinions that are not commonly held, or points of disagreement between philosophers and the public, among philosophers themselves, or among the public themselves. The example of pleasure illustrates: the public generally believes pleasure is good, but philosophers disagree. This shows how a dialectical question may rely on opinions that are not absolutely well-known but are still suitable for dialectical debate. The classification helps students understand the range of premises permissible in dialectic.

Key Terms
مسألة جدلية
المشهورات الغير المطلقة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, which of the following is NOT a way a dialectical question can serve?
A) Its acceptance directly achieves the goal.
B) It is a logical rule that aids reasoning.
C) It is derived from revelation.
D) It helps produce another premise.

Key Takeaway
A dialectical question is a premise that seeks acceptance to prove a practical or doctrinal conclusion, and it may serve directly, as a helper, or as a logical rule.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the premise: 'The world is temporally originated (hadith).' Explain whether it qualifies as a dialectical question according to the definition, and identify which of the three purposes it serves (direct, helpful, or logical rule).
